1201 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Kalaydjian v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. 68626


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


149 Cal. App. 3d 690; 197 Cal. Rptr. 149; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2421



December 9, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C363430, Julius M. Title, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that he was not entitled to recover the tenant relocation assistance he paid under defendant city's ordinance, Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 47.06. Plaintiff contended that the ordinance violated the federal and state constitutions and created an arbitrary classification by excluding "luxury housing."


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landlord paid $ 14,500 in relocation assistance to tenants displaced by his conversion of a 10-unit apartment house to commercial use. He filed a cause of action against defendant city seeking refund of the amounts paid, contending that the ordinance requiring such payments was unconstitutional as a violation of equal protection and due process. The trial court ruled for defendant, finding the ordinance constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The court determined that the city could reasonably conclude that changes in the use of residential property were reducing availability of rental housing, and that exacting a fee from the one benefiting from a converted use could mitigate the negative impact. The court also found adequate justification for defendant city's distinction between ordinary and "luxury" housing. First, "luxury housing" was exempt under the city's rent control ordinance, so unlike others, tenants displaced from "luxury" housing were presumably already paying market rents. Moreover, the city could reasonably find that tenants who were able to afford higher-priced housing would not require financial assistance to find alternate housing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that defendant city could have reasonably concluded that changes in the use of residentially zoned property would adversely affect the stock of rental housing in the community, and that the fee exacted from those benefiting from the changed use would cushion the displacement effect of that change. The court also found adequate justification for distinguishing between ordinary and "luxury" housing.

 

1202 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Flynn v. Higham


Civ. No. 68301


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


149 Cal. App. 3d 677; 197 Cal. Rptr. 145; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2419



December 9, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 15, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C393913, Eli Chernow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order dismissing the plaintiffs' case is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff children sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed plaintiffs' complaint in an action based on defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff children filed a complaint against defendants, publisher and author, that alleged defendants defamed plaintiffs' father as being a homosexual and Nazi spy. The trial court dismissed plaintiffs' complaint pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581(3). Plaintiffs' claimed that the trial court erred in sustaining defendants' demurrer because their complaint stated viable theories of recovery based on defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy. The court held that plaintiffs did not state a cause of action based on defamation for statements made by defendants regarding their deceased father. The court held that plaintiffs did not maintain an independent cause of action for the intentional infliction of emotional distress when the facts pleaded were not sufficient in supporting a cause of action for defamation. The court held that plaintiff did not have a cause of action for invasion of privacy because plaintiffs were never mentioned in defendants' book. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, and affirmed dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, and affirmed dismissal of plaintiff children's complaint in an action based on defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy.

 

1203 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of Whittier v. Walnut Properties


Civ. No. 65777


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


149 Cal. App. 3d 633; 197 Cal. Rptr. 127; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2415



December 8, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 3, 1984. McClosky, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 2, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SEC 29232, Vincent S. Dalsimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. On remand, the trial court shall make the necessary rulings on proffered evidence as to the status of the theatre as an "adult theatre" and as to the availability of alternate sites in the City of Whittier, and enter judgment in accordance with this opinion and such additional findings. Neither party shall recover costs in this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ruled in favor of respondent adult theatre, holding that language contained in a municipal code rendered the code unconstitutional in that the language prohibited the operation of any adult business at any location in the city.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged the lower court's judgment, in favor of respondent adult theatre, which held that Ordinance No. 2138, Whittier, Cal., Municipal Code § 9512(2)(c) was unconstitutional, in that the language prohibited the operation of any adult business at any location in the city. The court reversed the lower court's judgment, remanded, and held that while Ordinance No. 2138 was unconstitutional on its face because it prohibited the operation of any adult business within city limits, and such an absolute exclusion violated U.S. Const. amend. I, the substitute Ordinance No. 2254, Whittier, Cal., Municipal Code § 9512.1, enacted prior to the entry of judgment, was valid. The court held that the rights of the parties in an action in equity was determined on the basis of the law as it existed at the time of the determination, rather than at the time the complaint was filed. The court held that the substitute ordinance was reasonable if the ordinance dispersed adult theatres and did not prevent the entry of new theatres into the market place. The court held that the term adult theatre was specifically defined within the ordinance, and the term was neither vague nor overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment that the ordinance was unconstitutional and remanded for rulings as to the status of the theatre as an "adult theatre" and as to the availability of alternate sites. The court held that the substitute ordinance was reasonable if the ordinance dispersed adult theatres and did not prevent the entry of new theatres into the market place.

 

1204 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ostrow v. Mun. Court


Civ. No. 29457


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


149 Cal. App. 3d 668; 197 Cal. Rptr. 40; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2418



December 8, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. I-35820, Warren E. Slaughter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendants sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which denied appellants' petition for a writ of prohibition against respondent municipal court concerning prosecutions by respondent state under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal defendants were each charged in respondent municipal court with driving under the influence of alcohol and driving with greater than .10 percent blood alcohol under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a)-(b) by respondent state. The court affirmed the judgment that denied a writ of prohibition to appellants because § 23152 was not unconstitutional. The court found that Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) did not constitute a conclusive presumption, but defined and proscribed an offense. The court concluded that the criminal complaints gave each appellant notice of the precise offense with which he or she was charged and against which he or she was required to defend. The court rejected the contention that § 23152(b) was unconstitutionally vague or uncertain. The court determined that the offense defined in § 23152(b) fell within those regulatory offenses not requiring an intent element. The purpose of the statute was to eliminate a danger to the public, and the danger was the same whether the driver knew or did not know that his or her blood alcohol level was at or above .10 percent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the statute was not unconstitutional. The statute was not vague or uncertain and the offense of driving with greater than .10 percent blood alcohol fell within those regulatory offenses not requiring an intent element.

 

1205 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Alarcon


Civ. No. 24854


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


149 Cal. App. 3d 544; 196 Cal. Rptr. 887; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2408



December 6, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. D 133289, Paul Eugene Overton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The cause is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Each party shall bear their own costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retirement system sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which declared provisions of Cal. Gov't. Code § 75033 and Cal. Gov't. Code § 75033.5 of the Judges' Retirement Law inapplicable to the interests of respondent divorced couple in retirement allowances.


OVERVIEW: Appellants retirement system sought review of a judgment which declared provisions of Cal. Gov't. Code § 75033 and Cal. Gov't. Code § 75033.5 inapplicable to the interests of respondent divorce couple in retirement allowances. Respondents separated and an interlocutory judgment of dissolution reserved jurisdiction to determine the interest of the benefits under the Judges' Retirement Act. The superior court denied respondent's motion to withdraw his contributions to the retirement fund. On appeal, the court found that respondent's right to the § 75033.5 benefits was vested when he went on the federal bench even though payments were postponed to the attainment of age 63. Further the court found that the amount was reducible. The court held that while respondent's federal appointment affected the parties' community right to receive retirement benefits from the retirement fund, his election did not defeat the community interest in pension benefits. The court affirmed the judgment in part and reversed the judgment in part.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in part and reversed in part because respondent divorced couple's right to the retirement benefits was vested when respondent judge went on the federal bench, even though payments were postponed to the attainment of age 63.

 

1206 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


State Personnel Bd. v. Fair Employment & Housing Com.


Civ. No. 19549.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


149 Cal. App. 3d 429; 196 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2398; 33 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 745; 34 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P34,485



December 1, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 29, 1984. (See 39 Cal. 3d 422)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 280206, Irving H. Perluss, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrative agency sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent administrative agency's motion for declaratory judgment and denied appellant's cross-motion for summary judgment in a case involving a jurisdictional conflict between administrative agencies.


OVERVIEW: The applicants applied for civil service appointments. Their applications were rejected because of disqualifying physical conditions. Applicants appealed to respondent administrative agency, which upheld the disqualifications. Applicants then filed a petition for writ of mandamus pursuant to Cal. Gov't. Code § 1094.5 with appellant administrative agency, which found probable cause to believe discimination had occurred and issued formal accusations. Respondents filed suit in seeking to restrain appellants from filing accusations or holding hearings. Respondent filed a motion for declaratory judgment, which was granted, and appellants filed a cross-motion for summary judgment, which was denied. The court reversed and remanded with directions to enter judgment enjoining appellants because appellants' assertion of jurisdiction over these matters posed a present total and fatal conflict with respondent's constitutional powers of classification, examination, and appointment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of declaratory judgment and remanded with directions for the trial court to enter judgment enjoining appellant administrative agency, because appellant's assertion of jurisdiction conflicted with respondent administrative agency's constitutional powers.

 

1207 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Smith v. Silvey


Civ. No. 68405


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


149 Cal. App. 3d 400; 197 Cal. Rptr. 15; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2396



December 1, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C392008, Donald W. Pike, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The injunction granted by the lower court is hereby dissolved. Judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant ex-resident appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) granting plaintiff park owner's injunction restraining defendant from initiating complaints with public agencies in relation to plaintiff's mobile home park and from contacting any of the residents of the park, claiming the judgment violated defendant's constitutional right of free speech and petition for redress of grievances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant ex-resident resided in plaintiff park owner's mobile home park for five years. For five years after moving out of the park, defendant reported plaintiff to governmental agencies for violations ranging from liquor laws to building improvement regulations. Plaintiff filed a petition for injunction and an application for a temporary restraining order. The lower court ordered defendant to restrain from initiating further complaints and from contacting any residents, but authorized defendant to attend and participate in any public hearings concerning the park. On appeal, the court held that the injunction was dissolved because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.6 excluded constitutionally protected activity from its application. The court also found that the injunction was overbroad as its vague language served to prevent defendant from filing petitions with governmental agencies and from contacting residents on a lawful basis, both of which are constitutionally protected. Finally, the court held that defendant's behavior did not constitute "clear and present danger" or "serious substantive evil," and therefore did not merit the broad injunction.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting plaintiff park owner's injunction was reversed on the grounds that the injunction was overbroad and in violation of defendant's constitutional right to freedom of speech.

 

1208 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carter v. Mun. Court


Civ. No. 68821


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


149 Cal. App. 3d 184; 196 Cal. Rptr. 751; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2460



November 23, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C404585, Philip F. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied defendant's application for a writ of prohibition to restrain the trial court from applying two prior convictions to enhance punishment at the time of sentencing on conviction of driving while under the influence of alcohol, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a).


OVERVIEW: The lower court denied defendant's petition for a writ of prohibition to restrain the trial court from applying two prior convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol to enhance punishment for a recent conviction pursuant to the newly enacted Cal. Veh. Code §§ 23165, 23170. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because §§ 23165, 23170 were not ex post facto laws when applied to defendant's recent conviction. The court held that statutes did not criminalize prior innocent conduct, and did not increase the penalties for the prior convictions. The court stated that defendant's prior convictions were factors to be considered in determining the sentence for the recent conviction. The court also held that defendant's prior convictions were not unconstitutionally invalid because at the time of the convictions, the municipal court was not required to advise defendant of the increased sentence she faced for subsequent convictions of the same offense or the increased penalties under the newly enacted statutes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below, holding that the enhanced punishment statutes were not ex post facto laws when applied to criminal defendant's recent conviction for driving under the influence, and the prior convictions on the same offense were not constitutionally invalid.

 

1209 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Huntington Park Redevelopment Agency v. Martin


Civ. No. 67708.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


149 Cal. App. 3d 82; 196 Cal. Rptr. 628; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2452



November 22, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 2, 1984 (See 38 Cal. 3d 100)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner redevelopment agency, authorized to impose a sales tax as a means of financing the redevelopment of a blighted area within a California city, brought this original proceeding by petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent, petitioner's secretary, to publish, and thereby give effect to, an agency ordinance imposing a sales tax.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner redevelopment agency, a corporate public entity performing redevelopment of blighted property in a city, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, petitioner's secretary, to publish, and thereby give effect to, an agency ordinance authorizing collection of a sales and use tax within one of its redevelopment areas. Respondent refused to do so, arguing that the tax would violate the strictures of Cal. Const. art. XIII A and XIII B. The issue was whether petitioner's sales and use tax would violate the two-thirds majority vote requirement placed on special districts by Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, or would violate any provision contained in Cal. Const. art. XIII B. The court noted that the legislative scheme authorizing redevelopment agencies to impose their own taxes was conceived to avoid the taxing and spending limitations placed on California government by California taxpayers per Proposition 13. The court held that the subject tax could not be imposed absent a two-thirds majority vote within the redevelopment district because petitioner's participation in the tax-levying process made it a "special district" within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of mandate because respondent secretary's refusal to publish petitioner redevelopment agency's ordinance was proper, as the tax the ordinance authorized could not be levied without the approval of two-thirds of the voters, and that approval was not obtained.

 

1210 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



O.N.C. Freight Sys. v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. AO16132


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


149 Cal. App. 3d 11; 196 Cal. Rptr. 592; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2445



November 21, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 21, 1983, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 786410, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commercial trucking corporations challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found in favor of respondent Department of Motor Vehicles in appellants' complaint for declaratory relief regarding the payment of prorate registration fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commercial trucking corporations brought an action against respondent Department of Motor Vehicles that sought a declaration that they were entitled to a refund because appellants both paid the prorate registration fees on the same vehicle for the same period of time. The lower court ruled in favor of respondent, and the court affirmed. The court determined that respondent's policy of collecting prorate registration fees, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code §§ 8150-8163 and the Uniform Vehicle Registration and Proration and Reciprocity Agreement, was proper as double registration of the vehicle did not result in double taxation, and respondent was allowed to treat commercial vehicles differently from noncommercial vehicles. The court ruled that as there was a rational basis for respondent's collection policy--to make sure no one escaped paying for his use of the state's highways--appellants were not denied equal protection of the laws. The court also held that respondent's policy did not offend the Commerce Clause as it did not discriminate against interstate commerce by favoring intrastate registrants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for respondent Department of Motor Vehicles in appellant commercial trucking corporations' action for declaratory relief because respondent's policy for collection of prorate registration fees was valid under the statute, did not deny appellants equal protection of the laws, and did not offend the commerce clause.

 

1211 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Dodson)


Civ. No. 69961


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


148 Cal. App. 3d 990; 196 Cal. Rptr. 431; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2377



November 15, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 15, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its order of August 15, 1983, dismissing the section 5300 petition and to enter a new and different order denying the motion to dismiss and calendaring the matter for further hearings pursuant to section 5300; except that at any such hearings the People shall produce updated medical evidence reflective of real party's mental condition as of the time of the hearing.

For the reasons given in explaining our decision to proceed in this matter by way of extraordinary writ, and in order "to prevent mootness or to prevent frustration of the relief granted" this decision is hereby made final as to this court forthwith. ( Rule 24(a), Cal. Rules of Court.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state requested a petition for a writ of mandate from respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed the state's petition pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5300 to commit patient, the real party in interest, to a psychiatric hospital.


OVERVIEW: Patient, the real party in interest in this matter, was taken into custody pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5150 when he attacked a psychiatrist. Real party in interest was confined to a psychiatric hospital pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5250. Appellant state filed a petition requesting that real party in interest be committed for 180 days pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5300. Respondent trial court granted real party in interest's motion to dismiss the petition. Appellant petitioned for a writ of mandate, which the court granted. The court held that § 5300 withstood constitutional scrutiny, and that respondent abused its discretion in declaring the statute unconstitutional. The court held that real party in interest's argument required an unrealistic interpretation of the statute because the statute made clear that past conduct was relevant only as a prognosticator of probable future behavior. Finally, the court held that the § 5300 petition and supporting affidavits filed by appellant constituted a prima facie showing that the standards for confinement existed, and respondent abused its discretion in granting real party in interest's motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court granted appellant state's petition for writ of mandate and held that respondent trial court erred in dismissing appellant's petition to commit real party in interest patient to a psychiatric hospital. The court held that the petition and supporting affidavits filed by appellant constituted a prima facie showing that the standards for confinement existed.

 

1212 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Brown Co. v. Appellate Department


Civ. No. 29870


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


148 Cal. App. 3d 891; 196 Cal. Rptr. 258; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2364



November 8, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] We conclude there is no basis for annulment of the decision of the appellate department by writ proceeding. Accordingly, we reluctantly conclude the writ of review heretofore issued must be discharged and this proceeding dismissed, and it is so ordered. In the interests of justice nevertheless Livingston shall recover its costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a petition for a writ of review of the judgment from respondent, the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which held in real party in interest's favor in petitioner's suit against real party in interest to enforce a mechanic's lien.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest contracted with a general contractor for the construction of a house. The general contractor then contracted with petitioner whereby petitioner was to supply concrete needed for the job. Thereafter, petitioner purported to serve a preliminary notice as required by Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3097, 3098, on the general contractor as the reputed owner, and sued in the trial court to enforce the mechanic's lien. The trial court awarded judgment to petitioner and respondent reversed holding that the preliminary notice was premature and invalid. Petitioner filed a petition for review with the court. The court held that although the preliminary notice was sent before the concrete was delivered, it was not premature. Further, petitioner served the preliminary notice on the general contractor as the reputed owner reasonably and in good faith, and therefore petitioner's service attempt was not invalid for failure to serve the actual owner of the property. However, the court was without jurisdiction to annul the decision where, under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1068, a writ of review could only be granted when the trial court exceeded its jurisdiction. Writ discharged.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's writ discharged and the matter dismissed where, although respondent's decision was legally erroneous, the court was nonetheless without jurisdiction to grant petitioner a writ of review as respondent did not exceed its jurisdiction in rendering that judgment.

 

1213 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ohmer v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 68132


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


148 Cal. App. 3d 661; 196 Cal. Rptr. 224; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2338



November 2, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 6, 1984.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied and the alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, mother and custodial parent, sought to restrain respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) from proceeding with a hearing initiated by the father to modify a prior child custody order. The appellate court denied relief, but the California Supreme Court entered a stay and retransferred to the appellate court, with directions to issue an alternative writ and hear the matter.


OVERVIEW: By an order to show cause, a father sought to modify prior child custody order, claiming that petitioner, mother and custodial parent, was not properly caring for the couples' child. By stipulation of the parties the matter was referred to a psychiatrist and court appointed custody investigator. Ultimately, the matter came before the trial court for hearing. The appellate court denied petitioner's request to stay the proceedings. However, she filed a petition for hearing before the supreme court, which stayed the trial court hearing and retransferred to the appellate court with directions to issue an alternative writ and resolve the issues presented by the petition. The appellate court denied the petition and discharged the alternative writ. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying petitioner's requests for a continuance when petitioner had ample time to prepare her case. Also, the trial court properly precluded petitioner's cross-examination of the trial court's custody investigator and psychiatrist when she voluntarily waived this right by stipulating to the referral service. Moreover, the use of the stipulation and waiver did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The appellate court denied the writ to restrain a hearing to modify a child custody order sought by petitioner, mother and custodial parent, and discharged an alternative writ. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying a continuance to petitioner nor violate her due process rights by requiring a waiver of her right to cross examine court appointed investigators before referring the custody dispute to these personnel.

 

1214 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Walter H. Leimert Co. v. Cal. Coastal Com


Civ. No. 6458


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


149 Cal. App. 3d 222; 196 Cal. Rptr. 739; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2465



November 1, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 50126, William R. Fredman and Wickson R. Woolpert, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of the decision by the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) that dismissed appellants' action for declaratory relief, interference with contract, interference with business advantage, and inverse condemnation after a decision by appellee California Coastal Commission denied appellants a permit to subdivide a tract of land.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owners commenced steps to subdivide and develop their property. Appellants contracted with a water district to provide water services. Appellee regional California Coastal Commission held hearings on the permit application by the water district to rehabilitate the existing water distribution system. Appellants were denied a permit to subdivide a tract by appellee after it made findings, under the coastal act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq., which cited concerns of competition for water and environmental impact. The trial court sustained appellees' demurrers to appellants' complaint. The court affirmed and determined that administrative mandamus was appellants' proper remedy instead of declaratory relief because appellants' action did not challenge the constitutionality of the coastal act. The court further stated that appellants were required to exhaust their administrative remedies prior to bringing their action in court. The court also found that appellants had not filed their complaint within the statute of limitations set forth in Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30801.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant property owners' application for a permit to subdivide their property. The court stated that appellants' proper remedy was an administrative mandamus action, that appellants had not exhausted their administrative remedies, and appellants had not filed their claim within the statute of limitations.

 

1215 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Lewis


Civ. No. 30219


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


148 Cal. App. 3d 614; 196 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2331



October 31, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Central Orange County Judicial District of Orange County, No. 82-CS00443, Barbara Tam Nomoto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which affirmed his conviction under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) in the municipal court for driving with a blood alcohol level of .10 percent or more. Appellant alleged § 23152(b) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: When appellant was arrested, Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) provided that it was unlawful for any person who had 0.10 percent or more, by weight, of alcohol in his or her blood to drive a vehicle upon a highway or upon other than a highway in areas which were open to the general public. The statute further required that percent, by weight, of alcohol was to be based upon grams of alcohol per 100 milliliters of blood. Appellant was convicted under this section on evidence of blood alcohol breath tests of .13 and .14. The jury was unable to agree on the companion charge of driving under the influence of alcohol under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a). Upon its review of the appellate court's affirmation of appellant's conviction by the municipal court, the court, considering the constitutionality of § 23152(b), affirmed the conviction ,finding that, although the subsections of § 23152 were overlapping, they represented different offenses and that the legislature's decision to enact that statute in that manner was permissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court because appellant's conviction was not unconstitutional where he was convicted of violating one section of a statute that, permissibly, proscribed two overlapping offenses.

 

1216 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Strand Property Corp. v. Mun. Court


Civ. No. 28741


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


148 Cal. App. 3d 882; 200 Cal. Rptr. 47; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2363



October 27, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 10, 1983. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 25, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 496334, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal is dismissed and the writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with violating an ordinance that regulated adult motion picture theaters. They appealed an order by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) overruling their demurrer which challenged the ordinance for violating U.S. Const. amend. I and for unconstitutionally establishing a monopoly for existing businesses.


OVERVIEW: Defendants corporation and individuals charged with violating a city ordinance which regulated adult motion picture theaters. Defendants filed a demurrer in municipal court, challenging the ordinance for violating U.S. Const. amend. I, and for unconstitutionally establishing a monopoly for existing businesses. The demurrer was overruled, and defendants brought a motion to dismiss, which was denied. Defendants then filed an application for a writ of prohibition, which was denied. Defendants sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling against defendants because it found that the underlying ordinance dispersed adult entertainment establishments in a manner that did not severely restrict free speech rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. The ordinance, by its language, did not appear to be motivated by a distaste for the speech itself, but by a desire to preserve neighborhoods. Further, the court held that it was not necessary for the city to independently establish a factual basis to support the ordinance as applied to local conditions. The ordinance was sufficiently narrowly drawn to prevent problems which accompanied a concentration of adult entertainment establishments.


OUTCOME: The purported appeal was dismissed and the writ was denied because the court found that the ordinance was sufficiently narrowly drawn and therefore did not severely restrict free speech rights.

 

1217 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Harsany v. Cessna Aircraft Co.


Civ. No. 26190


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


148 Cal. App. 3d 1139; 196 Cal. Rptr. 374; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2433



October 25, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 17, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 393412, 393445, and 393446, Alfred Lord, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant aircraft company sought review of a judgment entered by the Superior Court of San Diego (California), after a jury awarded prejudgment interest to respondent administratrix in a case involving the crash of an airplane. The case was tried on a theory of strict products liability and negligence.


OVERVIEW: In a negligence and strict products liability action after the crash of an airplane, the trial court instructed the jury that it was entitled to award prejudgment interest upon an award to respondent administratrix from the date of the loss of the aircraft. Appellant aircraft company sought review of the part of the judgment entered after the jury awarded prejudgment interest to respondent. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. It reasoned that under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 3288, the trier-of fact may award prejudgment interest for the breach of an obligation not arising from contract. It held that respondent was entitled to be placed in the same position, from a monetary standpoint, that she would have been in had the crash not occurred. The court also held that the instruction to award interest from the date of the crash was proper because it correctly pointed to the time respondent's loss occurred. It reasoned that compensation would naturally be based on that date. The court also held that evidence of respondent's receipt of insurance proceeds was barred by the collateral source rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which awarded prejudgment interest to respondent administratrix in a negligence and strict products liability action against defendant aircraft company that arose out of the crash of an airplane. It did so because statute permitted the trier of fact to award prejudgment interest in an action for the breach of an obligation not arising from contract.

Fisher v. City of Berkeley


Civ. No. 52332.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


148 Cal. App. 3d 267; 195 Cal. Rptr. 836; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2303



October 24, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Dec. 28, 1983 (See 37 Cal. 3d 644)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 536602-6, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted respondent city's motion for judgment on the pleadings, declaring the city of Berkeley's Ordinance Adopting a Rent Stabilization and Eviction for Good Cause Program, Measure D, to be valid both on its face and as applied.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlords filed a complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief against respondent city, challenging the validity of the city of Berkeley's Ordinance Adopting a Rent Stabilization and Eviction for Good Cause Program, Measure D, alleging that the ordinance provisions constituted unreasonable restraints on alienation by inhibiting sales at a fair market value in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 711, which provided that conditions restraining alienation were void. The superior court granted respondent's motion for judgment on the pleadings, declaring Measure D to be valid both on its face and as applied. Appellants sought review of the superior court's judgment. On appeal, although the court found Cal. Civ. Code § 711 inapplicable to an ordinance, it declared that Measure D was an invalid ordinance on its face. The court reversed the superior court's judgment, holding that the ordinance was not reasonably related to its stated purpose of preventing excessive rents. The court found the ordinance consisted of unreasonable and cumbersome procedures that would result in unnecessary burdens on appellants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's granting of respondent city's motion for judgment on the pleadings, holding that the rent regulation ordinance was invalid on its face, inexcusably cumbersome, and not reasonably related to its stated purpose of preventing excessive rents, and thus it would deprive appellant landlords of due process of law if permitted to take effect.

 

1219 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cotati Alliance for Better Hous. v. City of Cotati


Civ. No. 53178


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


148 Cal. App. 3d 280; 195 Cal. Rptr. 825; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2304



October 24, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of all parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied December 28, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 108247, Joseph P. Murphy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which granted respondent alliance's petition holding that appellant's enacted ordinances were unconstitutional because due process required a value-based standard for determining rent adjustments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city initiated an ordinance to prevent exploitation of housing shortages by the imposition of excessive rent charges. Respondent alliance filed a complaint for relief challenging the validity of the ordinance. Respondent argued that the ordinance was facially invalid because it used an investment-based standard for determining rent adjustments. The trial court agreed, ruling it unconstitutional because due process required the use of a value-based standard instead, and enjoined enforcement. Appellant sought review. The court reversed that finding holding that an ordinance was invalid on its face only if its terms did not permit its administrators to avoid confiscatory results in its application. Appellant's ordinance was not so restrictive. The investment-based standard allowed the board enough flexibility to avoid confiscatory results in the ordinance's application; it was at least debatable that the return-on-investment-standard bore a rational relationship to a legitimate public purpose; and, it ensured that inflationary factors were not built into an ordinance's rent ceiling adjustment mechanism, and thereby ensured the rent control scheme's integrity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment by holding that a rent control ordinance requiring that landlords receive a fair investment return was constitutionally valid on its face as an economic regulation reasonably related to furthering an important government purpose.

Michael W. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 30231.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


148 Cal. App. 3d 297; 195 Cal. Rptr. 844; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2305



October 24, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 19, 1984 (L.A. 31855)
May 2, 1985, matter retrans. to Ct. App., 4th Dist; Div. 3, for reconsideration in light of Ramona R. v. Superior Court (1985) 37 Cal. 3d 802)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile offender requested that the court direct respondent Superior Court of Orange County (California) to vacate an order, which declared him unfit for treatment as a juvenile, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(b),(c), and refused to assure immunity to petitioner, based on the probation department's report, the murder allegation, and post mortem photographs of the victim.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner juvenile offender was declared unfit for treatment by respondent superior court, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(b),(c), based on the probation department's report, the murder allegation, and post mortem photographs of the victim, he requested that the court direct respondent to vacate its order. The court ordered the issuance of a peremptory writ prohibiting respondent from proceeding any further, other that to order preparation of a new probation report and to conduct a new hearing concerning petitioner's fitness, pursuant to § 707(c). The court held that petitioner's statements to the retained psychiatric expert and to a probation officer charged with preparing a § 707(c), fitness report were inadmissible for any purpose in any subsequent proceeding to determine guilt, because the limited exclusionary rule, which prevented the introduction of testimony by petitioner at the fitness hearing, except for the purpose of impeachment, was extended further to prevent the evidence of his statements to his probation officer, even for impeachment purposes.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ prohibiting respondent superior court from proceeding further, other than to order preparation of a new probation report and to conduct a new hearing concerning petitioner juvenile offender's fitness, because the subsequent use of statements to his probation officer in the detention interview and evidence of his mental condition were inadmissible under his privilege against self-incrimination.

 

1221 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Eversole v. Superior Court


No. AO21778


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


148 Cal. App. 3d 188; 195 Cal. Rptr. 816; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2297; 9 Media L. Rep. 2436



October 21, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let the writ issue commanding respondeat superior court to take no further action in this case other than to dismiss.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of respondent trial court, Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), convicting him of several counts of rape, unlawful sexual intercourse, and one count of oral copulation. Defendant argued that his commitment was unlawful within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 995 citing that the magistrate's closing of the preliminary hearing violated defendant's right to a public preliminary examination.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of raping two minor girls. During the preliminary hearing, over defendant's Cal. Penal Code §995 objection, the trial court by motion pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 868.7 closed the preliminary hearing during the testimony of one of defendant's alleged victims. The trial court found serious psychological harm might have followed if she were forced to testify in public. The trial court relied upon the victim's age, its own observations, her mother's testimony as to her preference for a closed hearing, and the testimony of the victim and her mother that testifying in an open hearing and publication of the testimony could possibly cause serious psychological harm to the victim. The court held that Cal. Penal Code § 868.7 involved two determinations prior to any decision whether to close the hearing and while the trial court found that serious psychological harm could result to the victim, it did not adhere to the second requirement by determining whether alternative procedures were available to avoid the harm. Accordingly, the judgment of the trial court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court ordering that the preliminary hearing be closed not because there was an insufficient showing that serious psychological harm was threatened, but because the trial court failed to make the additional statutorily required determination that no alternative procedures were available to avoid the perceived harm.

 

1222 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Valentine v. City of Oakland


No. AO18305


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


148 Cal. App. 3d 139; 196 Cal. Rptr. 59; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2291



October 20, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 554276-5, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the first sentence of paragraph three of the judgment of April 9, 1982, is modified in part to read as follows: ". . . City of Oakland Resolution No. 59916 CMS and the tax levied thereby in the amount of 0.1530% for the 1981 Pension Liability Fund are declared void and unconstitutional as in contravention of article XIII A of the California Constitution to the extent that they allow tax revenues generated thereby to be used to fund obligations of the City of Oakland to the Public Employees' Retirement System." As so modified, the judgment is affirmed, and the cause is remanded to the superior court to fix and award reasonable attorney fees, if any, to respondent Valentine for services rendered on this appeal ( Code Civ. Proc., § 1021.5). The order of May 13, 1982, awarding attorney fees and costs is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which declared that a property tax override was void and unconstitutional as contravening Cal. Const. art. XIII A in an action for declaratory relief brought under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 4808 by respondent taxpayer.


OVERVIEW: In an action for declaratory relief brought by respondent taxpayer under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 4808, the trial court held that a tax levied by appellant city to fund three pension systems was void and unconstitutional because the tax was of a greater percentage than allowed under Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 1(a). Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed a modified judgement after examining the Cal. Const. art. XIII A § 1(b) exemption to § 1(a). For the purposes of the § 1(b) exemption, the court held that the obligations under the three pensions systems constituted "indebtedness" and that the sums paid by petitioner to avoid defaulting on those obligations constituted the payment of "interest and redemption charges." However, the court held that only two of the three pensions systems had been directly submitted to and approved by voters as required by the § 1(b) exemption. Therefore, the court affirmed a modified judgment, finding that the tax levied by petitioner had been valid under § 1(b) to the extent that such tax funded petitioner's obligations to the two pension systems which had been approved by voters prior to the effective date of Cal. Const. art. art. XIII A.


OUTCOME: The court modified a judgment declaring that a property tax override was void and unconstitutional because the tax levy was authorized and hence constitutional to the extent that such levy applied to fund two pension systems approved by voters and affirmed the judgment as modified because the tax levy was unauthorized and unconstitutional to the extent that such levy applied to fund a third pension system not approved by voters.

 

1223 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Novick v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. 65316


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


148 Cal. App. 3d 325; 195 Cal. Rptr. 747; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2307



October 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 21 and November 2, 1983. The petition of plaintiff and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 162923, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment on the appeal is reversed; the order on the cross-appeal denying an award of attorney's fees is affirmed. Each party to bear his or its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment against it awarding appellee individual damages arising out of a civil rights lawsuit filed against appellant and various city police officers. Appellee challenged the trial court's determination denying his motion for attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the awards to appellee individual for violations of his civil rights emanating from an illegal search of his residence and business, his subsequent arrest, and his criminal prosecution in connection with his role in distributing pornography. The court concluded that appellee was awarded damages for the emotional strain of having to defend against criminal charges that were later filed, and for the attorney's fees incurred and not for per se the illegal search and seizure that occurred. The court held that this particular injury was not actionable under 42 U.S.C.S § 1983. The court found that while the filing of criminal charges by the city prosecutor most certainly was a foreseeable occurrence, such conduct, if independently commenced, acted as a severance of the chain of causation between appellee's cause of action under § 1983 and the damages that were awarded as the proximate result of that violation. No evidence existed to support an argument that the prosecutor's judgment was other than independent because causation had been severed. Appellee's request for attorney fees properly denied because he did not have a §1983 action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because appellee individual was awarded damages for the emotional strain of having to defend against criminal charges that were filed against him and not for the illegal search and seizure and this particular injury was not actionable as a civil rights violation.

 

1224 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Duran


Crim. No. 15149


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


147 Cal. App. 3d 1186; 195 Cal. Rptr. 724; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2275



October 14, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Certified for publication with the exception of section I.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 22, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 54908, Donald W. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified by adding 18 days to Duran's presentence credits. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which certified him as an adult, convicted him on one count of second degree murder pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code, §§ 187, 189, and modified the sentence of judgment by subtracting 18 days from the amount of credit defendant received for the time spent in the California Youth Authority for diagnostic testing.


OVERVIEW: Sixteen-year-old defendant participated in two gang-related killings, was certified as an adult and entered a guilty plea to one count of second-degree murder. Before sentencing, the trial court remanded him to the California Youth Authority (YA) for a diagnostic study. The trial court subtracted 18 days from the amount of credit defendant received. The court concluded that the trial court's amenability finding was supported by substantial evidence. But the court modified the trial court judgment by adding 18 days and found that a juvenile who was not committed to the YA but simply remanded there for an amenability evaluation pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2 and then sentenced to prison was entitled on equal protection grounds to conduct credits against his prison sentence for the diagnostic time spent at YA. An adult confined in a diagnostic facility before sentencing was entitled to conduct credits against his prison sentence for time spent at the facility, therefore a juvenile remanded to YA for an amenability evaluation and then sentenced to prison was also entitled to conduct credits against his prison sentence for the diagnostic time spent at YA.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by adding 18 days to defendant's presentence credits because a juvenile remanded to the California Youth Authority (YA) for an amenability evaluation and then sentenced to prison is entitled on equal protection grounds to conduct credits against his prison sentence for the diagnostic time spent at YA.

 

1225 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of L.A. v. Amwest Sur. Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 66646


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


147 Cal. App. 3d 961; 195 Cal. Rptr. 554; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2253



October 11, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1983, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 412055, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bail bond surety sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent county's motion for summary judgment in respondent's action seeking forfeiture of bail, and denied appellant's motion to vacate and set aside summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bail bond surety received notice of forfeiture from respondent county after a defendant appellant had posted bail bond for did not appear. Respondent's summary judgment motion on bail was granted, and appellant's motion to vacate was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to respondent and denial of appellant's motion to vacate, holding that there was no due process violation, as appellant was furnished with the notice of nonappearance, and appellant's motion to vacate was heard and denied. The court found that the summary judgment notice requirements of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c did not apply to bail bond summary judgments as, unlike § 437c, which dealt with adversarial civil actions, an action to enforce against a surety the obligation under a bail bond was not an adversary civil action as the surety had consented to summary judgment in the form of the bond. The court determined that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1058a was inapplicable, as there was no indication the legislature intended it to apply to bail bonds posted in criminal cases, and Cal. Penal Code § 1306, as a special statute, controlled over § 1058a, a general statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to respondent county, and the denial of appellant bail bond surety's motion to vacate and set aside summary judgment, because the statutes in question did not violate due process, and the civil procedure notice requirements on summary judgment did not apply to summary judgment on a bail bond.

People v. La Fargue


No. AO16228


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


147 Cal. App. 3d 878; 195 Cal. Rptr. 438; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2244



October 7, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Parts I, III, IV, and V are not published, as they do not meet the standards for publication contained in rule 976(b), California Rules of Court.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied November 2, 1983, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above on November 2 and 3, 1983. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 106548, Ollie Marie-Victoire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to conduct resentencing proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him of assault with force likely to produce great bodily injury pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 245(a) and sentenced him to the upper term of imprisonment of four years.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal was convicted of assault with force likely to produce great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 245(a) and sentenced to the upper term of imprisonment of four years. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not err by refusing to instruct the jury on the definition of "great bodily injury" as requested by appellant, because the term was not a technical term that required further elaboration and there were no unusual circumstances that required that the term be further defined. In addition, the court found that there was no impropriety in the trial court's reliance on the circumstances of youth, vulnerability, premeditation, and violence resulting in harm. However, the record did not support the trial court's finding to aggravate appellant's sentence based upon his having served a prior prison term in Cuba, because there was no showing that the Cuban proceedings satisfied American constitutional standards. On remand for resentencing, the trial court was instructed that it could not consider the prior prison term served pursuant to the Cuban conviction unless the constitutional validity thereof was determined first.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant criminal's conviction and held that the trial court did not err by refusing to instruct the jury on the definition of great bodily injury as requested by appellant and that there was no impropriety in the trial court's reliance on certain aggravating factors. However, the record did not support the trial court's finding to aggravate appellant's sentence based upon his having served a prior prison term in Cuba.
