People v. Harbor Hut Restaurant


Crim. No. 44177


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Six


147 Cal. App. 3d 1151; 196 Cal. Rptr. 7; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2271



October 6, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 21, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the San Luis Obispo County Judicial District of San Luis Obispo County, No. M-46888, Harold Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Appellate Department of the Superior Court for the County of San Luis Obispo is reversed, and the matter is ordered remanded to the Municipal Court for the County of San Luis Obispo.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff people challenged the decision of the Appellate Department of the Superior Court, San Luis Obispo County (California), which directed the suppression of evidence in an action involving a warrantless inspection of defendant wholesale fish dealer.


OVERVIEW: After finding previous reporting violations, Department of Fish and Game officers entered defendant wholesale fish dealer for the apparent purpose of determining whether defendant was maintaining adequate records of commercially caught fishes. Without attempting to obtain a search warrant, the officers discovered certain fish and requested to view defendant's records regarding those fish; they were incomplete. Defendant was cited for illegal possession of sport-caught fish in a seafood restaurant and for failure to fill out receipts for fish found on the premises, under Cal. Fish & Game Code §§ 7121, 8011. The fish and incomplete records were seized. The municipal court denied defendant's motion to suppress the fish, but the appellate department of the superior court reversed, directing the evidence to be suppressed. On appeal, the court reversed the decision to suppress the fish, finding that the warrant requirement would impede the "specific enforcement needs" of the statutory scheme surrounding the regulation of the wholesale fish industry. The statutes which permitted inspection were sufficiently circumscribed so as to satisfy the requirements of the U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision directing the suppression of evidence against defendant wholesale fish dealer, seized after a warrantless inspection. The warrant requirement would have impeded the enforcement needs associated with the regulation of the wholesale fish industry. The inspection program, in terms of certainty and regularity of its application, was a constitutionally adequate substitute for a warrant.

 

1228 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Roberts v. Gulf Oil Corp.


Civ. No. 5934


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


147 Cal. App. 3d 770; 195 Cal. Rptr. 393; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2237



October 4, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 1, 1983, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of defendants and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 28, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 163394, Joseph F. Noriega, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the denial of Assessor's access to "[unit] engineering reports, or records containing any report or study on the fire flood project to be carried out on the Fruitvale NW Unit and relied on in the purchase of leases in 1977 and 1978, and any report or study on any other enhanced recovery project to be carried out on leases purchased in 1977 and 1978 in Kern County and relied on in the purchase of such leases." In all other respects, it is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county tax assessor obtained a subpoena duces tecum under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 468, seeking information from defendant oil corporation. Defendant moved to limit the subpoena. The Superior Court of Kern County (California) held an evidentiary hearing, then ordered defendant to produce some of the information requested. Defendant appealed, and plaintiff cross-appealed as to the information not required to be produced.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county tax assessor sought detailed business records of defendant oil company with respect to oil and gas properties defendant owned in the county. Defendant sought to quash the subpoena, as to what it called interpretive data as opposed to raw data. The trial court held an evidentiary hearing, then ordered defendant to produce its proved reserves ledger, but suppressed other reports. Defendant appealed, and plaintiff cross-appealed as to the information suppressed. The court affirmed as to the information ordered produced, and further ordered the additional information produced. Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 441(d), 442, and 470 contained broad grants of power to the assessor to demand relevant information. Proprietary information was protected by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 451, 408, and 1605.4, which required the assessor to maintain confidentiality of such information. To the extent defendant had a recognizable privacy interest, when weighed against the assessor's need to fulfill its duty to properly assess property, the right of the assessor to the information was clear.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of defendant oil company as to the denial of plaintiff assessor's access to certain reports and studies, and affirmed the judgment insofar as it allowed plaintiff assessor access to defendant's information, because the statutory scheme granted the assessor broad powers to access the information it needed to properly assess the property, and any proprietary information was adequately protected.

 

1229 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Curran v. Mount Diablo Council of the Boy Scouts


Civ. No. 66755


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


147 Cal. App. 3d 712; 195 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2233; 38 A.L.R.4th 607



October 3, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 2, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 6, 1984. Mosk, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C365529, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment in favor of defendant is reversed and the trial court is directed to overrule the demurrer of defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in which the trial court sustained defendant association's demurrer and dismissed plaintiff's action for violations of the common law right of fair procedure and the Unruh Civil Rights Act for expulsion on the basis of homosexuality.


OVERVIEW: Defendant association expelled plaintiff from its membership based on his homosexuality. Thereafter, plaintiff sued defendant, alleging that his expulsion was based on unfair procedure and an improper reason and was in violation of the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act). The trial court sustained defendant's general demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed the action. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and directed the trial court to overrule the demurrer, holding that the expulsion constituted a violation of both the common law right of fair procedure and the Act. Using the status of homosexuality as a basis of expulsion was substantively arbitrary and therefore violative of the common law right of fair procedure. Further, homosexuality could not lawfully be used as a basis to expel in the absence of a rational connection between homosexual conduct and any significant danger of harm to defendant resulting from the continued membership of plaintiff. Finally, the court determined that defendant was a business establishment within the meaning of the Act and was therefore prohibited from all discrimination in the provision of its services.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of defendant association and directed the trial court to overrule the demurrer, holding that defendant's expulsion of plaintiff from membership on the basis of his homosexuality constituted a violation of both the common law right of fair procedure and the Unruh Civil Rights Act.

 

1230 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Beach


Crim. No. 43047


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


147 Cal. App. 3d 612; 195 Cal. Rptr. 381; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2224



September 30, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 364777, James M. Ideman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: This case is remanded back to the trial court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed herein. The judgment of conviction is otherwise affirmed on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found her guilty of involuntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192.2. Appellant claimed that that banishment was an unconstitutional condition of probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192.2 as a necessarily included offense of the charge of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187. The trial court granted appellant probation which included banishment and appellant appealed. The court remanded for resentencing finding that banishment was an unconstitutional condition of probation because its value did not manifestly outweigh the impairment of appellant's constitutional rights. The court otherwise affirmed appellant's conviction finding that community service was within the sound discretion of the trial judge, interlineation of jury instructions was not reversible error, no evidence rebutted the presumption that the trial judge showed counsel modified jury instructions prior to showing them to the jury, defendant did not have a right to refuse instructions on lesser included offenses, brandishing a weapon was not a lesser included offense to the crime of murder, failure to instruct the jury on general intent was harmless error, trial court's control of cross examination was not a manifest abuse of discretion, and there was no prosecutorial misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court for resentencing because banishment as a condition of probation was unconstitutional, and affirmed the remainder of defendant's conviction for involuntary manslaughter.

 

1231 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bach v. County of Butte


Civ. No. 21774


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


147 Cal. App. 3d 554; 195 Cal. Rptr. 268; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2218



September 29, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 15, 1983. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 67168, Loyd H. Mulkey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which held that he failed to state a claim for relief in his action against defendants city, county, and officials under the Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorney sued defendants city, county, and officials under the Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. §1983, alleging that they conspired to injure him by filing and prosecuting a criminal action against him although it was baseless. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer on the grounds that the complaint failed to state a cause of action, because it was barred by prosecutorial immunity. On appeal by plaintiff, the court affirmed, basing its determination on federal law. While a municipality's policy or custom resulting in constitutional injury might be actionable even though individual public servants were shielded by good faith immunity, plaintiff's conclusory allegations in his complaint lacked sufficient specificity to make a case. The court declined to hold that acts of a deputy prosecutor constituted the custom or policy of local governmental agencies, as it would invite end runs around the prosecutorial immunity doctrine. The only arguable bases in plaintiff's complaint for defendants' liability was predicated on enactment and enforcement of an earthquake resolution or the acts of the deputy district attorney, neither of which was sufficient as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing plaintiff attorney's claim against defendants city, county, and officials, as they had prosecutorial immunity when they filed criminal charges against plaintiff, even if such charges turned out to be baseless.

 

1232 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Zavala


Crim. No. 43973


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


147 Cal. App. 3d 429; 195 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2205



September 27, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1983, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 23, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. V 091675, Juaneita M. Veron, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed, and the case is remanded to the municipal court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District (California), which granted respondent's motion to have a prior conviction declared constitutionally invalid, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 23102.2. The appeal was transferred from the appellate department of the superior court on the court's motion, pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 62(a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with driving under the influence of alcohol and with having a prior conviction for the same offense. The trial court granted respondent's motion to have the prior conviction declared constitutionally invalid and the state, sought review. The only evidence produced was the municipal court's docket sheet, which reflected that respondent had been advised of and waived his rights. The court ordered a reporter's transcript of the advisement and waiver as part of its power to judicially notice the records of other courts under Cal. Evid. Code §§ 452(d)(1), 459(a). The court pointed out that the transcript showed that respondent acknowledged before the court that he had heard and understood the advisement in Spanish and then his guilty plea and waiver of rights was taken. Respondent's reliance on the docket alone was insufficient. In reversing and remanding, the court found that when the docket showed that a further record existed, respondent had the obligation to examine the complete record and furnish proof pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 23102.2. While the state had the burden of proof, respondent had the burden of production.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed because respondent failed to show with specificity how his constitutional rights were denied and failed to produce evidence on the question of whether his rights were denied, even though further evidence was available on that question. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

1233 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Velasco v. Mun. Court


No. AO20777


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


147 Cal. App. 3d 340; 195 Cal. Rptr. 108; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2195



September 26, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the San Francisco Municipal Court to set aside its order overruling petitioner's demurrer and to enter a new order sustaining the demurrer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent municipal court (California) to set aside its order that overruled petitioner's demurrer to charges under Cal. Penal Code § 650 1/2(4), (5) because the statute was unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. The court had previously issued an alternative writ of mandate after the superior court denied a writ.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner filed a demurrer to two counts of willfully, wrongfully, and for accomplishing a lewd and licentious purpose, using the name of another in a manner that had a tendency to affect moral reputation in the estimation of the person(s) to whom it was used, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 650 1/2(4), (5). Respondent Municipal Court for the San Francisco Judicial District of the City and County of San Francisco overruled the demurrer and petitioner sought a writ of mandate in the superior court, which was denied. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent to set aside its order and to enter an order sustaining the demurrer because there was no point to construing the statute to avoid ambiguities. Section § 650 1/2(4), (5) was void for vagueness and overbreadth. The court noted its duty to effect some reasonable interpretation to save the provisions from being struck down as unconstitutional. However, the court could not discern the legislative will, and any gloss that might be applied to the language would reflect only the court's view of what the law should be. The court concluded that such a posture was a usurpation of the legislative function.


OUTCOME: The peremptory writ of mandate was issued, directing respondent municipal court to set aside its order and to enter a new order sustaining petitioner's demurrer because the statute was void for vagueness and overbreadth. When construed to avoid ambiguities and to operate in areas permitted by the first amendment, the statute overlapped defamation and obscenity laws and the statute expressly did not apply where other punishment was prescribed.

People v. Superior Court (brotherton)


No. AO21494


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


147 Cal. App. 3d 281; 195 Cal. Rptr. 96; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2190



September 23, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 25, 1983. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.

CASE SUMMARY:

Petitioner people's motion for relief under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(o) was denied and the case was dismissed. Petitioner filed a new complaint under § 1387. Real party moved to suppress the evidence, claiming that petitioner was barred from introducing evidence previously suppressed. Respondent granted real party's motion. The court denied petitioner's writ of mandate ruling that petitioner failed to pursue available appellate remedies following respondent's order which granted the motion to suppress. The court held that petitioner was precluded under § 1538.5(d) from relitigating the same suppressed issues in the new complaint filed under § 1387.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner people's writ and ruled that because petitioner failed to pursue available appellate remedies following respondent superior court's order granting real party's motion, it was precluded from relitigating the same suppressed issues in the new complaint.

People v. Salas


Crim. No. 42408.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


147 Cal. App. 3d 158; 195 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2177



September 21, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certified for partial publication. * 


* The opinion is certified for publication, other than rubrics I, II, and III.


Hg. den. Dec. 28, 1983 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A333544, Nancy Belcher Watson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the appellate court's reversal of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). The trial court had granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence and dismissed his case but on an appeal by the People, the appellate court convicted and sentenced defendant pursuant to a plea agreement for one count of narcotics possession for sale.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and co-defendant were jointly charged with narcotic offenses. The trial court granted a motion to suppress evidence and the cases were dismissed. On an appeal by the People, the appellate court reversed the order. Parties entered into a negotiation and defendant pled guilty to one count of drug possession for sale. The sentencing of defendant was delayed for several months because defendant did not appear and a bench warrant was necessary to bring him before the court. When defendant appeared, his court-appointed counsel sought to be relieved because defendant had retained private counsel. On the ground that he had been retained only to argue defendant's request for extensive prejudgment credit, new defense counsel sought a continuance that was denied. Defense counsel tried to argue the credit matter, but refused to comment on probation report on the ground of inadequate time to prepare. Defendant was sentenced in accordance with negotiated plea. Defendant argued he was entitled to prejudgment credit. The reviewing court affirmed the order and denial of prejudgment credit because there was no factual relationship between the charge in prior case and the present charge.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant was properly convicted and sentenced pursuant to a plea agreement for one count of narcotics possession for sale, because the application of caselaw regarding the denial of extensive prejudgment credit was not unconstitutional and defendant could not say that he had relied on a more favorable construction regarding the sentencing issue when he committed his offenses.

 

1236 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Rue-Ell Enters. v. City of Berkeley


Civ. No. 51415


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


147 Cal. App. 3d 81; 194 Cal. Rptr. 919; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2168



September 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 10, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 518682-6, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) rendering judgment in favor of respondent city in appellant's complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief against city alleging that a property tax relief ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to commercial leases.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sued respondent city, arguing that city's renter property tax relief ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to two commercial leases pursuant to U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Cal. Const. art I, § 9. Under the ordinance, city required a one-year reduction in rents provided in existing leases on commercial property measured by a percentage of the landlord's property tax savings resulting form the passage of Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIII A. The trial court rendered judgment for city. The court affirmed. The respondent was empowered to pass such legislation without violating constitutional prohibitions against impairment of the obligation of contracts. The trial court properly found no substantial impairment because the combined effect of the tax relief measure and the ordinance was to allow landlords to gain a net windfall of 20 percent of their tax savings. Thus, it did not restrict appellant's gains under the leases, but permitted it to retain 20 percent of its unexpected windfall.


OUTCOME: The order rendering judgment in respondent city's favor was affirmed because city, by virtue of its authority in the exercise of its police power to regulate rents, could require a one-year reduction in rents provided in existing leases on commercial property without violating the constitutional prohibitions against impairment of the obligation of contracts.

 

1237 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Pope v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 67621


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


146 Cal. App. 3d 1132; 194 Cal. Rptr. 883; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2156



September 14, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 21, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C331120, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county tax assessor sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which rendered judgment in favor of respondent board of equalization and respondent property owners, and which established a base year for valuation in accordance with rules promulgated by appellant board of equalization.


OVERVIEW: Respondent board of equalization promulgated rules directing appellant county tax assessor to determine the base value for a completed portion of a new multi-stage construction project in the year in which that portion was completed. Appellant determined the base value for buildings owned by respondent property owners by re-appraising the entire portion when all construction was complete. Respondent property owners, as interveners, sought a declaration to establish the base year for their properties. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondents. The court held that the base year value should be set at the time a given unit within a multiple structure development was capable of being occupied and utilized without regard to the completion of the remaining structures. The court rejected appellant's contention that construction was not complete until all major tenant improvements were installed and held that it was reasonable to characterize a building as "available for use" at the point its final certificate of occupancy issued, regardless of whether the tenant improvements were substantially complete. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent board of equalization and respondent property owners because the rules promulgated by respondent board of equalization were proper for the determination of base years for new construction.

L& M Prof'l Consultants v. Ferreira


Civ. No. 24836


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


146 Cal. App. 3d 1038; 194 Cal. Rptr. 695; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2147



September 13, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 5, 1983. Hearing Denied November 23, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 441118, 444681, James L. Focht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which affirmed a resolution of respondent city that consented to an easement for respondent developer on appellants' property and granted such easement pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 1001 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1245.325.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city adopted a resolution that consented to a private condemnation by respondent developer of an easement across property owned by appellant landowners. The trial court refused to invalidate the resolution and awarded respondent developer a judgment of condemnation for the easement under Cal. Civ. Code § 1001 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1245.325. Appellants contended that § 1001 and § 1245.325 were unconstitutional, that respondent city denied them due process, and that respondent city's resolution of consent was invalid. The court affirmed and held that § 1001 was constitutional because a requirement of great necessity for a private condemnation was not vague. The court held that § 1001 and § 1245.325 were constitutional on their face because no public use requirement was necessary and that § 1001 and § 1245.325 were constitutional as applied because a taking to provide sewer drainage was for the purpose of public use. The court held that respondent city's decision did not violate the principles of due process and that respondent city's decision was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which affirmed a resolution of respondent city that consented to an easement for respondent developer on appellants' property and granted such easement. The court held that the state eminent domain statutes applied in the case were constitutional and that respondent city's decision was supported by substantial evidence.

Boyll v. State Pers. Bd.


No. AO17474


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


146 Cal. App. 3d 1070; 194 Cal. Rptr. 717; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2149



September 13, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 10, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 80443, William E. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which dismissed her claims against respondent state agencies. Appellant contended that she was not disabled from working as a correctional officer by the terms of Cal. Gov't. Code § 1029 because no felony judgment was entered against her, that she was pardoned of any wrongdoing, and that § 1029 was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to her.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant was disqualified from securing a position as a correctional officer by respondent state agencies. The decision was based on appellant's prior felony drug conviction. However, in light of appellant's successful completion of a rehabilitation program, the felony judgment was dismissed under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3200. Furthermore, appellant was eventually granted a full and unconditional pardon for the offense. Appellant filed a complaint against respondents. Respondents demurred, and the trial court sustained the demurrer without granting appellant leave to amend. On review, appellant argued that she was not convicted within the meaning of Cal. Gov't. Code § 1029, that the pardon and dismissal of charges removes any disability under § 1029, and that § 1029 was unconstitutional on its face and as applied to appellant. The court ruled that because judgment had not been entered against appellant, she was not "convicted" or "disabled" as defined by § 1029. Because appellant was never convicted of a felony, the court declined to address the constitutional issues or the effect of a pardon on appellant's eligibility to become a correctional officer.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision to dismiss appellant applicant's claims against respondent state agencies was reversed. The court held that because appellant never had a felony conviction entered against her by a court, she could not be disabled from applying for a position as a correctional officer.

San Diego Union v. City Council


Civ. No. 26018


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


146 Cal. App. 3d 947; 196 Cal. Rptr. 45; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2136



September 7, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 454659, Gerald J. Lewis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city council challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of respondent newspaper mandating appellant hold public sessions when discussing or determining salaries of city nonelected officers or employees as enumerated in Cal. Gov't Code § 54957 and permanently enjoining appellant from meeting in executive session to discuss and/or determine such salaries.


OVERVIEW: Respondent newspaper obtained an order enjoining appellant city council from discussing and determining salaries of nonelected city employees in closed session. Appellant challenged the order, claiming that the "personnel exception" to the Ralph M. Brown Act (Act), Cal. Gov't Code § 54957, relieved appellant of the duty to hold open sessions when matters of "employment," including salaries were being discussed. The court disagreed, holding that the purpose of the Act, to open government to the citizens, required a narrow interpretation of the "personnel exception." The court determined that the "personnel exception" to the rule requiring open sessions only pertained to discussions of an employee's job performance, not salary determinations, which impacted a city's budget. The court also held that because the Act addressed a matter of statewide concern, it was not unconstitutional as applied to a municipality. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the Brown Act prohibited exclusion of the public during appellant city council sessions at which salaries are discussed, but permitted exclusion for the specific purpose of evaluating a nonelected employee's job performance.

 

1241 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Kawasaki Motors Corp. v. County of Orange


Civ. No. 30496


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Three


146 Cal. App. 3d 780; 194 Cal. Rptr. 608; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2118



August 30, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 349657, Linda Hodge McLaughlin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant County of Orange appealed the decision from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted summary judgment to plaintiff taxpayer in its suit to recover tangible property taxes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer was a motorcycle corporation which was exempt from local taxation for its imports under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 225 et seq. The exemption scheme was held unconstitutional, and defendant County of Orange assessed escaped taxes against plaintiff. Subsequently, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 225.3 was passed to prevent retroactive application of the new rule. Plaintiff filed suit to recover its payments. Summary judgment was granted for plaintiff, and defendant appealed. The court affirmed. Section 225.3 was not an unconstitutional violation of the commerce clause. While the general rule was that a court decision overruling a statute or prior decision would have full retroactivity, states had the equitable power to limit retroactivity. The statute had a legitimate public interest, that of lessening the tax burden on certain businesses, and it was the most legitimate means of achieving the public interest. The alternative was to void the entire tax and refund all taxes which would have been an enormous burden. Limiting retroactivity was the most reasonable, constitutional means.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of plaintiff taxpayer in action against defendant County of Orange to recover its escaped tax assessment was affirmed. The legislation limiting the retroactivity of a court decision which had found that a tax exemption rule was unconstitutional was itself not unconstitutional because it served the legitimate function of limiting the tax burden on business and was the least intrusive means to accomplish the goal.

 

1242 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Armstrong v. County of San Mateo


No. AO15543


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


146 Cal. App. 3d 597; 194 Cal. Rptr. 294; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2100



August 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 23, 1983. Smith, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 10, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein. Bird, C. J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 233408; Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 428754; Melvin E. Cohn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state board of equalization and defendant counties sought review of the judgments of the Superior Court of San Mateo County and Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that granted plaintiff taxpayers tax refunds and injunctive relief in plaintiffs' action challenging the interpretation of the 2 percent inflation factor provision of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(b).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers contended that the legislative and administrative interpretation of the 2 percent inflation factor provision of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(b) was unconstitutional. The trial courts found in favor of plaintiffs. On appeal, defendant state board of equalization and counties contended that the constitutional provision was ambiguous and required the legislative and administrative interpretations that were applied. The court agreed with defendants' contentions, finding that the provision was ambiguous because it was susceptible to two mutually exclusive interpretations. The court noted that the provision did not prohibit the interpretation applied by the legislature, that the results of the interpretation statutes did not produce absurd consequences, and that the legislature's interpretation was not in conflict with the constitution. The court held that in the absence of a conflict between the challenged statute and the state constitution, the legislature's interpretation of the constitutional provision was presumptively valid. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment of the trial courts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the trial courts. The court held that the legislature's interpretation of the constitutional provision applied because plaintiff taxpayers did not prove that the provision was intended to have a different meaning, because the interpretation was compatible with the provision, and because the presumption under the rules of construction compelled a finding that the legislature's interpretation was valid.

I.S.L.E. v. County of Santa Clara


No. AO21741


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


147 Cal. App. 3d 72; 194 Cal. Rptr. 854; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2167



August 25, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 452159, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting summary judgment to plaintiffs on the first and third causes of action is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter summary judgment in favor of defendants on those two causes of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city, county board, and others, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, an organization and others, in plaintiff's action attacking the annexations of lands.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, an organization and others, filed suit against defendants, a city, county board, and others, challenging the validity of defendant city's 16 annexations of areas under 100 acres in size, or islands, substantially surrounded by defendant city, pursuant to the Municipal Organization Act of 1977 (Act), Cal. Gov't Code § 35000 et seq. Cal. Gov't Code § 35150(f). The Act allowed an annexation of islands without an annexation election and plaintiffs argued that § 3150(f) denied them equal protection of the law because residents of areas exceeding 100 acres in size were entitled to vote on annexation to a city under the Act. Plaintiffs also claimed that the multiple annexations constituted a municipal reorganization subject to an election. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, but the appellate court reversed, holding that the multiple island annexations were not a municipal reorganization. Also, the Act was constitutional because it was rationally related to the legitimate state purpose of avoiding annexation election expense for small communities. The Act further promoted the efficient formation of city boundaries.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, an organization and others, in plaintiffs' suit challenging the validity of 16 annexations made under the Municipal Organization Act (Act). The Act did not offend equal protection by dispensing with the need for an annexation election for the 100 acre areas annexed, although providing for elections for larger area annexations.

 

1244 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Chevrolet Motor Div. v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


No. AO15529


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


146 Cal. App. 3d 533; 194 Cal. Rptr. 270; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2096



August 25, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied September 23, 1983, and the petitions of all the appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied October 20, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 777974, Frank W. Shaw, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a car dealer and two car dealer associations, and the New Motor Vehicle Board (Board), sought review of an decision by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) granting a peremptory writ of mandamus. The writ ordered the Board to set aside its decision to sustain appellant car dealer's protest to respondent corporation's decision not to extent a franchise agreement.


OVERVIEW: The New Motor Vehicle Board (Board) sustained appellant car dealer's protest to respondent corporation's decision not to offer a new franchise agreement when appellant car dealer's old agreement expired. Respondent filed an action seeking to require the Board to vacate its decision. Appellants, a car dealer and two car dealer associations, and the Board challenged the trial court's grant of a peremptory writ of mandamus, and an order that a decision of the Board to be set aside. On appeal the court affirmed the order to set aside the Board's decision because the trial court did not err when it concluded that participation of the Board's dealer members in the proceedings denied respondent of an unbiased tribunal. The court stated that the presence of biased members on the Board presented a substantial probability that decisions in dealer-manufacturer disputes would have been made on the basis of inappropriate considerations, and the fact that those members did not technically decide the disputes did not alter that probability. The Board was still required by Cal. Veh. Code § 3001 to have four dealer members. The statute neither required nor authorized manufacturer members.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's order that the New Motor Vehicle Board (Board) set aside its decision to sustain appellant car dealer's protest to respondent corporation's decision not to extend a franchise agreement. The trial court did not err in concluding that the presence of the Board's dealer members denied respondent of an unbiased tribunal.

 

1245 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Babylon


Crim. No. 12541.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,THIRD Dist.


146 Cal. App. 3d 386; 194 Cal. Rptr. 134; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2082; 54 Rad. Reg. 2d (P 6;F) 1085



August 23, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Dec. 1, 1983 (See 39 Cal. 3d 70)


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Municipal Court District of Sacramento County, Nos. 48874M and 48983M, Roger K. Warren, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the order of the Municipal Court for the Municipal Court District of Sacramento County (California), which convicted them, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 593e, of selling devices with the intention of facilitating the interception or decoding of an over-the-air transmission by a subscription television service.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sold conversion equipment for profit, knowing that it would be used to intercept a paid-for television signal sold by a distributor to its subscribers and transmitted by a multipoint distribution service (MDS) under contract to the distributor. Defendants were convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 593e, which prohibited interception of over-the-air encoded transmissions authorized by the Federal Communication Commission. Defendants appealed. The court affirmed, because § 593e was intended to encompass transmissions from MDS stations as well as commercial television broadcast stations. The court held that § 593e was not void for vagueness or overbreadth, but gave defendants warning of the criminal nature of facilitating the interception of a pay television service, regardless of its source. The court held that in the absence of congressional intent to preempt state law with the Federal Communications Act, the state properly exercised its police power by criminalizing the interception and decoding of subscription television transmissions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because the statute under which defendants were convicted was not void for vagueness or overbreadth, but was a valid exercise of the state's police power in criminalizing the interception and decoding of subscription television transmissions.

 

1246 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City Council v. S.


Civ. No. 52226


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


146 Cal. App. 3d 320; 194 Cal. Rptr. 110; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2076



August 22, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 445534, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city finance director sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which ruled in favor of respondent city council in a tax validation proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city council tentatively approved a resolution to establish a maintenance district for median islands in the vicinity of an industrial park, and proposed to levy assessments based on estimated benefits. When respondent filed a petition for validation of the maintenance district, appellant city finance director filed an answer challenging the levy of assessment, contending that the state statute relating to maintenance districts required respondent to levy on an ad valorem basis, and that the special levy was subject to the limitation of Cal. Const. art. XIIIB. Respondent filed suit, and the trial court entered judgment in favor of respondent. Affirming the trial court's judgment, the court held that maintenance of the median islands was strictly of local interest and respondent was free to levy the special assessment on an estimated benefits basis. The court further held that the proceeds of the special assessment were not "proceeds of taxes" and, therefore, were not subject to the limitations of Cal. Const. art XIIIB.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent city council because the special assessment levied on the basis of estimated benefits within the maintenance district was not an ad valorem tax, nor was it the proceeds of taxes within the meaning of the state constitution.

 

1247 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Valley View Home of Beaumont v. Dep't of Health Servs.


Civ. No. 29340


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


146 Cal. App. 3d 161; 194 Cal. Rptr. 56; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2060



August 17, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 1, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 145616, John H. Barnard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant department of health services appealed the order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which entered judgment, based on the law of forfeiture, in favor of plaintiff provider on its claim for money due from the state for services rendered to Medi-Cal beneficiaries.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff provider rendered intermediate care to developmentally disabled patients, most of whom were Medi-Cal beneficiaries. Plaintiff received payment for services pursuant to a provider agreement with defendant department of health. Plaintiff failed to timely submit treatment authorization request forms by the September 30, 1980, expiration date for the six-month authorization period. Defendant authorized treatment as of the date it received the forms but denied services rendered between October 1 and 19, 1980, under Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, § 51343(b). Plaintiff filed a claim for breach of contract, substantial performance, and quasi-contract. The trial court entered judgment for plaintiff for the value of services rendered from October 1 to 19. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed because the trial court properly relieved plaintiff from a forfeiture because of clerical inadvertence. The court held that the equities militated in favor of relaxing the regulation, considering that the beneficiaries' condition did not change, that defendant had authorized the same level of care in prior years, and that plaintiff did not commit fraud or gross negligence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award for the value of services rendered because a gross injustice would have arisen if plaintiff provider was not relieved of a loss in the nature of a forfeiture and from the regulation of defendant department of health requiring the timely submission of treatment authorization forms for Medi-Cal beneficiaries.

 

1248 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Oliver


Crim. No. 4914.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


146 Cal. App. 3d 890; 194 Cal. Rptr. 561; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2130



August 12, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 20, 1983 (See 168 Cal. App. 3d 920)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A622258, Edward M. Ross, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a jury conviction from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and arson in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 451(b), with a finding of special circumstances that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in the commission of arson as provided by Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(viii).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged jury convictions for first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and arson in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 451(b). The jury also found to be true the special circumstance that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in the commission of arson, as provided by Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(viii). Defendant threw a Molotov cocktail into a house with the intention of setting it ablaze to get even with the woman who lived there because she had spurned defendant's marriage proposal. Instead, the woman's guest was killed in the flames. On appeal, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's felony-murder finding, whether defendant's intent was to cause a fire or to cause a death. The indictment was not fatally defective, even though it incorporated an outdated statutory citation for arson, because Cal. Gov't. Code § 9604 required any reference to an outdated statutory enactment to be treated as a reference to a current enactment. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first-degree murder and arson convictions as well as the jury's special finding that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in committing arson. It was immaterial to defendant's felony murder conviction whether defendant set the blaze with the intention of killing the woman's guest or not.

 

1249 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Zike v. State Pers. Bd.


No. AO15409


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 817; 193 Cal. Rptr. 766; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2018



August 10, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 2, 1983, and the petition of all respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 5, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 545155-1, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  Accordingly, we reverse the judgment and remand the matter with directions to the trial court to issue the writ of mandate compelling the board to pay plaintiff his back salary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent state personnel board to set aside its decision to deny him back salary. Respondent justified its decision under Cal. Gov't Code § 19503.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state employee who had been terminated under the automatic resignation provision of Cal. Gov't Code § 19503 and was then reinstated sought back pay from respondent state personnel board. The trial court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent to set aside its decision to deny him back pay. The court reversed the trial court. The court observed that due process protections must have been accorded to the valuable property right of a state employee to continue in his position. It held that invoking the summary procedures of § 19503 where a factual dispute regarding the authority for a state employee's absence from work was present violated procedural due process by failing to provide adequate preremoval safeguards. The court concluded that respondent erred in denying appellant any back pay because, pursuant to due process procedures, appellant should not automatically been deprived of his salary for the period prior to his hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the matter with directions to issue a writ of mandate compelling respondent state personnel board to pay appellant state employee his back salary. It held that invoking the summary provisions of the automatic resignation statute violated procedural due process by failing to provide adequate preremoval safeguards.

Escondido Imps. v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


Civ. Nos. 26797, 26802


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 834; 193 Cal. Rptr. 772; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 2020



August 10, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 26, 1983, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 5, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. N16587 and N16588, Don Martinson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an automobile dismantler and an automobile dealer, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant Department of Motor Vehicles, and denied summary judgment to plaintiffs on their complaint for a declaration that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 4456.1 and 9263, which provided for assessment of certain service fees without advance notice or hearing, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, an automobile dismantler and an automobile dealer, claimed that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 4456.1 and 9263, which provided for assessment of certain service fees without advance notice or hearing, were unconstitutional because they represented a taking of a significant property interest without notice or opportunity to be heard. The trial court held that there was no constitutional violation, and the court affirmed. The court held that the nature and timing of the process due, including whether predeprivation process was required, called for consideration of three distinct factors: the private interest that would be affected by the official action, the risk of an erroneous deprivation of such interest through the procedures used and the probable value, if any, of additional or substitute procedural safeguards, and the governments interest, including the function involved and the burdens that additional or substitute process would entail. Applying those factors, the court held that given the burden on the state of applying an additional procedural requirement and the minimal private interest directly involved, due process did not require a preassessment hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which granted defendant Department of Motor Vehicles summary judgment in plaintiffs', automobile dismantler and automobile dealer, complaint for a declaration that statutes unlawfully assessed service fees without advance notice or hearing, because the state's interest and the burden of applying additional procedural requirements outweighed the minimal private interest involved and the low probability of error..

 

1251 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Fain


No. AO22049


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


145 Cal. App. 3d 540; 193 Cal. Rptr. 483; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1987



July 28, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a rehearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 30, 1983. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The writ of habeas corpus is granted. Petitioner shall be discharged from the custody of the California Department of Corrections, subject, however, to the parole heretofore granted petitioner and the conditions and restrictions contained therein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus seeking review of an executive order issued by the governor of the state of California, which suspended petitioner's release date and ordered the state Board of Prison Terms to retain him in custody pending a parole rescission hearing before the governor's representative and a gubernatorial decision as to petitioner's suitability for parole.


OVERVIEW: Three weeks prior to petitioner inmate's scheduled release, the governor issued an executive order suspending petitioner's release date and ordering the state Board of Prison Terms (board) to retain petitioner in custody pending a parole rescission hearing before the governor's representative and a gubernatorial decision as to his suitability for parole. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus and the court granted the petition, holding that the governor did not possess the power to withhold a parole release date, which power was committed exclusively to the board, and had acted in excess of his jurisdictional authority. The court found that nowhere in the state penal code was there any provision for the governor to share the board's power to grant or withhold parole. The court found there were fundamental differences between pardon and parole and, more significantly, the statutory, regulatory and case law distinctions between the revocation of parole and the rescission of a parole release date.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner inmate's writ of habeas corpus because the governor did not possess the power to withhold a parole release date and had acted in excess of his jurisdictional authority. The court ordered that petitioner be discharged from the custody of the California Department of Corrections, subject to the parole granted petitioner and the conditions and restrictions contained therein.
