Morris v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 22274


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


145 Cal. App. 3d 561; 193 Cal. Rptr. 496; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1988



July 28, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 29, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its order of September 23, 1982, and to issue a new order denying Walker's motion to designate his wife as a legal representative. The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged. The stay previously issued is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners warden and Department of Corrections sought review of the order of respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which allowed real party in interest inmate to designate his wife as his legal representative pursuant to Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15, § 3175.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest inmate instituted civil proceedings in propria persona against petitioners warden and Department of Corrections (department) challenging conditions of his confinement and sought to have his wife designated his legal representative under Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15, § 3175. Respondent superior court granted this application and petitioners sought a peremptory writ compelling respondent to vacate this order. On appeal, the court granted the peremptory writ. The court held that the U.S. Const. amend. VI right to counsel did not apply to collateral postconviction proceedings and that an inmate's rights, when representing himself, were not identical with rights exercised by an attorney. The court held the Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15 § 3175 limitation of confidential legal visits to attorneys or their designees did not violate inmates' right of adequate, effective, and meaningful access to the courts, the regulation was reasonable in light of security risks in penal institutions, deference was due petitioner department's determinations, and the regulation did not deny real party in interest reasonable access to the courts.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners warden and Department of Corrections' (department) petition for peremptory writ compelling respondent superior court to vacate its order permitting real party in interest inmate to designate his wife as his legal representative because respondent department's regulations limiting legal representatives to attorneys was reasonable in light of security risks and did not deny real party interest access to the courts.

 

1253 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Carian v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


Civ. No. 20243.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


145 Cal. App. 3d 508; 193 Cal. Rptr. 434; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1986



July 27, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 15, 1983 (See 36 Cal. 3d 654)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an agricultural employer, sought a statutory review of a final decision and order of respondent, the California Agricultural Labor Relations Board, which determined that petitioner had committed unfair labor practices in failing to provide a complete and accurate pre-petition employee list and in unlawfully interrogating employees through the use of employee information cards.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board charged petitioner, an agricultural employer, with unfair labor practices for failing to provide a complete and accurate pre-petition employee list and for unlawfully interrogating employees through use of employee information cards. The administrative law officer found in respondent's favor, and respondent affirmed the decision. Petitioner sought judicial review. In affirming in part, the court concluded that, although employees were entitled to withhold private information from their employer, this did not excuse petitioner from providing the most complete and accurate information available to him to the respondent in accordance with established regulations. In annulling in part and remanding, however, the court concluded that the determination that petitioner had committed an unfair labor practice in using employee information cards to gather such information was contrary to law and unsupported by substantial evidence where there was no evidence that the purported "interrogation" resulted in any interference, restraint, or coercion of employees in the exercise of their rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part where the right of employees to withhold private information from petitioner agricultural employer did not relieve petitioner of his obligation to provide respondent agricultural labor relations board with the most complete information available. The judgment was annulled in part and remanded because petitioner's use of employee information cards to gather information did not constitute an unfair labor practice.

 

1254 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Alternatives for Cal. Women v. County of Contra Costa


Civ. No. 49524


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


145 Cal. App. 3d 436; 193 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1977



July 27, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On August 26, 1983, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 189902, Robert Joseph Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment under review is reversed. The cause is remanded to the superior court with directions to enter a judgment in favor of plaintiff and appellant Alternatives for California Women, Inc., consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied appellant's injunctive relief and declared that an ordinance of respondent county, Contra Costa County Ordinance Code § 56-4.408, which prohibited door-to-door solicitation in the evenings, was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant association received contributions by door-to-door canvassing and challenged Contra Costa County Ordinance Code § 56-4.408, which was enacted by respondent county to prohibit soliciting between sunset and sunrise. Respondent contended that the ordinance was necessary to prevent crime and to protect citizens' privacy. Respondent amended the ordinance during the pendency of the appeal, but the court did not render the appeal moot because the amendment did not significantly affect the material part of the ordinance and because the issue was one of continuing public interest and likely to arise again. The court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional because regulation of U.S. Const. amend. I, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2 rights to free speech must be narrowly drawn to promote compelling and substantial state interests. Limitations placed on solicitors was insufficiently related to the deterrence of crime and respondent's interests could be advanced by less restrictive means. The court also held that time, manner, and place regulations must be applicable to all speech without regard to content. This ordinance only affected persons soliciting funds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's declaration that an ordinance of respondent county, which prohibited door-to-door solicitation in the evenings, was constitutional. The court held that limitations placed on solicitors were insufficiently related to a compelling and substantial state interest such that appellant's First Amendment rights to free speech could be regulated.

 

1255 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Cullen


Civ. No. 22908


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 424; 193 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1975



July 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 15, 1983, and the petition of appellant husband for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. D120720, Carlos A. Cazares, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of an order of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which awarded appellant wife a 1/2 interest in part of the amount appellant would have received for longevity retirement from the United States Army if he had served 20 years on active service.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband and appellant wife were divorced after 29 years of marriage. Appellant husband retired from the United States Army on a disability pension three months and three days short of the longevity service period of 20 years. The trial court held that appellant husband's right to retire had vested by his service of more than 19 1/2 years pursuant to 10 U.S.C.S. § 1405. The trial court ordered that appellant wife receive 1/2 interest in 13/20ths of the amount appellant husband would have received for longevity retirement if he had served 20 years on active service. The court stated that, because appellant husband received nondisability retirement pay, then waived it and elected to receive the disability pension at a higher amount, the additional compensation attributable to appellant husband's disability was community property. The court found that pertinent federal statutes did not address whether disability retirement pay was to be treated by state courts as individual property or in the manner of state law. The court held that the application of federal law did not preclude the application of state law. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court because the additional compensation appellant husband received was attributable to appellant husband's disability and was community property he shared with appellant wife.

 

1256 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Stenquist


Civ. No. 28033


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 430; 193 Cal. Rptr. 587; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1976



July 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. D71705, Benjamin W. Hamrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted respondent wife's motion to compel after appellant had ceased making payments to respondent of a portion of his disability pay, as previously ordered by the California Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: The California Supreme Court approved an award to respondent wife of that portion of appellant husband's disability pay which represented one-half of her community interest in what would have been appellant's normal retirement pension. Appellant ceased making payments to respondent and respondent filed a motion to compel continuance of those payments. Appellant opposed the motion but it was granted by the superior court and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the judgment and found that there was no violation of the federal supremacy clause because there had been no treatment by Congress to indicate that a state could not award a portion of disability retirement pay of a member of the armed forces of the United States to a spouse as community property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that ordered appellant husband to pay a portion of his military disability benefits to respondent wife. The court found that congress had not yet considered or reached a decision concerning the power of a state to award to a spouse, as community property, a portion of the disability retirement pay of a member of the armed forces. Accordingly, the judgment of the trial court did violate the federal supremacy clause.

 

1257 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Trimont Land Co. v. Truckee Sanitary Dist.


Civ. No. 19641


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


145 Cal. App. 3d 330; 193 Cal. Rptr. 568; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1968



July 25, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 24, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 277271, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the permanent injunction heretofore entered is dissolved. The matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings described in part IV, above. Each side shall bear its costs of suit on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and intervenor appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California), granting declaratory and injunctive relief in favor of plaintiff which limited the number of sewer connections defendant could grant to properties within its territorial confines in order to reserve a portion of its total capacity for plaintiff's use pursuant to a contract entered into by defendant and plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a sanitary district, entered into a contract with plaintiff, owner of land outside defendant's district, in which defendant perpetually guaranteed to provide plaintiff with sewage service for facilities it intended to construct. Plaintiff brought an action against defendant seeking a limit on the number of sewage connections defendant could grant to properties within defendant's service area. Intervenor, a landowner within defendant's district, joined in the action, arguing such relief limited its ability to obtain sewage service. The trial court held in favor of plaintiff, and defendant and intervenor appealed. The court ruled Cal. Health & Safety Code § 6823, which allowed sanitary districts to contract with landowners, did not give defendant power to grant a contractual preference for disposal capacity in the event of a shortage in capacity to those outside the district, who were not ready to use the capacity, where the preference prohibited service to members of the district ready to use the capacity. The court reversed the judgment, concluding that § 6823 could not be construed to allow defendant to prefer outsiders over its members in the event of a shortage.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of plaintiff was reversed where a contract between a governmental body and a private party was construed by the same rules which applied to the construction of contracts between private persons, and defendant could not prefer landowners outside of its district over landowners within its district in the event of a shortage.

 

1258 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Habrun v. Dep't of Social Services


Civ. No. 26265


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 318; 193 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1966



July 22, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Certified for publication with the exception of sections II, IV, and V.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied August 9, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 470656, Judith McConnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff facility operators appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which refused to reinstate their license to operate a residential care facility for the elderly and claimed that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1550 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The California Department of Social Services suspended plaintiff facility operators' license to operate a residential health care facility due to numerous violations including abuse and negligence. Plaintiffs filed suit to have their license reinstated but the lower court refused and they appealed. The court affirmed and found that plaintiffs incorrectly claimed that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1550 deprived them of due process. The court found that due process permitted the state to temporarily deprive a citizen of enjoyment of a property right if the state's interest was both compelling and urgent and if a timely hearing was provided. The court held that § 1550 was constitutional because the state's interest in the safety of senior citizens in residential care facilities was both compelling and urgent. In addition, § 1550 provided a timely hearing following license suspension and reasonably balanced the rights of facilities operators against the rights of residents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and found that the statutory provision was constitutional because the safety of senior citizens in residential care facilities was a compelling interest. The court determined that the state believed that plaintiffs' residents were in imminent danger and thus due process was satisfied.

 

1259 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Frank v. Municipal Court


No. AO18345.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


145 Cal. App. 3d 200; 193 Cal. Rptr. 299; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1954



July 21, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 27, 1983 (S.F. 24651).
Sept. 20, 1984, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 3, for reconsideration in light of People v. McCovey (1984) 36 Cal.3d 517.
Sub. opn. was not certified for publication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Indian sought a writ of prohibition to respondent municipal court, contending that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 1.18 was unconstitutionally vague because it did not define "subsistence" or "ceremonial" in the context of illegal fish possession.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Indian was charged with illegally possessing more than two salmon outside his reservation's boundaries where they did not have the required markings or were not possessed for subsistence or ceremonial purposes. The lower court sustained petitioner's demurrer and dismissed the complaint, and the lower appellate court reversed. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition contending, among other things, that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 14, § 1.18 was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to define "subsistence" or "ceremonial." The court had granted an alternative writ and held that as this regulation expressly referred to federal regulations, which defined subsistence in the context of fishing, the meaning of that term was readily ascertainable. While the regulation did not define "ceremonial", the court noted, petitioner could not complain of a statute's vagueness when his conduct fell clearly within its bounds. The absence of the required markings on the fish alone, the court explained, was a regulatory violation. Further, petitioner did not claim that the fish were possessed for ceremonial purposes. The alternative writ was discharged and the peremptory writ denied.


OUTCOME: The court's alternative writ was discharged and the peremptory writ denied because petitioner Indian clearly violated state fishing regulations prohibiting possession of more than two salmons outside reservation boundaries where they were not properly marked or possessed for ceremonial or subsistence purposes.

 

1260 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Stephen P.


Crim. No. 42162


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


145 Cal. App. 3d 123; 193 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1906



July 20, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 17, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. J-29828, Joe D. Hadden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: There is no merit to appellant's contentions that (1) all of the evidence went to whether he was responsible for the fire and none to how he was doing on probation under the previous juvenile court orders; and (2) it was error to compute his maximum possible time in custody by including the penalty for arson becasue arson was not alleged by way of a section 602 petition. However, for the court's failure to make an express finding that the previous order has been rehabilitatively ineffective, we remand the cause for further proceedings. ( In re Michael B. (1980) 28 Cal.3d 548, 553, fn. 2 [169 Cal.Rptr. 723, 620 P.2d 173].)

A supplemental petition was filed June 11, 1981. Among other things, it alleged that the minor is a ward of the court and the previous orders of the court have not been effective in the rehabilitation of the minor by reason of three described violations of the arson statutes ( Pen. Code, §§ 451 [***2] , 452), previously sustained petitionson the minor are "8/18/80 240 PC" and "8/18/80 647(a) PC" and the maximum confinement time on the previously sustained petitions is eight months; and requested a change in the dispositional orders of the above dates and aggregation of confinement time at the disposition of the matter. Although labeled "supplemental petition," the petition was a proper one under sections 602 and 777, Welfare and Institutions Code in that all allegations necessary for each petition were included therein. ( Welf. & Inst. Code, §§ 656, 777, subds. (a) and (b).) It gave full notice to the minor and his counsel that the hearings in the juvenile court resulting from the filing of the petition would encompass the adjudication and disposition of the alleged violations of the Penal Code as well as the issue of the minor's continuation as a ward of the court and the possible increase of his maximum period of confinement. "[The] issues may be presented in a single unitary petition which contains the requisite allegations, and may be disposed of in the course [***3] of a single proceeding otherwise conforming to law. (See In re Reynaldo R., supra, 86 Cal.App.3d 250, 254-255 [150 Cal.Rptr. 71], involvinguse of a single petition filed under §§ 602 and 777; see also, In re John G., supra, 72 Cal.App.3d 242, 245 [139 Cal.Rptr. 849]; In re Aaron N., supra, 70 Cal.App.3d 931, 941 [139 Cal.Rptr. 258].) [para. ] The filing of a unitary petition satisfies the essential demands of due process. (See In re Arthur N., supra, 16 Cal.3d 226, 233 [127 Cal.Rptr. 641, 545 P.2d 1345].)" ( In re Michael B. (1980) 28 Cal.3d 548, 554 [169 Cal.Rptr. 723, 620 P.2d 173].) That all parties were aware that this was such a petition and fully apprised of the allegations of the petition and possible disposition is reflected in the nature of the proceedings, the evidence adduced on the adjudication hearing, the probation officer's report, the dispositional hearing, the arguments of counsel and the comments of both parties and the court (see In re Reynaldo R. (1978) 86 Cal.App.3d 250, 255 [150 Cal.Rptr. 71]). It is clear that this petition was treated by both [***4] parties and the court throughout the juvenile proceedings as a 602/777 petition. At the dispositional hearing the court asked "the maximum period of imprisonment in this matter," and the prosecutor answered "Three years, fourmonths." No comment was made by minor's counsel. The court then accepted the recommendation of the probation officer and "ordered that Stephen be continued a ward of the Ventura County Juvenile Court pursuant to section 602 of the Welfare and Institutions Code" and that he be returned to the home of his mother on probation. The court computed "the total maximum term to be 3 years four months." The only inquiry by minor's counsel concerned the requirement of probation that the minor's mother attend at least eight sessions of family counseling. No objection was voiced to, indeed there was no discussion of the maximum term, in fact, the court said to the minor "Stephen, as you heard when I asked Ms. Clontz for the maximum period of time you could receive in this, it is very substantial." Minor's counsel surely knew the maximum period of confinement included the penalty for arson. No surprise concerning the consequences flowing from [***5] the petition was expressed and no objection was made. All parties fully understood the computation of the maximum period of three years and four months and knew that it included the arson penalty -- the upper term of three years (§ 451, subd. (d), Pen. Code) -- and one-third of the middle term or two months each for simple assault ( §§ 240, 241, Pen. Code) and child molestation ( § 647a, Pen. Code). 4 In light of the foregoing, we cannot find the absence of express reference in the petition to section 602 in any manner prejudiced the minor.


4 On July 15, 1981, a petition under section 602 was filed in San Diego County charging the minor with a violation of section 594, subdivision (b)(2), Penal Code (malicious mischief), sustained on June 24, 1981, and ordered transferred to Ventura County. There appears to have been no disposition of the San Diego petition.

However, the flaw in the juvenile process was the failure of the court to find that the previous order has been rehabilitatively ineffective. "Before the previous dispositional order may be modified, the court must make an express finding the previous order in fact [***6] has been rehabilitatively ineffective. ( In re Reynaldo, supra, 86 Cal.App.3d at p. 255; In re Denise C. (1975) 45 Cal.App.3d 761, 766-767 [119 Cal.Rptr. 735].)" ( In re Michael B. (1980) 28 Cal.3d 548, 552-553 [169 Cal.Rptr. 723, 620 P.2d 173].) The court's failure to do so here mandates alimited reversal (28 Cal.3d, at p. 553, fn. 2).

The order sustaining the supplemental petition is reversed solely for the failure of the superior court to make an express finding that the previous dispositional order has been rehabilitatively ineffective, and the cause is remanded with directions to make a determination on said issue. In all other respects the order sustaining the supplemental petition is affirmed. The dispositional order is reversed and the cause remanded with directions to the superior court to reinstate said order in the event it makes a finding that the previous dispositional order has been rehabilitatively ineffective, or to make a new and different order as it deems proper in the event it makes no such finding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) for arson.


OVERVIEW: Appellant minor set fire to a classmate's school locker, and was accompanied by friends. The court determined that the evidence tended to connect appellant to the commission of arson, and that the use of the out of court statements made by appellant's friends were permissible. The court found that the state made a prima facie case. The court determined that the grant of immunity to appellant's friends was not improper. The court determined that appellant's protections against double jeopardy were not violated because the court corrected its error immediately and appellant was subjected to only one proceeding. The court reversed the order that sustained the supplemental petition solely for failure to make an express finding that the previous dispositional order had been rehabilitatively ineffective, and the cause was remanded with directions to make a determination on said issue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant minor's conviction having determined that the state made a prima facie case of arson against appellant. The order was reversed solely for the failure of the superior court to make a finding that the previous dispositional order was rehabilitatively ineffective.

 

1261 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Beasley


Crim. No. 6348


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


145 Cal. App. 3d 16; 193 Cal. Rptr. 86; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1899



July 15, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, Nos. 249896-2 and 279486-5, A. Dennis Caeton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments in actions No. 249896-2 and 279486-5 are affirmed and the matters are remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) convicting him of grand theft person under Cal. Penal Code § 487 and sentencing him on that charge as well as on a prior conviction of heroin possession under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, for which defendant was on probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of grand theft person under Cal. Penal Code § 487. He was sentenced on the charge plus an earlier conviction for heroin possession under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, for which he was on probation. Defendant claimed that the trial judge erred in not initiating narcotics addict commitment proceedings pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051. The court affirmed the judgments but remanded the case. The court held that a person was eligible for commitment where he was emotionally or psychologically dependent, or in imminent danger of becoming so, on cocaine. The court held that the trial judge erred in interpreting § 3051 as requiring that the trial judge determine that the defendant was addicted to narcotics before commitment proceedings could be initiated, when all that was required was a finding that the defendant might be addicted. The court directed the trial judge on remand to reexamine the issue of whether commitment proceedings should be initiated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgments relating to defendant's conviction for grand theft person and prior conviction for heroin possession, but remanded the case so that the trial court could reexamine whether narcotics addiction commitment proceedings should be initiated against defendant.

 

1262 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Dickson


Crim No. 42521


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


144 Cal. App. 3d 1046; 192 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1897



July 15, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 28, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 21, 1983. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A362871, Irwin Garfinkel, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's denial of appellant's motion to suppress under Penal Code section 1538.5 is reversed. The cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this reversal and the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered upon his plea of guilty to the charge of manufacturing methaqualone for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence seized during an unlawful search and seizure.


OVERVIEW: An officer noticed the odor of ether emanating from defendant's home and, believing that he had discovered a drug-manufacturing laboratory, called for backup. Officers entered the home and discovered materials used to manufacture narcotics. Defendant was arrested and subsequently convicted of manufacturing illegal drugs. He challenged his conviction, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his suppression motion on the basis of an unlawful search. In reversing and remanding for further proceedings, the appellate court concluded that the trial court erred in denying the motion where police failed to establish that their intrusion into defendant's dwelling was justified on the basis of exigent circumstances. While the odor of ether might have been sufficient to justify the issuance of a search warrant, it did not establish sufficient probable cause for a warrantless search. Further, the officers failed to demonstrate the existence of exigent circumstances that would justify their entry in the absence of probable cause. Police conduct was inconsistent with a believe that entry was necessary to prevent imminent harm to persons and property located in defendant's home.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for manufacturing methaqualone for sale was reversed and the matter was remanded for further proceedings. The trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence on the basis of an illegal search and seizure. The smell of ether did not constitute probable cause sufficient to justify the warrantless entry, and the police failed to establish exigent circumstances to substitute for the lack of probable cause.

Inmates of the Riverside County Jail v. Clark


Civ. No. 27464


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


144 Cal. App. 3d 850; 192 Cal. Rptr. 823; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1877



July 12, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. Indio 33526, Warren E. Slaughter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order is modified to provide for prompt reconsideration of the determination that the metal security doors remain open, and to reflect that if no unassigned sworn personnel are available to fill vacant staff positions, appellant is allowed six months from the date of this opinion to fill such positions. The partial stay of the superior court's order is dissolved, and the trial court's order is affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff challenged an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), that granted a writ of habeas corpus filed by respondent inmates. The order held that the housing for pretrial detainees constituted punishment without due process of law in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and the housing conditions for sentenced prisoners constituted cruel or unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sheriff challenged an order granting a writ of habeas corpus to respondent inmates. The order found that housing conditions of pretrial detainees was punishment without due process of law in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and housing conditions for sentenced prisoners constituted cruel or unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. In affirming, the court noted that the trial court applied the appropriate standards. Further, the overcrowding, understaffing, and outmoded, inadequate facility, upon which the trial court based its decision, were reasonable criteria and under the contemporary standards of decency set out in Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15, §§ 1000 et seq., were unconstitutional. The court noted that the jail administrators had had over 20 months of experience under the order. On remand, the trial court was to reconsider whether metal security doors were to remain open in light of the court's findings that this was an effective way to deter assaults by prisoners on other prisoners. Also, if no unassigned sworn personnel were available to fill vacant staff positions, appellant was allowed six months to fill the positions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that directed appellant sheriff to fill both sworn and unsworn personnel. The court granted appellant six months to fill the positions. The court modified the trial court's opinion and provided for prompt reconsideration of the determination that the metal security doors remain open.

 

1264 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Guthrie


Crim. No. 11943


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


144 Cal. App. 3d 832; 193 Cal. Rptr. 54; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1876



July 11, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - See post, page 848.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 61172, A. Richard Backus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) of first degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code §§ 459- 460, on the grounds that the phrase "inhabited dwelling house" in § 460 was unconstitutionally vague, and that the trial court failed to instruct the jury that defendant could not be convicted of first degree burglary if the jury had a reasonable doubt that he believed the house was inhabited.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree burglary under Cal. Penal Code §§ 459- 460. The court held that the phrase "inhabited dwelling house" was not unconstitutionally vague because it was possible, as evidence showed, for a dwelling to be "inhabited" although temporarily unoccupied. The court found no indication that the legislature intended to discard the distinction between "inhabited" and "occupied." The court held that the trial court properly rejected defendant's proffered instruction regarding reasonable juror doubt whether defendant knew the dwelling was inhabited. The court found that the instruction did not state that defendant's knowledge was reasonable and bona fide, the proposed instruction was an incomplete statement of the law, and the trial court had no duty to give a correct instruction on mistake-of-fact. The court held that actual knowledge that a dwelling house was inhabited was not an element of the offense under § 459. The court refused to imply a knowledge of inhabitation requirement because burglary of a dwelling could be committed without entering the dwelling in a way that would impart knowledge of whether it was inhabited.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree burglary because the phrase "inhabited dwelling house" was not unconstitutionally vague, actual knowledge that a dwelling was inhabited was not an element of the crime, and the trial court properly rejected a proffered jury instruction regarding reasonable doubt about inhabitance.

 

1265 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wilkinson v. Madera Cmty. Hosp.


Civ. No. 6109


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


144 Cal. App. 3d 436; 192 Cal. Rptr. 593; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1918



June 28, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Madera County, No. 26259, Donald R. Fretz, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor challenged the denial by the Superior Court of Madera County (California) of a writ of mandate to compel defendant hospital to reappoint plaintiff to its medical staff even though he failed to maintain malpractice insurance as required by defendant's rules.


OVERVIEW: Defendant hospital refused to reappoint plaintiff doctor to its medical staff because he failed to maintain malpractice insurance as required by defendant's rules. Plaintiff filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel reinstatement, which was denied by the lower court. Plaintiff appealed, raising various constitutional attacks on Cal. Ins. Code § 1319 and the action of defendant's board. Plaintiff claimed that § 1319 unconstitutionally delegated authority to defendant's board and to insurance companies. He claimed it permitted them to arbitrarily determine who could practice medicine. The court disagreed. It found that the statute simply allowed hospitals the discretion to require staff doctors to carry malpractice insurance. Inherent in the statute was the determination that noninsured doctors could be barred from practicing in a hospital. The court rejected defendant's argument that the statute lacked sufficient safeguards to prevent hospitals from devising arbitrary insurance requirements. The purpose of § 1319 was to protect the financial integrity of hospitals. Thus, defendant's actions were proper under the statute, and there was no constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the refusal of the lower court to order plaintiff doctor's reinstatement as defendant hospital did not violate plaintiff's constitutional rights in refusing to reappoint him to the medical staff when he failed to carry the required malpractice insurance.

 

1266 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gregori


Crim. No. 13348


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


144 Cal. App. 3d 353; 192 Cal. Rptr. 555; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1910



June 24, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Certified for publication with the exception of section III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A petition for a rehearing was denied July 11, 1983, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 8, 1983. Hearing Denied September 6, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR51763, Raul Rosado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction of four felony convictions is modified to reflect a single misdemeanor conviction. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) convicting him of four felony counts of failure to pay child support under Cal. Penal Code § 270. The father alleged, inter alia, that he was denied equal protection because the statute punished nonsupporting fathers who insisted upon an initial judicial resolution of their paternity more severely than nonsupporting fathers who conceded paternity.


OVERVIEW: The father also claimed a second equal protection violation arguing that the district attorney selectively enforces the felony provision of § 270 because fathers whose children and their mothers receive public assistance are more likely to have suffered earlier paternity adjudications. The father also alleged that § 270 was unconstitutionally vague because defendants do not know when they have completed the crime, or, that failure to provide support is a continuing offense that can result only in a single conviction. The court held that, although there was a clear and compelling state interest served by the statute, the distinction in punishment based only on the earlier legal determination of paternity did not further the purpose of the statute. Therefore, a felony conviction could not be imposed on a first conviction. Also, because the father acted in a constant fashion with a single intent in mind, his acts only constituted a single offense. Finally, the court found no selective enforcement because no evidence was produced that the district attorney was more likely to prosecute fathers of children whose mothers received public assistance than other fathers.


OUTCOME: The judgment of four felony convictions was modified to reflect a single misdemeanor conviction and was affirmed, as modified.

Burg v. Municipal Court


No. AO19853.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


144 Cal. App. 3d 169; 192 Cal. Rptr. 531; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1861



June 22, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 25, 1983 (See 35 C.3d 257)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 507133, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of driving under the influence of alcohol, Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b). Appellant contended that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and violated his due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b). Appellant sought review of his conviction, claiming that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and violated his due process rights under Cal. Const. art. I, § 13, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court found that the statute gave a sufficiently definite warning of the proscribed conduct. The court found that the terms of the statute were made reasonably certain by charts, the California drivers' handbook, and other publications. The court found that no other specificity was required, because the statute gave fair warning of the proscribed conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol, holding that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because it conveyed a sufficiently definite warning of the proscribed conduct.

 

1268 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dotson


Crim. No. 20308.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


144 Cal. App. 3d 177; 192 Cal. Rptr. 513; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1862



June 22, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Oct. 20, 1983, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 67744, C. Richard Bartalini, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, accompanied by special circumstances, and sentenced him to life in prison without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder as a result of a death which occurred during the commission of a burglary of a home, although the facts indicated he did not commit the actual murder. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction of murder, but remanded for resentencing. The court held that the fact that crimes charged at the preliminary examination were used as the basis of a post-preliminary amendment alleging special circumstances did not deprive appellant of fair notice of the charges against him. The court held that, implicit in the trial court's finding that the confession was voluntary, was the determination that the confession was not the product of the delay between arrest and arraignment. The court held that the record afforded no basis for determining whether the judge would have considered striking the special circumstances findings if he had been aware that he had discretionary power to do so, and remand for resentencing was thus necessary.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of first degree murder accompanied by special circumstances, and sentencing defendant to life imprisonment was modified by deleting the finding on the robbery special circumstance. The case was remanded to the trial court with directions to consider, whether, in the exercise of discretion, the other finding of special circumstances should be stricken.

People v. Roper


Crim. No. 43780


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


144 Cal. App. 3d 1033; 193 Cal. Rptr. 15; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1896



June 22, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 22, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A376206, James M. Ideman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea and convicted appellant for failure to file state income tax returns in violation of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19406.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction for failure to file state income tax returns in violation of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19406. The trial court denied appellant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea and denied appellant's motion to set aside the information under Cal. Penal Code § 995. Appellant sought review, but the court affirmed, finding no abuse of discretion regarding appellant's guilty plea, because appellant had no valid reason for the withdrawal request where he informed the trial court that he believed paying income taxes was voluntary. Appellant's motion under Cal. Penal Code § 995 regarding his lack of intent did not survive his guilty plea. Finally, appellant had not been denied effective assistance of counsel, because there were no meritorious defenses that could have been raised but that were overlooked by counsel.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant's conviction for failure to file state income tax returns was upheld, because there was no abuse of discretion in the denial of appellant's motion to withdraw his guilty plea, in that appellant had no valid reason for withdrawal. Appellant's challenge regarding his lack of intent did not survive his guilty plea.

Bobb v. Mun. Court


No. AO17704


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


143 Cal. App. 3d 860; 192 Cal. Rptr. 270; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1821; 39 A.L.R.4th 432



June 14, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of defendant and respondent for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 11, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 77832, Edmund J. Leach, Jr.



DISPOSITION: The judgment accordingly is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant female juror sought review of order of Superior Court of Monterey County (California) denying appellant's writ of certiorari and confirming contempt order entered by respondent judge and claimed that voir dire questions posed by respondent judge were a gender-based denial of equal protection under the California constitution.


OVERVIEW: Appellant female juror was asked by respondent judge about her marital status during voir dire and appellant refused to answer because male jurors were not asked the same questions. Respondent judge held appellant in contempt and appellant sought writ of certiorari, claiming that the questions denied her equal protection under the California constitution, since they were gender based and discriminatory, and the trial court denied appellant's request for certiorari, for which appellant sought review. The court reversed the trial court's order denying relief to appellant, and held that respondent judge's contempt order was invalid, because the voir dire questions posed to appellant by respondent judge were gender based and denied appellant equal protection under the California constitution when the same questions were not asked of male jurors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment denying appellant female juror's writ of certiorari in an attempt to vacate respondent judge's contempt order based on appellant's refusal to answer voir dire questions about appellant's marital status, because the questions were not posed to male jurors and were consequently a denial of equal protection based on gender.

 

1271 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Heffington v. County of Stanislaus


Civ. No. 6939


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


143 Cal. App. 3d 838; 192 Cal. Rptr. 202; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1818



June 10, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 169306, Norman S. Reid, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed from a judgment of dismissal by the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), in favor of defendant county after the latter's demurrer to plaintiff's civil rights action was sustained without leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizen brought a civil rights action against defendant county. He contended that defendant, by virtue of its policy and custom, encouraged its agents to harass and annoy certain citizens labeled as undesirable by it. He also contended that as a proximate result of this custom, defendant was encouraged to unlawfully deprive plaintiff of his constitutional rights. The trial court dismissed the action after it sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend. The court reversed the trial court. It held that plaintiff's complaint was sufficient against a general demurer. It reasoned that, as required, plaintiff, who sought to show that he was injured by governmental custom, showed that the governmental custom was made by its lawmakers or by those whose edicts or acts may be fairly said to represent official policy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of dismissal of plaintiff citizen's civil rights action against defendant county. It did so because plaintiff's complaint was sufficient to sustain a general demurrer.

 

1272 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Drum v. Fresno County Dept. of Public Works


Civ. No. 6056


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


144 Cal. App. 3d 777; 192 Cal. Rptr. 782; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1947



June 10, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 8, 1983, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 29, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, Nos. 254421-1, 254698-4, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed. The trial court is ordered to vacate its order dated June 10, 1980, whereby it issued a writ of mandate. The court is ordered to grant the preliminary injunction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted respondent homeowners' petition for writ of mandate, and denied appellant's request for preliminary injunction, in a suit filed over a dispute involving respondents' construction of a two-story garage under a zoning variance and building permit granted by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent homeowners received a variance from appellant county to build a garage. Respondents began to build a two-story structure, based on a building permit. After appellant received telephone calls from respondents' neighbors questioning whether the two-story structure was permitted by the variance, appellant issued a stop-work order, as the structure was not consistent with the variance. Respondents filed a petition for writ of mandate, and appellant filed a request for injunction. The trial court granted respondents' writ of mandate and denied appellant's request for injunction. On appeal, the court reversed and ordered the trial court to issue the injunction. The court held that permitting the construction of a structure which did not comply with the terms of the variance violated the mandatory notice requirements of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 14, 65901, and 65905, and of Fresno County, Cal. Zoning Ordinance § 877(B)(5)(c), as the neighboring property owners had received notice only of a variance for a garage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent homeowners, and ordered the trial court to issue an injunction in favor of appellant County of Fresno, because the notice to neighborhood property owners did not adequately apprise them of the improvement to be built.

 

1273 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Morris E. v. Superior Court


No. AO21176.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


143 Cal. App. 3d 687; 192 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1802



June 7, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 11, 1983 (S.F. 24612)
May 2, 1985, matter retrans to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 3, for reconsideration in light of Ramona R. v. Superior Court (1985) 37 Cal. 3d 802.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juveniles challenged a juvenile court ruling by respondent, the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which determined that petitioners were not fit and proper subjects to be dealt with under the Juvenile Court Law pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707, and petitioners sought a writ of mandate/prohibition.


OVERVIEW: Petitions were filed to have petitioner juveniles adjudged wards of the court because of committing a murder by use of a knife. Respondent trial court held a fitness hearing pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 and petitioners declined to testify as they feared that with the "Truth-in-Evidence" portion of Proposition 8, Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), their testimony could be used against them. Petitioners challenged the fitness petitions on the ground that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(b) and (c) were unconstitutional. Respondent denied the motions, conducted the hearing, and found petitioners unfit for treatment under Juvenile Court Law. Petitioners challenged and sought a writ of mandate/prohibition. On appeal, the court discharged the alternative writ of mandate and denied the peremptory writ because the statutes decreed that juveniles who committed certain enumerated crimes were presumed unfit for juvenile court treatment and were required to prove otherwise. Further, the court held that there was no unfairness in requiring petitioners to make the choice whether to waive their privilege against self-incrimination to both the fitness hearing and trial.


OUTCOME: The court discharged petitioner juveniles' alternative writ of mandate and denied their petition for a peremptory writ from the judgment that determined them as unfit and proper subjects to be dealt with under the Juvenile Court Law, because there was no unfairness in requiring the juveniles to make the choice whether to waive their privilege against self-incrimination to both the fitness hearing and trial, rather than only during the trial.

 

1274 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Deukmejian v. Superior Court


No. AO22431


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


143 Cal. App. 3d 632; 191 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1795



June 3, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the Marin County Superior Court to set aside its order refusing to quash the notice to appear and to enter a new order nullifying the effect of the notice that petitioner appear as a witness.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner governor sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent, Superior Court of Marin County (California), to set aside its order refusing to quash the notice to appear and requiring him to testify in a lawsuit concerning conditions at a state prison.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner governor challenged a ruling by respondent trial court requiring him to testify in a lawsuit concerning conditions at a state prison. Petitioner was a defendant in that action and had been given notice to appear to testify at a trial. His motion to quash the notice to appear was denied by respondent. The court concluded that respondent erred in failing to quash the notice to appear because real parties in interest made an insufficient showing of need for petitioner's testimony. The court held that petitioner could not be compelled to participate in seeking a remedy for prison overcrowding under the guise of being called as a witness. The court disapproved of such a blurring of the lines separating judicial and executive authority. The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate which directed respondent to quash notice to appear.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that respondent trial court erred in failing to quash a notice to appear because real parties in interest made an insufficient showing of need for petitioner governor's testimony. The court also found that requiring petitioner to testify would cause the judiciary to potentially interfere with petitioner's executive authority. The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate which directed respondent to quash notice to appear.

 

1275 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Alfaro


No. AO19583.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


143 Cal. App. 3d 528; 192 Cal. Rptr. 178; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1782



June 2, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Reprinted with additional opn. on den. rehg. 144 C.A.3d 683 


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Monterey County Judicial District of Monterey County, No. 187426 and 37 other cases, Maurice Jourdane, William F. Moreno, Raymond H. Simmons and Ross Bigler, * Judges.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who were charged with violating Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) and some who were also previously convicted of that violation, sought review of an order by the Appellate Department of the Monterey County Superior Court (California), which consolidated the actions and reversed judgments sustaining demurrers in all cases.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of violating Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b), which proscribed driving with a blood-alcohol level of 0.10 percent or greater, and some were also previously convicted of that violation. The municipal court sustained demurrers, and the appellate court consolidated the actions and reversed. On appeal pursuant to Cal. R. Civ. P. 62, the court affirmed the municipal court's judgments of dismissal on the ground that § 23152(b) was unconstitutionally vague because it was not based on conduct or symptoms of alcohol consumption which could be identified by those in potential violation of its provisions, but only on a blood test not available to the lay person. Moreover, calculation of one's blood-alcohol level depended on a number of factors including weight, the amount of alcohol ingested, the time since drinking began, the speed of absorption into the blood, and the rate at which alcohol was dissipated from the blood. Because there was no reasonably ascertainable method of knowing when one's conduct became criminal, § 23152(b) was fatally vague in its notice provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the municipal court's judgments of dismissal because the statute making it a criminal offense to drive with a blood-alcohol level of 0.10 percent or greater was unconstitutionally vague; the lay person had no access to chemical testing and the statute was not based on conduct or symptoms of alcohol consumption.

People v. Alfaro


No. AO19583.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


144 Cal. App. 3d 683; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1939



June 2, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Opn. on den. rehg. July 1, 1983; Hg. granted Aug. 25, 1983 (Crim. 23277)
Jan. 26, 1984, app. dism. without prejudice to a motion in municipal court for reconsideration of its orders in light of Burg v. Municipal Court (1983) 35 Cal.3d 257, 272-273.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Monterey County Judicial District of Monterey County, No. 187426 and 37 other cases, Maurice Jourdane, William F. Moreno, Raymond H. Simmons and Ross Bigler, * Judges.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the judgment of the Municipal Court for the Monterey County Judicial District of Monterey County (California), which dismissed defendants' charges of driving with a blood-alcohol of 0.10 percent or greater in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendants had been charged with violating Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b), which proscribed driving with a blood-alcohol of 0.10 percent or greater. Defendants demurred to complaints collectively, alleging that § 23152(b) was void for vagueness. The municipal court sustained the demurrers, and the people appealed. The cases were consolidated by order of the superior court, which reversed the judgments and remanded all cases to the municipal court. The consolidated cases were thereupon transferred to the court pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 62. The court affirmed the municipal court's judgments of dismissal, concluding that § 23152(b) was fatally vague in its notice provisions and hence unenforceable because it allowed persons to drink and drive but gave no reasonably ascertainable means of knowing when such conduct became criminal without reference to recondite scientific data.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing the charges against defendants, finding that the statute had unfair terms of notice, given that the individual could only speculate as to how and when his blood-alcohol ratio would reach the criminal point and was, thus, void for vagueness.

In re Nordin


No. AO20481


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


143 Cal. App. 3d 538; 192 Cal. Rptr. 38; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1783



June 2, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus with the California Supreme Court, which issued an order to show cause to the court regarding defendant's complaint alleging that the trial court erred in denying him bail.


OVERVIEW: The court discharged the show cause order from the California Supreme Court and denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus. The court found that there was clear and convincing evidence that defendant had threatened to kill a sheriff's deputy, and that there was a substantial likelihood that the threat would be carried out if defendant were released on bail. The court held that the record supported the denial of defendant's bail under Cal. Const. amend. I, § 12(c) and that there was clear and convincing evidence to support the bail denial. The court held that defendant's rights under U.S. Const. amend. VIII were not violated because the state had the right to determine the standards of eligibility for admission to bail, and that the state's regulation of the penal system was not the same as imprisonment. The court held that the state had an interest in protecting an individual from harm and that the denial of bail was not unfair or abusive.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the show cause order and denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus because there was clear and convincing evidence to support the denial of bail since defendant had shown a substantial likelihood of carrying out a threat against a sheriff's deputy.

People v. Barney


Crim. No. 11966


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


143 Cal. App. 3d 490; 192 Cal. Rptr. 172; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1779



May 31, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified June 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 75106, Lucian B. Vandergrift and Reginald M. Watt, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the convictions for incest and commission of a lewd act on the body of L.L. on or about February 8, 1981. The judgment (order of probation) is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which convicted defendant of one act of incest, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 285, and two lewd acts upon the body of a minor in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with incest and lewd acts upon the body of a minor in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 285 and 288(a),only the latter of which occurred in the county where defendant was tried. Defendant unsuccessfully objected to trial on the incest charge because the venire excluded residents of the district in which the crime was alleged to have occurred, in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VI. Defendant also objected to an instruction permitting identification of the time of commission of the offenses as "on or about" the date stated in the information. The court reversed the incest conviction because the exclusion of jurors from the district in which the crime was committed clearly violated the plain language of the amendment. The court also reversed as to one lewd act conviction because the trial court erred in giving an instruction permitting identification of the time of commission of the offense as "on or about" the date stated in the information, when the victim had testified at trial as to a specific time frame, and defendant had raised an alibi defense relying upon that testimony.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of incest because the exclusion from the jury of residents of the district in which the crime was alleged to have occurred violated the sixth amendment. The court reversed as to one lewd act conviction because the trial court erred in giving an instruction permitting identification of the time of commission of the offense as "on or about" the date stated in the information.

 

1279 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cal. Ass'n of Dispensing Opticians v. Pearle Vision Ctr.


Civ. No. 28420


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


143 Cal. App. 3d 419; 191 Cal. Rptr. 762; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1773



May 27, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 14, 1983. On June 21, 1983, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 491222, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant franchise vision centers appealed a Superior Court of San Diego County (California) order granting plaintiff association a preliminary injunction against defendants based on plaintiffs' complaint alleging defendants' violation of professional regulatory statutes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, an association of licensed opticians, sought injunctive relief against defendants, franchise vision centers, arguing, under various sections of California Business and Professions Code, that defendants were engaging in unfair business practices by illegally operating as registered opticians, advertising services of an optometrist, maintaining optometrists adjacent to their facilities, and exercising control over optometrists. A preliminary injunction was granted, and defendants appealed. The court affirmed, holding that defendants' franchise agreements, advertisements, and practices violated various provisions of Business and Professions Code. The court found that defendants were advertising "total eye care," implying that they also provided the examinations. Defendants failed to distinguish between optometric care and sales of eyewear. Furthermore, defendants' intention to exercise corporate control over optometrist professionals was against public policy. The evidence of control over optometrists was uncontradicted, and the franchise agreements required optometrists to operate under a name that could not be approved for an optometry license.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of a preliminary injunction against defendants because there was substantial evidence that defendants violated various state statutes by illegally operating as licensed optometrists, advertising optometry services, and intending to exert control over optometrist professionals.

 

1280 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Miller v. Murphy


Civ. No. 54345


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


143 Cal. App. 3d 337; 191 Cal. Rptr. 740; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1765



May 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1983. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 765727, John Anthony Kline, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pawnbrokers sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus and complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief against respondents, the police chief and others, after appellants challenged the constitutionality of pawnbroker regulations established by respondents.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, the police chief and others, established pawnbroker regulations, which imposed more requirements, such as fingerprinting customers, than Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 21628, which governed pawnbroker transaction reporting. The court affirmed the denial of appellant pawnbrokers' petitions for relief. The court found that under § 21628, the legislature did not preempt the subject and only set forth the minimum requirements for reporting, under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 21638, and that the local government could add requirements if they did not counter the legislative intent to prevent thefts and recover stolen property. The court found that the statute was presumably constitutional and also found that the fingerprinting requirements were not subject to strict review because they were a less serious intrusion upon personal security. The court used the test of reasonableness and found that the regulations only repeated what was in Cal. Fin. Code §§ 21200.5, 21200.7, and were not impermissible restrictions on appellants' right to do business. The court found that the statute was not subject to strict scrutiny because it did not distinguish between the rich and the poor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant pawnbrokers' petition for writ of mandamus and a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief against respondents, police chief and others, after it found that the local regulations for transaction reporting of pawnbrokers were not preempted by the state legislature, and that they did not infringe on fundamental interests and therefore did not require strict scrutiny.

Nash v. City of Santa Monica


Civ. Nos. 60079, 61923.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


143 Cal. App. 3d 251; 191 Cal. Rptr. 717; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1758



May 25, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Mod. of opn. on den. rehg. June 21, 1983; Hg. granted Aug. 11, 1983 (L.A. 31798)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C311502, Jerry Pacht, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and rent control board, challenged the issuance of a writ of mandate by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ordered appellants to give respondent apartment building owner a demolition permit.


OVERVIEW: Respondent apartment building owner unsuccessfully sought a demolition permit pursuant to the Santa Monica Rent Control Charter Amendment § 1803(t). Respondent filed for a writ of mandate to command appellant rent control board to issue respondent the permit. The trial court issued the writ of mandate, finding the refusal to issue the permit amounted to a deprivation of property without due process, and a taking for public use without just compensation. The court affirmed the issuance of the writ of mandate because the ordinance violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court found that the standard of review was strict scrutiny as the ordinance impinged upon a personal freedom, an individual's right to cease operating a business, by making it impossible for a property owner simultaneously to keep his land yet cease being a landlord. The court assumed appellant city's and appellant board's interest in preserving the stock of rental housing was compelling, but found there were less intrusive means of achieving appellants' goals. The court ruled that the ordinance infringed more than was necessary in the instant situation and was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the issuance of a writ of mandate to respondent apartment building owner because the Santa Monica Rent Control Charter Amendment provision requiring a landlord to obtain a permit before demolishing a building was unconstitutional as it infringed upon respondent's right to cease operation of a business.

City of Indio v. Arroyo


Civ. No. 29032


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


143 Cal. App. 3d 151; 191 Cal. Rptr. 565; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1746



May 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 9, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. I-33742, Warren E. Slaughter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment against defendants is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), that granted plaintiff a preliminary injunction preventing defendants from continuing to display a mural erected on the property of their gas station.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a suit against defendants for injunctive relief that would prevent them from maintaining a mural erected on the property of their gas station. It was alleged that the mural violated plaintiff's city codes and defendant's application for a variance was denied. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction concluding that a violation had occurred, that the provisions of the sign ordinance were not overbroad nor uncertain, not unconstitutional on their face, and did not interfere with defendant's U.S. Const. amend I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2 rights. The trial court further stated that such an injunction did not violate defendants' freedom of speech. Defendants appealed that decision and plaintiff argued defendants lacked standing to assert First Amendment interests. The court found that the mural, although on a commercial establishment, was an ideological expression, which was guaranteed a high degree of constitutional protection. Since the mural was not commercial speech the ordinance's purpose was not served; and therefore, the ordinance was overbroad as applied to such murals. The absence of a clear objective standard for getting a permit was violative as well.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment against defendants holding that plaintiff's ordinance was unconstitutional on its face and violative of the First Amendment because no clear objective standard for getting a permit was outlined and the planning director was empowered with sole discretion whether to grant a permit. The mural was protected as an ideological expression rather than commercial speech, which was sought to be regulated in the ordinance.
