Newco Leasing v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 66506


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


143 Cal. App. 3d 120; 191 Cal. Rptr. 588; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1742



May 19, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C222066 and C220442, Delbert E. Wong, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff leasing companies each instituted an action against defendant California State Board of Equalization, seeking a refund of use taxes paid under protest. The actions were consolidated. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered judgment for defendant board and plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff leasing companies each instituted an action seeking a refund of use taxes paid under protest from defendant California State Board of Equalization. Defendant board prevailed in the superior court and plaintiffs appealed. The court of appeal affirmed. The exemption from the payment or collection of sales and use tax enjoyed by individuals or business entities engaged in the leasing to the public of certain types of motor vehicles, did not also exempt from tax the transfer and assignment of the vehicles and the leases from one lessor to another. Defendant properly found that absent an agreed upon price between the parties to the transaction, the auditors were correct in deeming that the assumption of the remaining unpaid liability of the seller of each vehicle was the consideration in the transfer of the equipment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The exemption from the sales and use tax applied to the leasing to the public of certain types of motor vehicles did not apply to the transfer and assignment of the vehicles and the leases from one plaintiff lessor to another. Defendant board's auditors properly found that the assumption of the remaining unpaid liability of the seller of each vehicle was the consideration in the transfer of the equipment.

People v. Brown


Crim. No. 42815.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


142 Cal. App. 3d 997; 191 Cal. Rptr. 427; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1718



May 16, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 18, 1983, and Reporter or Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. AO20874, John A. Arguelles, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187 and attempted robbery pursuant to §§ 211 and 664. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in refusing to suppress X-rays taken of him while in custody and bullets removed from his body. Appellant also alleged that the trial court erred in failing to dismiss the special circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged a judgment from the trial court convicting him of murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187 and attempted robbery under Cal. Penal Code §§ 211, 664. Appellant claimed that the trial court erred in refusing to suppress X-rays taken of him while in custody and in refusing to suppress the bullets removed from his body. Appellant also claimed that the trial court should have dismissed the special circumstances. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court properly refused to suppress certain evidence. The X-rays were not an invasion of his privacy because the officers knew he received gunshot wounds in his side, had been X-rayed previously on his own initiative, and had scheduled an operation for removal of the bullets. In addition, the affidavits established that the magistrate considered and balanced the factors justifying the minimal intrusion into appellant's body to remove the bullets. Finally, appellant's assertion that Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i), relating to special circumstances, was constitutionally vague was unsupported because the statute sufficiently provided a standard of conduct as well as a standard for the ascertainment of guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to suppress X-rays of appellant and bullets removed from his body because, based on the evidence, they did not constitute an invasion of his privacy and were a minimal intrusion into his body. In addition, a statute providing for special circumstances was not unconstitutionally vague because it provided a standard of conduct and a standard for ascertainment of guilt.

Schrier v. San Mateo County Employees' Ret. Ass'n


Civ. No. 49986


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Five


142 Cal. App. 3d 957; 191 Cal. Rptr. 421; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1713



May 12, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 8, 1983, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 236067, Thomas M. Jenkins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff's deputy sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied his petition for writ of administrative mandamus against respondent county retirement board members, following respondents' determination that appellant was no longer disabled and that his disability pension was to be terminated pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 31730.


OVERVIEW: On review, the appellate court affirmed the denial of appellant sheriff's deputy's petition for writ of administrative mandamus. The appellate court held that Cal. Gov't Code §§ 31729 and 31730 did not compel respondent county retirement board members to continue appellant's disability retirement where appellant was substantially able to perform the duties of a given permanent assignment within the office or department in which he was employed. The appellate court further found that substantial evidence existed to support the trial court's finding that appellant was no longer disabled from performing his duties. The appellate court rejected appellant's assertion that a "full range of duties" construction was imposed on Cal. Gov't Code § 31729, instead finding that the statute's proper construction placed emphasis on whether appellant was substantially unable to perform his usual duties. The appellate court concluding by rejecting as meritless the assertions that Cal. Gov't Code § 31730 was unconstitutional on due process and equal protection grounds and that appellant's return to work under the statute would have deprived him of retirement benefits as a safety member.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the order that denied the request for writ of administrative mandamus, finding that appellant sheriff's deputy was not entitled to such relief because respondent county retirement board members had no obligation to continue appellant's disability retirement where appellant was substantially able to perform his usual duties.

Ramona R. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 67283.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


142 Cal. App. 3d 909; 191 Cal. Rptr. 402; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1709



May 11, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 11, 1983 (L.A. 31800)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, (California) that found her unfit for juvenile court proceedings. Petitioner was charged with murder.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner minor was accused of murdering her guardian. The petition to have her declared unfit for juvenile court proceedings was granted, and she was charged with murder. Petitioner filed a writ of mandate requesting that a new fitness hearing be conducted. In granting her writ, the court held that petitioner should not be deprived of the opportunity to present her evidence at the fitness hearing. Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d) took precedence over, and nullified the judicially declared rules of evidence established in In re Wayne H. Petitioner's silence at the fitness hearing deprived her of the only effective means of controverting the statutory presumption and the People's proof on the issue of fitness. She could have been able to present extenuating or mitigating circumstances that would have convinced the juvenile court she was amenable to juvenile court treatment. The court declared that statements made by a juvenile to a probation officer for use in and testimony given by a juvenile on the fitness hearing were inadmissible in any subsequent juvenile adjudication hearing or any subsequent adult proceeding to determine guilt except for the purpose of impeachment.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order that determined petitioner minor was unfit for juvenile court proceedings, holding petitioner should not have been deprived of the opportunity to present her evidence at the fitness hearing. The court ruled that petitioner could not be penalized for asserting her constitutional privilege against compelled self-incrimination, and have sanctions imposed to compel testimony which was not immunized.

Conservatorship of Valerie N.


Civ. No. 53699.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


143 Cal. App. 3d 667; 191 Cal. Rptr. 283; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1800



May 10, 1983  
May 10, 1983


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted June 7, 1983 (See 152 C.A.3d 224)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 100974, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners conservators sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County, (California) denying petitioners' application for authority to have respondent conservatee sterilized.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners conservators applied for authority to have their daughter, respondent conservatee, sterilized. The probate court denied the application and the court of appeals affirmed. Though not in effect at the time of the decision, petitioners argued that Cal. Prob. Code § 2356(d) was unconstitutional as a violation of the right to privacy. The statute prohibited the sterilization of any person who was under conservatorship in proceedings conducted pursuant to the provision of the Probate Code pertaining to guardianship and conservatorship. At issue was whether the right to sterilization which was held by a competent person could have been denied to one who was not competent. The state had a compelling interest in enacting the statute. Additionally, with regard to petitioners' due process argument, the Legislature's determination to end the state's participation in the sterilization of incompetent persons was reasonably related to a proper legislative goal.


OUTCOME: The order denying petitioners conservators' application for authority to have respondent conservatee sterilized was affirmed because the statute prohibiting the sterilization of any person who was under conservatorship was constitutional and did not violate a conservatee's right to privacy.

Kite v. Campbell


Civ. No. 64787


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


142 Cal. App. 3d 793; 191 Cal. Rptr. 363; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1697



May 9, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C339011, Eli Chernow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with instructions to permit plaintiff to file an amended complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a minor, appealed from an order of dismissal issued by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), after the court sustained defendant physician's general demurrer to plaintiff's second amended complaint without leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a minor, appealed the trial judgment that sustained defendant physician's general demurrer to her second amended complaint and issued an order that dismissed her case without leave to amend. On appeal, plaintiff contended Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.5 denied her equal protection and due process, and also was vague as it applied to minors. Holding that it was axiomatic that a demurrer should not be sustained without leave to amend if a reasonable possibility existed that either a defect in a complaint could be cured, or that the pleading could state a cause of action if it was liberally construed, the court reversed the judgment and issued instructions for the court below to permit plaintiff to file an amended complaint. The court found that § 340.5 was constitutional, that the statute created a three-year statute of limitations period during which minors may file medical malpractice suits, and that the statute incorporated common law tolling provisions for minors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment with instructions to permit plaintiff, a minor, to file an amended complaint. The court also found the medical malpractice statute of limitations was not vague, and did not deny plaintiff equal protection or due process, therefore it was found to be constitutional as it applied to minors.

Goodman v. Cory


No. AO15729


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


142 Cal. App. 3d 737; 191 Cal. Rptr. 272; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1681



May 6, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 765108, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a licensed private investigator specializing in locating unclaimed property, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) in the suit he brought against respondent state controller claiming that the California Unclaimed Property Law, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1582, which limited fees for his service, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensed private investigator specializing in locating unclaimed property sought review of the superior court's decision holding that the California Unclaimed Property Law, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1582, which limited fees for his service, was constitutional in the suit he brought against respondent state controller. Affirming the decision, the court noted that while the state could not suppress or prohibit legitimate business relationships, there was no fundamental vested right to conduct business entirely free of reasonable governmental rules and regulations. As appellant conceded, the statute was enacted to protect the public from overcharging for recovery of unclaimed property by unscrupulous probate searchers. The court concluded the statute furthered a legitimate goal by rational means. Rejecting appellant's disparate treatment claim because of the more lenient standards of Cal. Prob. Code § 1020.1, the court said that statute authorized judicial oversight to protect heirs from assigning property interests for grossly unreasonable consideration, and the lack of judicial oversight with appellant's service fully justified the more restrictive conditions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision upholding the state statute limiting fees for locating unclaimed property in the suit appellant licensed private investigator brought against respondent state controller. The court explained that the statute furthered a legitimate goal by rational means, and the more lenient statute regarding lost heirs did not show unconstitutional disparate treatment, but provided protection in different manner.

 

1290 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Potts v. Halsted Fin. Corp.


Civ. No. 66665


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


142 Cal. App. 3d 727; 191 Cal. Rptr. 160; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1679



May 5, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 23, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC49733, Bernard S. Selber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought reversal of a judgment of nonsuit by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which terminated his action for negligence against respondent property owner. The trial court held that respondent was immune from liability for appellant's injuries under Cal. Civ. Code § 846 as appellant was on the property without express invitation and did not pay consideration.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought an action against respondent property owner after he fell from the roof of a building under construction on a site owned by respondent. The trial court terminated his action for negligence on the ground that Cal. Civ. Code § 846 exempted property owners from negligence liability to persons on their property for recreational purposes without express invitation and without paying consideration. The court held that a property owner who began construction on private dwelling units had already withdrawn that portion of its land from public recreational access by making it unsuitable for such purposes. The court declined to adopt a case precedent which held that § 846 applied to property unsuited for recreation, and noted that the general development of state tort law discarded arbitrary application of blanket immunities. Accordingly the judgment was reversed because the court held that respondent's liability to appellant was not barred by § 846, and that the case should have been decided according to general principles of negligence law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because the court determined that respondent's liability to appellant was no longer statutorily barred after respondent began construction on the site. The court held that the case should be decided according to general principles of negligence law instead.

 

1291 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Panfili


No. AO18363


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


145 Cal. App. 3d 387; 194 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1971



May 4, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied June 3, 1983, and appellants' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied July 27, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Nos. 25864, 25879, and 25989, Ellen Sickles James, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied their motion to suppress evidence and granted the people's motion to overturn the magistrate's order suppressing evidence. Appellants were sentenced to prison for the offenses of robbery and use of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and co-appellant committed multiple convenience store robberies. Appellants were identified by witnesses and a convenience store employee gave appellant marked bills. Police officer entered the vehicle used in the robbery and found evidence in plain view that identified appellants. Arresting officer found one of the marked bills in appellant's personal effects and other marked bills were found in appellants' personal effects after booking procedures were completed. The trial court denied appellants' motion to suppress and sentenced appellants to prison terms for the offenses of robbery and use of a firearm. The court held that the warrantless search of appellants' vehicle was not illegal because the only evidence obtained through the challenged search related to identity of appellants, and independent sources of identification existed. The court also held that searches of appellants' personal effects after booking procedures were completed were not illegal as the arresting officer merely isolated the property, and examination and inventory during a booking procedure was statutorily required. Accordingly the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the court held that no reversible error was found where evidence obtained in the warrantless search of appellants' car related to their identity and witnesses could independently identify appellants. The court also held that examination of appellants' personal effects during booking procedure was statutorily permitted.

 

1292 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. 8,000 Punchboard Card Devices


No. AO16429


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


142 Cal. App. 3d 618; 191 Cal. Rptr. 154; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1669



May 3, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H-77937-3, Raymond L. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which found in favor of respondent boys' club in the people's declaratory judgment suit against respondent, seeking authorization to destroy 8,000 punchboard card devices seized from respondent, which respondent was selling as bingo games for charity.


OVERVIEW: The people sued respondent, seeking authorization to destroy punchboard card devices seized from respondent that were being sold as bingo games for charity. The trial court found in favor of respondent. The court affirmed. In 1976, the electorate approved an amendment to the California Constitution, which provided that the legislature by statute could authorize cities and counties to provide for bingo games for charitable purposes. Cal. Const. art. IV, § 19(c). At the time of the amendment, the definition of "bingo" contained in Cal. Penal Code § 326.5 did not include punchboards. Thereafter, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 326.5(n), punchboards were added to the statutory definition of "bingo." The amended statutory definition was constitutional because there was no clear intent by the electorate that the term "bingo" in the constitutional amendment be defined as it was in the original enabling legislation. The constitutional amendment did not define bingo, nothing in the ballot arguments favoring the constitutional amendment shed light on the intended meaning of the word, and there was no common meaning of the term. Thus, the amended statutory definition was required to be upheld.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed the judgment for respondent, holding amended statutory definition of "bingo" contained in California Penal Code was constitutional. When electorate approved constitutional amendment authorizing bingo games for charity, there was no clear intent to define "bingo" as it was defined by the Penal Code in effect at the time.

 

1293 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Foster v. Civil Service Com


Civ. No. 66493


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Seven


142 Cal. App. 3d 444; 190 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1650



April 27, 1983  
April 27, 1983


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C376412, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, who was terminated from his employment, challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied petitioner a writ of mandate to compel respondents, Civil Service Commission of Los Angeles County and others, to reinstate him as a county employee.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondents to reinstate him as a county employee and pay him accumulated back salary. The trial court denied the petition and petitioner has appealed the judgment. The record disclosed that petitioner was employed as a telephone operator. Petitioner was suspended without pay and discharged from county service due to his continuous lack of observance of working hours. Petitioner contended that the denial of a continuance was an abuse of discretion. The granting or denying of a continuance was a matter within the court's discretion, which could not be disturbed on appeal except upon a clear showing of an abuse of discretion. The granting of continuances was not favored and the party seeking a continuance must make a proper showing of good cause. The judgment was affirmed because petitioner admitted that the only purpose of the motion for a continuance was to allow time to properly analyze the full administrative record and no emergency or any of the grounds for good cause was shown as petitioner had almost a week to conduct such an analysis before the hearing.


OUTCOME: The denial of the writ of mandate to reinstate petitioner's employment with respondents was affirmed because the denial of petitioner's request for a continuance was not an abuse of discretion. Petitioner had a week to analyze the full administrative record and petitioner failed to show good cause for the issuance of a continuance.

 

1294 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Palos Verdes Shores Mobile Estates v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. 63482


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


142 Cal. App. 3d 362; 190 Cal. Rptr. 866; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1643



April 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 24, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 297292, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted a preliminary injunction to respondent that enjoined appellant from enforcing a rent stabilization ordinance as to respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, City of Los Angeles, development department, and rent commission, challenged an order that granted a preliminary injunction, which enjoined appellant city from enforcing its rent stabilization ordinance, City of Los Angeles, Cal., Ord. No. 152, 120, §§ 151.00-151.19. The lower court found that the guidelines adopted by the Rent Adjustment Commission, pursuant to the stabilization ordinance failed to satisfy due process standards because they did not articulate a reasonably ascertainable standard for determining a just and reasonable return, and the standards as the guidelines might have intended to articulate would not, in their application to all landlords, avoid confiscatory results. Rent control legislation was constitutionally valid so long as the legislation was reasonably calculated to eliminate excessive rents and at the same time provide landlords with a just and reasonable return on their property. The judgment was reversed and remanded because the maintenance of profit approach provided for in the administrative guidelines was a reasonable means for accomplishing the legitimate governmental purpose of ensuring a just and reasonable return.


OUTCOME: The preliminary injunction entered against appellant city was reversed because the rent control ordinance passed by appellant was constitutionally valid. The ordinance accomplished the purpose of ensuring a just an reasonable rate of return and the administrative guidelines utilized the maintenance of profit approach.

 

1295 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Amigos de Bolsa Chica, Inc. v. Signal Properties, Inc.


Civ. Nos. 23146, 23778.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


142 Cal. App. 3d 166; 190 Cal. Rptr. 798; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1626



April 21, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 304915, Oretta F. Sears, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which dismissed some counts in their first and second amended complaints against respondents, state and property owner, in appellants' action to invalidate a boundary and land exchange agreement made pursuant to Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 6307, 6336, 6357 to settle various disputed claims between respondent state and respondent property owner.


OVERVIEW: Appellant residents brought an action against respondents, state and property owner, alleging that a boundary and land exchange agreement was invalid because respondent state violated the California Constitution in removing lands from the public trust. Appellants sought review after their claims were dismissed. The appellate court, after concluding that it did have jurisdiction, affirmed the dismissal of appellants' claims, finding that they were barred by the applicable statute of limitations. Because the third complaint was dismissed, the court found that the decision of the second complaint was appealable but that the filing of the second complaint barred any appeal from the original complaint. The court reviewed the authority of respondent state and found that it was permitted under appropriate circumstances in furtherance of the trust to terminate the public trust with respect to a parcel of property and that its ability to do so was vital to its proper administration of the trust. The court concluded that respondent state's enactment of a limitations period was wholly consistent with the public trust doctrine and was not in violation of the California Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant residents' claims, finding that they were barred by the statute of limitations, and concluded that the land exchange between respondent state and respondent property owner was not a violation of the California Constitution because respondent state had the authority to terminate the public trust with respect to land if the termination was in furtherance of trust purposes.

People v. Crane

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO

April 20, 1983

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted May 13, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-39733, William S. Lee, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), convicting him of murder, robbery, burglary, and possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions for murder, robbery, burglary, and possession of a firearm after killing a jewelry storeowner. The court rejected defendant's argument that prospective jurors who were opposed to the death penalty should not have been excluded from the determination of defendant's sentence. Exclusion of those jurors did not constitute an exclusion of a cross-section of the community because they were excluded on the basis of their individual bias. The court stated that life imprisonment without the possibility of parole was not a cruel and unusual punishment and was not disproportionate to the crime of murder. Therefore, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for murder, robbery, burglary, and possession of a firearm. The court found that it was not improper to exclude jurors who were opposed to the death penalty from defendant's sentencing determination and stated that the imposition of a life sentence without parole was not cruel and unusual punishment.

People v. Superior Court (vernal D.)


Civ. No. 67975


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


142 Cal. App. 3d 29; 190 Cal. Rptr. 721; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1610



April 20, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 11, 1983, and the petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to conduct a hearing on petitioner's application to extend Youth Authority control over Vernal D.; unless waived, Vernal D. is entitled to a trial by jury on the issue of dangerousness; his dangerousness must be established by proof beyond a reasonable doubt; and he may not be involuntarily committed on anything less than a unanimous verdict of that jury.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner youth authority sought a writ of mandate seeking the annulment of an order from respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed petitioner's application under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1800 to extend real party in interest juvenile's time in the control of petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest juvenile was committed to petitioner youth authority and, prior to his release, petitioner sought to extend the time of confinement under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1800. Respondent superior court dismissed petitioner's application. The court issued a writ of mandate ordering respondent to conduct a hearing on petitioner's application. The court found that variation in the degree of sentence, based on dangerousness, was within the state's police powers. The court found that § 1800 did not restrict the court's power to act on the filing of an untimely application. The court found that the expenditure of four weeks for documentation was not unreasonable, and there was no prejudice to real party in interest. The court found that the real party in interest was entitled to a trial by jury, subject to involuntary confinement by a unanimous verdict.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate ordering respondent superior court to conduct a hearing on petitioner youth authority's application to extend real party in interest juvenile's time of confinement. The court held that real party in interest could only be involuntarily confined by a unanimous verdict.

Gregory v. City of San Juan Capistrano


Civ. No. 27114


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


142 Cal. App. 3d 72; 191 Cal. Rptr. 47; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1615



April 20, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Hearing Denied July 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 356300, Luis A. Cardenas and Thomas F. Crosby, Jr., Judges.



DISPOSITION: Insofar as it determines section 2-2.906 of the ordinance unconstitutional and enjoins its enforcement, the judgment is affirmed. In all other respects the judgment is reversed. Because plaintiffs have prevailed in part on appeal and by instituting and maintaining this action have caused numerous amendments to the ordinance to eliminate constitutionally questionable provisions and omissions (see fn. 1, ante), in the interests of justice, plaintiffs shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), granting summary judgment in favor of plaintiff mobilehome owners and intervenor mobilehome park; the trial court concluded that defendant's mobilehome rent control ordinance was preempted by state law and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mobilehome owners sought a declaration of the unconstitutionality of defendant city's mobilehome rent control ordinance and an injunction against its enforcement. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment. Intervenor park owner joined in plaintiffs' summary judgment motion. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs and intervenor, concluding that defendant's ordinance was preempted by state law and unconstitutional. During the trial, defendant amended its ordinance numerous times in an effort to meet constitutional objections raised by plaintiffs. On appeal, defendant argued the appellate court had to review the then current version of the ordinance. Intervenor objected because the latest version of the ordinance had not been administratively interpreted and the factual matrix had not been developed in a proceeding prior to appellate review. The appellate court held defendant's rent control ordinance was unconstitutional and enjoined its enforcement, thus affirming that part of judgment but in all other respects the judgment was reversed because the rest of ordinance was not in direct conflict with state law.


OUTCOME: The appellate court determined a section of defendant city's mobilehome rent control ordinance was unconstitutional and enjoined its enforcement, thus affirming that part of the judgment in favor of plaintiff mobilehome owner's and intervenor mobilehome park, but in all other respects the judgment was reversed because state regulation did not preempt the substance of defendant's rent control ordinance.

Lee v. Gates


Civ. Nos. 56433, 56620, 56750, 56754


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


141 Cal. App. 3d 989; 190 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1597



April 18, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 221405, C 247095, C 228704, C 224112, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment with directions to the superior court to dismiss.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and its police, challenged decisions from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted preliminary injunctions prohibiting enforcement of city ordinances against respondents, owners and employees of nude bars. The Supreme Court transferred the four consolidated cases to the court for review in light of another decision.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, city and its police, sought to prosecute owners and employees of nude bars for violations of local ordinances. Respondents, owners and employees of nude bars, sought injunctive relief to prohibit enforcement of these ordinances. The trial court found that respondents had a high probability of success at trial and granted the preliminary injunctions. The court affirmed the trial court's granting of the injunctions, and appellants' petition to the California Supreme Court for hearing was granted. The California Supreme Court transferred the cases to the court for reconsideration. Appellants imposed a moratorium on the enforcement of the local ordinances, but respondents argued that the enforcement of these sections was dependent on the discretion of appellants. Appellants argued that the appeals were moot because they recognized that the ordinances were unconstitutional and would not enforce them. The court found that there was no reasonable expectation that respondents would prosecute under a penal code section that was clearly unconstitutional and, therefore, the case was moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments entering preliminary injunctions prohibiting enforcement of city ordinances against respondents, owners and employees of nude bars, and directed the superior court to dismiss the action because the case was moot where appellants, city and its police, recognized that the ordinances were unconstitutional.

 

1300 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Arnelle v. City


Civ. No. 50622


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


141 Cal. App. 3d 693; 190 Cal. Rptr. 490; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1561



April 6, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 1, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 759303, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which sustained respondent city's demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed appellant's action for the recovery of appellant's attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney was appointed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 987(b) to represent an indigent defendant in a criminal case, but his compensation was not agreed upon in advance. After the trial, appellant submitted a bill for his fees, but the trial judge determined that a much smaller amount was reasonable, and respondent city paid him that amount. Appellant filed a complaint for the remainder of his fee, alleging quantum meruit, breach of oral contract and breach of contract, but the lower court sustained respondent's demurrer without leave to amend, and a judgment of dismissal was entered. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, holding that, in the absence of an express contract, an attorney appointed to represent an indigent party has no contractual right to recover a specific amount of fees and that the trial judge has the discretion to award such an attorney a reasonable fee based upon the factors set forth in § 987.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, holding that because respondent city did not have an express contract with appellant attorney, appellant had no contractual right to recover all of his claimed fees incurred in his representation of an indigent defendant.

 

1301 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Canavin v. Pacific Southwest Airlines


Civ. No. 24595.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


141 Cal. App. 3d 577; 196 Cal. Rptr. 82; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1552



March 31, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Apr. 25, 1983 (See 148 Cal. App. 3d 512)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Jud. Council Co. No. 623, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, wife and children, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), that awarded them a judgment in a wrongful death action against respondent airline after a plane crashed killed decedent, appellants' husband and father. Appellants challenged the lump-sum unapportioned award entered in their favor.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, wife and children, brought a wrongful death action against respondent airline after a crash killed decedent, who was their husband and father. Because respondent conceded liability, the sole issue before the jury was damages. Appellants sought review of a lump-sum unapportioned award. The appellate court reversed finding that it was reversible error to fail to instruct the jury on the discount rate applicable to a lump-sum award. The court reasoned that the jury could have relied upon decedent's after tax income and respondent's greater discount rate, which would have resulted in double taxation to appellants. The court found that it was error to deny prejudgment interest for past economic losses, but that the lower court was correct that prejudgment interest was not available for future earnings. The court found that grief and sorrow damages were not recoverable in a wrongful death action pursuant to California's common law. The court concluded that a wrongful death action was a single action regardless of the number of heirs and as such an aggregate verdict was permissible.


OUTCOME: The judgment for appellants, wife and children, was reversed because trial court committed prejudicial error by not instructing jury on discount rates applicable to a lump-sum award because the jury may have relied simultaneously on net income after taxes and greater discount rate proposed by respondent airline resulting in double taxation to appellants, and that it was error to deny prejudgment interest for past economic losses.

In re Branson


Nos. AO20323, AO20324.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


141 Cal. App. 3d 482; 190 Cal. Rptr. 412; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1543



March 29, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 30, 1983 (Crim. 23174), and cause ordered dism. as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prison inmates sought a writ of habeas corpus in the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), alleging overcrowding and inhumane jail conditions and naming the county board of supervisors and the sheriff as respondents.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prison inmates filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, alleging inhumane conditions. The superior court found in favor of petitioners and ordered respondent county to correct the conditions. Thereafter, respondents, county board of supervisors and sheriff, filed a petition for extraordinary relief from the order. A settlement on the writ was reached whereby the habeas corpus was converted to an action for violation of the petitioners' civil rights. The settlement provided that all existing orders would be vacated by the consent decree. The clerk issued a remittitur directing the superior court to enter final judgment and decree. However, the court recalled the remittitur because it had not approved the decree. Respondents filed a motion to reissue the remittitur. The court held that the remittitur was issued by reason of inadvertence or mistake and denied the motion. Further, the court held that since the action by the inmates was in effect a class action, the dismissal had to be approved by trial court in order to protect interests of all class members. As such, the court had no jurisdiction to approve the stipulated judgment or consent decree as to that issue.


OUTCOME: The habeas corpus petition was converted to an for violation of the petitioners' civil rights. Then, the court denied the motion to reissue the remittitur entering the stipulated final judgment and consent decree because the court had not approved the decree, and because the underlying cause of action was a class action that could only be dismissed by the trial court.

Marshall v. Brown


Civ. No. 63658


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


141 Cal. App. 3d 408; 190 Cal. Rptr. 392; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1536



March 28, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC 43318, George M. Dell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a new trial limited to damages is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant former employer appealed the decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granting a new trial on the issue of damages awarded to plaintiff former employee in her action against defendant for wrongful interference with prospective employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former employee filed a claim against defendant former employer for wrongful interference with prospective employment alleging slander and violation of Cal. Lab. Code § 1050 and § 1054. The jury awarded general damages and punitive damages, but plaintiff elected to receive the statutory treble compensatory damages. Defendant made a motion for a new trial and plaintiff made a motion for additur. The trial judge granted a new trial solely on the issue of damages. The court concluded that treble damages under § 1054 were not unconstitutional. The court further found that there was no requirement that the jury be informed its damage award would be trebled because its function was to determine the amount of damages and not the amount of the judgment itself. The court held that in the new damages trial, plaintiff could elect to receive either the statutory trebling of compensatory damages or punitive and compensatory damages, but not both, because the same purpose was served by the imposition of either punitive or treble damages.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting plaintiff former employee a new trial on damages in her action against defendant former employer. The statutory award of treble compensatory damages was not unconstitutional and there was no requirement that the jury be instructed that its award would be trebled. Plaintiff was to be permitted to elect between the receipt of both compensatory and punitive damages or treble damages alone.

 

1306 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dotson


Crim. No. 20308.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


141 Cal. App. 3d 336; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1529



March 25, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Rehg. granted Apr. 11, 1983 (See 144 Cal. App. 3d 177)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 67744, C. Richard Bartalini, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of first degree murder and the trial court's imposition of a life sentence without the possibility of parole. Defendant argued that the finding of special circumstances of the murder count should have been stricken because the special circumstances were not put before the magistrate at a preliminary examination. The court found that this did not deprive defendant of fair notice of the charges against him. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to exclude testimony concerning statements made to police. The court determined that the trial court did not err in finding that such statements were voluntarily made. The court agreed with defendant's contention that because he was acquitted of robbery, it was error to use the robbery charge as a special circumstance. The court determined that the record of pronouncement did not show that the judge would have considered striking the circumstances if he was aware that he could do so. Accordingly, the court modified the judgment by deleting robbery as a special circumstance and remanded the case for the trial court to determine if other special circumstances should have been stricken.


OUTCOME: The court modified defendant's life sentence for murder because the court found that because defendant was acquitted of robbery by the jury, robbery could not be used as a special circumstance to enhance his sentence.

 

1307 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Saint John's Hosp. & Health Ctr. v. City of Santa Monica


Civ. No. 65406


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


141 Cal. App. 3d 47; 190 Cal. Rptr. 74; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1694



March 18, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-348222, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent city to grant it a removal permit to demolish rental units on two properties in order to complete its plan for parking space. Appellant's permit application was based solely on the theory that respondent city had no jurisdiction to refuse to allow the demolition.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a parking facilities improvement plan, appellant hospital bought two properties containing rental units by negotiated purchase. Appellant applied for a removal (demolition) permit without following the procedure provided by respondent city's charter and regulations, on the theory that respondent had no jurisdiction to refuse the demolition. The application was denied, and appellant sought a writ of mandate. Assuming without deciding that appellant's right to demolish properties acquired by eminent domain was absolute, the court rejected appellant's theory that a negotiated purchase under threat of eminent domain carried with it the same consequences as an acquisition by eminent domain. Because the negotiated purchases involved no input on behalf of the public and no consideration of the purchase's public effect, the court held that appellant stood in the same position as any other property owner and was subject to all local use regulations. The court held that the regulations were not unconstitutional because respondent had removed the requirement that a property owner show more than that he could not secure a fair return on his investment by renting the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying a writ of mandate in appellant hospital's action to force respondent city to issue a removal permit. Appellant was subject to all local regulations as to use because it purchased the rental properties through negotiation. Respondent city's demolition regulations were constitutional because they had been amended to remove the challenged provision.

 

1308 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Goodman v. County of Riverside


Civ. No. 27400


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


140 Cal. App. 3d 900; 190 Cal. Rptr. 7; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1494



March 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 133871, Elwood M. Rich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which ruled against plaintiffs in the action filed against defendant county water board to recover the taxes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owners appealed the trial court's decision that ruled against them in an action filed against defendant county water board to recover taxes. The trial court ruled that the taxes in question fell within the exception noted, having been levied and collected to pay an indebtedness previously approved by the voters. On appeal, the court concluded that when the state's voters approved the Budget Act of 1960, they approved an indebtedness in the amount necessary for building, operating, maintaining, and replacing the project, and they intended that the costs were to be met by payments from local agencies with water contracts. Further, the court concluded that the voters necessarily approved the use of local property taxes whenever the boards of directors of the agencies determined such use to be necessary to fund their water contract obligations, and that the ad valorem taxes levied fall within the exception. Finally, the court found that the burden was on plaintiffs to show that defendant acted improperly, and they did not meet that burden. The judgment appealed from was affirmed by the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the voters necessarily approved the use of local property taxes whenever the boards of directors of the agencies determined such use to be necessary to fund their water contract obligations, and that the ad valorem taxes levied fall within the exception.

 

1309 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hunter-Reay v. Franchise Tax Bd.


Civ. No. 66039


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


140 Cal. App. 3d 875; 189 Cal. Rptr. 810; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1491



March 16, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 6, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 25, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-383276, Edward J. Wallin, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order of July 9, 1981, granting the preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant franchise tax board sought review of the decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued a preliminary injunction restraining appellant from selling or otherwise disposing of respondent delinquent taxpayer's automobile and aircraft pending trial on the petition for a tax refund.


OVERVIEW: Appellant franchise tax board issued a notice of personal income tax assessed and demand for payment, jeopardy assessment, against respondent delinquent taxpayer. Appellant seized three automobiles and one airplane belonging to respondent and after two of the automobiles were sold, respondent filed a complaint for refund of personal income taxes and a permanent injunction. Following two administrative hearing in which respondent's total tax amount due was reduced, the trial court issued a preliminary injunction restraining appellant from selling or disposing of the third automobile and aircraft pending trial on the petition for refund. The court reversed the trial court's preliminary injunction and held that the trial court's granting of the injunction violated Cal. Const. art. XIII § 32 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 19081, each of which prohibited injunctions against the collection of taxes. The court concluded that due process required that the taxpayer be afforded, if desired, an administrative hearing prior to sale of his property and found that the exclusive means of obtaining judicial review of the legality of a tax assessment was to file a civil petition for refund.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the preliminary injunction granted by the trial court that restrained appellant franchise tax board from selling or otherwise disposing of respondent delinquent taxpayer's automobile and aircraft pending trial on petition for a tax refund. The court concluded that the California constitution and revenue and taxation code prohibited injunctions against the collection of taxes and held appellant's procedure was constitutional.

 

1310 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Wallace v. Miller


Civ. No. 50130


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


140 Cal. App. 3d 636; 189 Cal. Rptr. 637; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1466



March 8, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Del Norte County, No. 75134, Charles M. Thomas, Jr., * Judge.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Del Norte County (California), which granted defendant rock quarry owner's motion to determine and enforce liability for a wrongfully issued temporary restraining order and awarded defendant damages and attorneys' fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 535 (repealed).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowners brought an action against defendant rock quarry owner to enjoin dynamite blasting. After issuing temporary restraining orders, the trial court determined that plaintiffs were not entitled to enjoin the blasting, and defendant filed a motion to determine and enforce liability for a wrongfully issued temporary restraining order pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 535 (repealed). The motion was granted, and defendant was awarded damages and attorneys' fees. Plaintiffs sought review, and the court reversed. The court found that in order to enforce liability pursuant to § 535, a bond, undertaking, or deposit had been required when the temporary restraining orders were issued. The court held that granting the § 535 motion had constituted reversible error because plaintiffs had not posted a bond or undertaking when the temporary restraining orders were issued, and therefore, defendant's had been required to pursue common law remedies. Finally, the court found § 535 was not discriminatory because any differentiation between classes of litigants established by § 535 was rationally related to the legitimate legislative purpose of providing for judicial efficiency.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order granting defendant rock quarry owner's motion to determine and enforce liability for a wrongfully issued temporary restraining order and awarding defendant damages and attorneys' fees because defendants had been unable to validly move to enforce liability as no bond or undertaking had been posted when the temporary restraining orders were issued.

 

1311 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Fleming


No. AO16639


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


140 Cal. App. 3d 540; 189 Cal. Rptr. 619; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1454



March 4, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 1, 1983. Mosk, J., and Kaus, J. were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 80192, John R. Kennedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed with respect to the determination of guilt. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to rearraign appellant and determine in the exercise of discretion, whether to impose consecutive terms for three counts of oral copulation in concert and one count of forcible oral copulation. The court will state reasons for those sentence choices.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of, and sentenced him for, a number of offenses, including several sexual offenses for which defendant was sentenced to consecutive terms. Defendant alleged that the trial court erred in denying his request for a psychiatric examination of the alleged victim of the sexual offenses and in sentencing him to consecutive terms.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction of and sentencing for, numerous crimes including sexual offenses, arguing that the trial court erred in not ordering the psychiatric examination of the sex-offense victim and erred in imposing consecutive sentences for each of the sex offenses pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(d). The court affirmed the determination of guilt, but reversed and remanded to the trial court the sentencing issue, with directions that the trial court determine, in the exercise of its discretion, whether to impose consecutive terms and to state the reasons for its sentence choice. The court found that the trial court had improperly imposed a consecutive sentence for each sex offense, because some of the acts were committed only minutes apart and therefore did not constitute "separate occasions" as stated in § 667.6(d) as the basis for mandatory consecutive sentences. The court held that some time must elapse between events to constitute separate occasions. The court found that the trial court properly denied the request for the psychiatric examination because such examination was legislatively prohibited and such prohibition did not deprive of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of guilt of defendant charged with numerous offenses, but reversed the consecutive sentences for the sexual offenses because consecutive sentences were not mandatory for different acts committed only minutes apart. The court instruct the trial court to consider on remand whether to impose consecutive sentences in the exercise of its discretion and to state the reasons for its sentence choices.

 

1312 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Federer v. County of Sacramento


Civ. No. 21377


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


141 Cal. App. 3d 184; 190 Cal. Rptr. 187; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1523



March 3, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 25, 1983. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 28, 1983. Richardson, J., did not participate therein. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J. were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 289757, A. Richard Backus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order sustaining the demurrer without leave to amend is modified by adding thereto an order dismissing the action. The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sustained respondent county's demurrer in a claim of negligence.


OVERVIEW: Inmates of a jail run by respondent county escaped allegedly due to the negligence of respondent's employees. Allegedly, a jail door was left open, allowing two violence-prone inmates to escape. Those inmates killed the children of appellant claimant. Appellant filed a claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging negligence and vicarious liability. The trial court held in favor of respondent and sustained, without leave to amend, the demurrer of respondent. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the trial court. It held that appellant's complaint was insufficient as a matter of law. The court held that the admission that the negligent act was contrary to state policy was a concession and that respondeat superior had no application in such a suit as the governmental entity did not implement an unconstitutional policy which resulted in a constitutional deprivation. The court held that respondent was not liable solely because it employed a tortfeasor. Rather, a local governing body was liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 only when, through its agents or employees, it implemented or executed official policy resulting in a constitutional deprivation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, which sustained respondent county's demurrer in a claim of negligence and vicarious liability, on grounds that respondent did not implement an unconstitutional policy which resulted in constitutional deprivations. Further, appellant would have to show that respondent, through its employees, implemented a plan resulting in a constitutional deprivation.

 

1313 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McLaughlin v. Superior Court


No. AO18674


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


140 Cal. App. 3d 473; 189 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1448



March 1, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On March 31, 1983, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: A peremptory writ of mandate consistent with this opinion will issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which directed petitioner and real party in interest mother to submit their temporary custody dispute to mediation, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4607(a), and denied his motion for a protective order to prevent the mediator from making a recommendation to the court unless he was permitted to cross-examine the mediator.


OVERVIEW: As part of dissolution proceedings, petitioner father and real party in interest mother has a custody dispute, so respondent superior court directed the parties to submit their temporary custody dispute to mediation, pursuant to the Cal. Civil Code § 4607, and denied petitioner's motion for a protective order. When petitioner sought a writ of prohibition to retrain respondent, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, permitting the mediation proceedings, but directing respondent not to receive a recommendation from the mediator on any contested issued unless it first issued a protective order to guarantee the parties the right to have the mediator testify and to cross-examine on the recommendations, unless the parties waived this right. The court held that the policy of not allowing cross-examination was constitutionally invalid, because it permitted respondent to receive a significant recommendation on contested issues but denied the parties the right to cross-examine its source. Further, the court concluded that § 4607(a), which provided that contested issues of child custody and visitation be referred to prehearing mediation proceedings, was mandatory.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, permitting the custody mediation proceedings, but directing respondent superior court not to receive a recommendation from the mediator on any issue contested by petitioner father or real party in interest mother, unless a protective order had been made to guarantee the parties the right to have the mediator testify and to cross-examine on the recommendation, or the rights had been waived.

 

1314 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Olson v. Cory


Civ. No. 64385.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


140 Cal. App. 3d 379; 189 Cal. Rptr. 570; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1442



February 28, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 23, 1983 (See 35 C.3d 390)
Superseded by Olson v. Cory, 35 Cal. 3d 390, 197 Cal. Rptr. 843, 673 P.2d 720, 1983 Cal. LEXIS 274 (1983)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judicial pensioners sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motion for an order specifying their right to receive interest as an issue without substantial controversy. Appellants contended that they met all the conditions for awarding interest pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 3287(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant judicial pensioners prevailed in class action lawsuit against respondent government controllers which sought a declaration that the an amendment to Cal. Gov't Code § 68203 was unconstitutional because it reduced the salary of judges by limiting the cost of living increases provided by the statute prior to amendment. Appellants' subsequent motion for an order specifying their right to receive prejudgment interest as an issue without substantial controversy was denied and appellants sought review on the grounds that they had met all the conditions for awarding interest pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 3287(a). The court reversed on the issue of prejudgment interest, holding that appellants met the three requirements necessary for recovery under § 3287(a): (1) There must be an underlying monetary obligation; (2) the recovery must be certain or capable of being made certain by calculation; and (3) the right to recovery must vest on a particular day. However, as to appellants' claim that the interest be paid from the Judge's Retirement Fund, the court affirmed, holding that appellants were not permitted an award of interest when the claim was against a special fund.


OUTCOME: The court reversed on the grounds that appellant judicial pensioners were entitled to prejudgment interest on their successful claim against respondent government controllers; however, the court affirmed the trial court on the issue of the interest being paid from the Judge's Retirement Fund, since prejudgment interest could not be paid from a special fund under California law.

 

1315 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Spencer)


Civ. No. 66604.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


140 Cal. App. 3d 304; 189 Cal. Rptr. 669; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1434



February 25, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 4, 1983 (L.A. 31762).
Case discharged January 19, 1984.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state brought a mandamus proceeding seeking a peremptory writ to vacate a conditional plea of guilty made by real party in interest and accepted by respondent trial court.


OVERVIEW: In a mandamus proceeding, petitioner state sought a peremptory writ to vacate a conditional plea of guilty made by real party in interest and accepted by respondeat trial court after a preliminary hearing at which real party in interest was held to answer on a charge of willfully and unlawfully, and maliciously discharging a firearm at an inhabited and occupied dwelling house in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 246. The court granted the peremptory writ commanding respondent to vacate the plea based upon its conclusions that the Cal. Penal Code § 1192.7 limitation upon plea bargaining was invoked. The court held that the trial court's improperly accepted real party's conditional plea when it negotiated a plea bargain with real party, a defendant in a case involving a serious felony. Further, plea bargaining was validly prohibited by § 1192, which did not impermissibly limit any inherent sentencing power of the trial court and did not violate ex post facto principles by its application to prosecutions of preenactment crimes. Finally, the decision of the trial court was reviewable on the petition for extraordinary relief based on the importance of the question raised.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ directing respondent trial court to vacate its order accepting real party's conditional plea of guilty and to conduct further proceedings, concluding that respondent exceeded its jurisdiction by accepting a plea bargain from real party, a defendant in a case involving a serious felony.

People v. Kelly


Crim. No. 11725.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


140 Cal. App. 3d 322; 189 Cal. Rptr. 338; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1435



February 25, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. May 19, 1983, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 60640, Robert W. Cole, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by a jury in the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), on six counts of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, six counts of false imprisonment, Cal. Penal Code § 236, three counts of burglary, Cal. Penal Code §459, one count of assault with a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5(a), and infliction of great bodily injury in the commission of one robbery count and the assault, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction by a jury on six counts of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211; six counts of false imprisonment, Cal. Penal Code § 236; three counts of burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459; one count of assault with a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5(a); and infliction of great bodily injury in the commission of one robbery count and the assault, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. Defendant contended his unlawful arrest rendered his subsequent statements to the police inadmissible and asserted an accomplice's trial testimony was insufficiently corroborated. The court affirmed the judgment and held that defendant was lawfully arrested pursuant Cal. Penal Code § 148 for his efforts to delay or obstruct the robbery investigation. The court also found that even if defendant's statements were the product of an illegal arrest, the statements were sufficiently an act of free will to purge the primary taint of an illegal arrest and further characterized the statements as admissions rather than confessions, which rendered the doctrine of reversible error per se inapplicable. The court also held that the accomplice's testimony was sufficiently corroborated by other evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the jury and held that defendant's arrest was lawful because he willfully delayed or obstructed an officer in the discharge of his official duties and found that even if defendant's statements were the product of an illegal arrest, they were sufficiently an act of free will to purge an illegal arrest's primary taint. The court also found an accomplice's statements were sufficiently corroborated.
