Klarfeld v. Cal.


Civ. No. 64309


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


142 Cal. App. 3d 541; 191 Cal. Rptr. 330; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1661



February 18, 1983 


NOTICE:  [***1] Certified for partial publication - Certified for publication, with the exception of section 1 of Discussion (p. 548).


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 5, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 323416, Paul Egly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs car owner and auto glass coating businesses appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for defendant state in their action to declare Cal. Veh. Code §§ 26708, 26708.5 unconstitutional and to enjoin defendant from enforcing the statutes by issuing traffic citations or preventing use of motor vehicles with transparent window coatings.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff car owner was cited for violating Cal. Veh. Code § 26708(a) for having tinted car windows. Plaintiff glass company manufactured and installed auto glass tint. Plaintiffs sought a declaration that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 26708 and 26708.5 were unconstitutional, overly broad and vague, and to restrain defendant state from enforcing them. The trial court entered judgment for defendant. On appeal, the court affirmed because the statutes and regulations were not unconstitutional, and plaintiffs failed to show any facts entitling them to affirmative relief. The statutes and regulations made clear that transparent coating could not be applied to a windshield or the front windows to the sides of the driver. However, it could be applied to the side windows to the rear of the driver and to the rear window if the vehicle had mirrors as required by Cal. Veh. Code § 26708(b)(8), and to the windshield if it met Cal. Veh. Code § 26708(c) requirements.


OUTCOME: The judgment for defendant in plaintiffs' action for a declaration of rights and an injunction was affirmed because the statutes and regulations for tinted vehicle windows were not unconstitutional, and plaintiffs failed to show any facts entitling them to affirmative relief.

 

1318 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Regents of Univ. of Cal. v. Public Employment Relations Bd.


Civ. No. 54414


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


139 Cal. App. 3d 1037; 189 Cal. Rptr. 298; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1406



February 17, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On March 8, 1983, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The order below is vacated and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner university sought a writ of review from the order of respondent public employment relations board that compelled petitioner to grant an employee organization access to its internal mail service and contended that the order violated the rights guaranteed under the Higher Education Employer-Employee Relations Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3568 and § 3571.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest union filed an unfair labor practice charge against petitioner university arising out of petitioner's refusal to permit unions to distribute literature through the intercampus mail system. It contended that the prohibition violated its rights set forth under the Higher Education Employer-Employee Relations Act (HEERA), Cal. Gov't Code § 3568 and § 3571. Respondent public employment relations board found against petitioner and ordered it to allow free access. Petitioner filed for a writ of review contending that the regulations were promulgated in an effort to comply with federal postal laws and regulations prohibiting private conveyance of mail over any established U.S.P.S. route. This court vacated the order and remanded the cause for findings on the threshold issue of whether the regulations were reasonable in light of the surrounding circumstances, including federal postal requirements. It was premature to decide the conflict between HEERA and federal postal law, if such a conflict existed; moreover, that decision rested solely within the power of the judiciary under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3.5(c).


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of respondent public employment relations board and remanded the cause for findings on the threshold issue of whether petitioner university's regulations were reasonable in light of the surrounding circumstances as it was premature to decide the conflict before it was determined whether a conflict existed.

 

1319 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Valtz v. Penta Inv. Corp.


Civ. No. 26918


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


139 Cal. App. 3d 803; 188 Cal. Rptr. 922; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1378



February 10, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of defendants and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 6, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 483648, J. Perry Langford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the writ is affirmed and the case is remanded for the superior court to set the Valtzs' attorney fees for trial and appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant corporation appealed the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) granting plaintiff shareholders a writ of mandate compelling defendant to allow plaintiffs to inspect and copy defendant's shareholder list.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff shareholders sought a writ of mandate to compel defendant corporation to allow them to inspect defendant's shareholders' list, claiming the right under Cal. Corp. Code § 1600. Plaintiffs writ was granted and defendant appealed. Defendant contended that extending § 1600 to a Delaware corporation with its principal executive office in California violated the full faith and credit, due process, commerce and equal protection clauses of the federal Constitution. The court affirmed, holding that full faith and credit need not be given the laws of another state where to do so would have violated public policy of the forum. The public policy of California was to place no proper-purpose restriction on a shareholder's right of inspection, and the California courts had to enforce the policy despite defendant's Delaware incorporation. The court held further that defendant failed to show any connection between inspection of shareholder lists and the movement of goods in interstate commerce. Defendant's equal protection argument failed because there was no suspect class or fundamental right involved and § 1600 applied equally to domestic and foreign corporations.


OUTCOME: The order granting plaintiff shareholders a writ of mandate compelling defendant corporation to allow plaintiffs to inspect and copy defendant's shareholder list was affirmed because the public policy of California was to place no proper-purpose restriction on a shareholder's right of inspection, and the California courts had to enforce that policy despite defendant's Delaware incorporation.

Valdes v. Cory


Civ. No. 21654


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


139 Cal. App. 3d 773; 189 Cal. Rptr. 212; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1376; 4 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1124



February 9, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 7, 1983, and the petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 13, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing all respondents in these proceedings to perform their legal and ministerial duties without regard to the invalid provisions of sections 58, 59, 60, and 61 and section 14, subdivision (b), of chapter 115 of the Statutes of 1982.

The intervener-real party in interest shall bear the costs of these proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners brought a petition for a writ of mandamus challenging the validity of certain provisions of 1982 Cal. Stat. 115 and to compel respondents to perform their legal duties without regard to the challenged provisions.


OVERVIEW: The state legislature enacted 1982 Cal. Stat. 115, which, in part, prohibited payment of state-employer contributions from the state general fund to the public employees' retirement fund. Petitioners were either active or retired state employees who were members of the public employees' retirement system (PERS), and they brought a petition for a writ of mandamus challenging ch. 115 as violative of the constitutional prohibition against impairment of contract. The court ruled that a public employee's pension constituted an element of compensation and that a vested contractual right to pension benefits accrued upon acceptance of employment. Further, the court held that a pension right could not be destroyed, once vested, without impairing a contractual obligation of the employing public entity. Also, the court found that the state and other public employers were contractually bound in a constitutional sense to pay the withheld appropriations to the PERS fund. The court granted the petition, concluding that once paid, appropriated employer contributions constituted a trust fund held solely for the benefit of PERS members and beneficiaries.


OUTCOME: Petition for a writ of mandamus was granted where modifications to an employee's vested contractual pension rights must reasonably bear some material relation to the theory of a pension system and its successful operation, and the state failed to meet its burden of demonstrating that the impairment of petitioners' rights was warranted by an emergency serving to protect a basic interest of society.

 

1321 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carlson v. Cory


No. AO18984


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


139 Cal. App. 3d 724; 189 Cal. Rptr. 185; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1371



February 7, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 13, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein. Mosk, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. The peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers and electors petitioned the court for a writ of mandate against respondent state controller challenging the constitutional validity of two initiative statutes enacted at the statewide election which in effect repealed inheritance and gift tax laws.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayers and electors sought a writ of mandate challenging the constitutional validity of two initiative statutes which repealed inheritance and gift tax laws. The court denied the writ, holding that the subject propositions were not unconstitutional limitations on the legislature's power to tax because Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 33, while empowering the legislature to pass laws regarding the taxation of property, did not in any way limit the plenary power of referendum and initiative reserved to the people in Cal. Const. art. II, §§ 8 and 9. The propositions did not impair the fiscal management function of the legislature because the rule that the initiative powers of local electors were not available where the inevitable effect would be to impair or destroy the efficacy of some other governmental power was inapplicable to statewide measures and the propositions neither destroyed nor severely limited the power of the state legislature to tax or to balance the budget. The statutory initiative was not used to circumvent the constitutional prohibition against referenda on tax levies and the prohibition against referenda on tax levies did not apply to statutory initiatives.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ sought by petitioner taxpayers and electors because nothing in the constitution prohibited the use of the statutory initiative to repeal tax laws.

 

1322 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Estate of Gibson


No. AO19492


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


139 Cal. App. 3d 733; 189 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1372



February 7, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 13, 1983. Mosk, J., and Grodin, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 58558, Norman Spellberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors challenged an order from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which fixed inheritance tax assessed by respondent state controller under prior law. Appellant asserted that recently passed Propositions 5, and 6, both of which amended Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13301 et seq., and repealed the gift and inheritance tax, were to be applied retroactively.


OVERVIEW: The trial court fixed an inheritance tax assessment made by respondent state controller in decedent's estate over appellant objector's protest. The court affirmed and noted that both Propositions 5, and 6, which were simultaneously passed by the voters, amended Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 13301 et seq., and repealed the gift and inheritance tax. Proposition 5 made the repeal retroactive. Proposition 6, which received more votes, was effective upon passage. If the retroactivity provision of Proposition 5 had been applied, the tax would have been far less than if the prior law had been applied. The trial court fixed the tax under the prior law. The court applied the rule of Cal. Const. art. II, § 10(b), which provided that the measure which received the highest affirmative vote prevailed when there was an irreconcilable conflict between the provisions of two measures approved at the same election. The court determined that the Propositions were in irreconcilable conflict. The court held that the retroactivity provision of Proposition 5 could not stand and therefore upheld the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and held that the inheritance tax assessed by respondent state controller was correctly made under the prior law because Proposition 6, which received more votes than Proposition 5, provided for prospective application. The court rejected appellant objectors' assertion that the two Propositions could be fully harmonized, and that the retroactivity provision of Proposition 5 could be given effect.

 

1323 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Padilla


Crim. No. 11947.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


139 Cal. App. 3d 682; 189 Cal. Rptr. 25; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1365



February 2, 1983 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Mar. 29, 1983, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Siskiyou County, No. 33748, Guy Martin Young, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California), which convicted him of assault with a deadly weapon or by means likely to produce great bodily injury and rape. Defendant claimed that the trial court erroneously refused to order a pretrial psychiatric examination of the victim of a sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon or by means likely to produce great bodily injury and rape. On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court improperly denied his request for a pretrial psychiatric examination of the victim, claiming that Cal. Penal Code § 1112 was unconstitutional. The court rejected this contention, finding that the statute abrogated a prior common law rule that allowed a trial court to order a psychiatric examination of a victim. The court found that the statute was not an unconstitutional infringement of the separation of powers clause under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3 because the inherent power of the courts could not have overridden a legislative prohibition. The statute did not violate substantive due process because the statute was not arbitrary, and the statute did not deny defendant equal protection of the laws because the use of psychiatric testimony to impeach a witness was generally disapproved in other criminal cases. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of assault with a deadly weapon or by means likely to produce great bodily injury and rape. The court found that the statute that abrogated a prior common law rule allowing a trial court to order a victim to undergo a psychiatric evaluation was constitutional under separation of powers, due process and equal protection challenges.

1325 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Intellidata Inc. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. AO16108


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


139 Cal. App. 3d 594; 188 Cal. Rptr. 850; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1354



January 31, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 755915, Robert W. Merrill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff computer service bureau challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), requiring it to pay sales tax on the gross receipts from the sale of its keypunching services, arguing that it should be exempt from such taxes as the true value of the transactions between plaintiff and its customers was the service rendered by plaintiff and not the cards themselves.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff computer service bureau sought review of a lower court judgment requiring it to pay sales tax on the gross receipts from the sale of its keypunching services, contending that such activities were exempt from sales tax because the true object of the transactions between plaintiff and its customers were the services rendered by plaintiff and not the media on which the services were delivered. On appeal, the lower court judgment was affirmed. In support of its ruling, the court held that as plaintiff took blank keypunch cards and altered them through the keypunching process so that they could be used to transfer data into each customer's computer, plaintiff produced tangible property to the special order of its customers pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6006(f). Accordingly, as tangible property such as keypunched cards may be taxed on their total value even though their primary value was attributed to an intangible element such as keypunching services, the court found that the lower court judgment was proper.


OUTCOME: The lower court judgment was affirmed as the court found plaintiff computer service bureau's activities subject to the sales tax because it produced tangible property within the meaning of the statute.

 

1326 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Civil Serv. Ass'n v. Civil Serv. Com


Civ. No. 53569


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


139 Cal. App. 3d 449; 188 Cal. Rptr. 806; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1342



January 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 20, 1983. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 761724, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, (California) denying a petition for writ of mandate whereby a public employee sought reinstatement as a clerk-stenographer with the San Francisco Police Department.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was terminated from the San Francisco Police Department after she refused to submit to a polygraph test. The trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate seeking reinstatement. The court of appeals affirmed. The Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3300 et seq., prohibiting any disciplinary action against peace officers for refusing to submit to a polygraph test did not offend constitutional equal protection principles even though civilian police department employees were not similarly protected. The Legislature determined that maintaining labor peace among public safety officers was more critical than among other public employees. The Legislature could have rationally concluded that the threat to the citizens of the state which might have resulted from an absence of police services was greater than that which might have resulted from the interruption of any other public service. Thus, because the classification rested on a reasonable differentiation fairly related to the object of legislation, it did not offend equal protection principles.


OUTCOME: The order denying a petition for writ of mandate was affirmed because a civilian police department employee was not protected by legislation prohibiting any disciplinary action against officers for refusing to submit to a polygraph test.

 

1327 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Scott v. Farrar


Civ. No. 65042


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


139 Cal. App. 3d 462; 188 Cal. Rptr. 823; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1343



January 26, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SEC 34539, Warren H. Deering, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered in their personal injury action against defendant, a school crossing guard. Plaintiffs claimed that the trial court erred in entering summary judgment for defendant and denying their motion for new trial based on newly discovered evidence, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 657.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a school crossing guard, was working at the crossing where plaintiff parents' minor son was hit by a car while crossing. Plaintiffs noticed the deposition of a witness. Before the deposition was held, defendant filed his motion for summary judgment. The evidence at the hearing established that defendant had no duty to man the crossing at the time the child crossed. The deposition testimony then established that defendant usually was at the crossing at the time of the accident. While the summary judgment evidence established no duty on the part of defendant, and summary judgment had been properly entered, plaintiffs' motion for a new trial had been improperly denied. Ordinarily, plaintiffs would have been prejudiced by their failure to seek a continuance under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c, except that they did not know what testimony their deponent would give. As such, the testimony was truly newly discovered, and it was material, in that it tended to prove that defendant assumed a duty he did not otherwise have. Thus, the trial court's order was reversed and the cause remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for defendant, a school crossing guard, and remanded for a new trial. The trial court erred in denying plaintiff parents' motion for a new trial, because plaintiffs' were not required to seek to continue the summary judgment hearing to after the deposition of one of their witnesses. The deponent gave material evidence that was considered newly discovered, thus, plaintiffs were entitled to a new trial.

Fullerton Union High Sch. Dist. v. Riles


Civ. No. 26040


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


139 Cal. App. 3d 369; 188 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1337



January 24, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 334693, Edward J. Wallin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to issue the writ of mandate as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant high school district sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that denied appellant's writ of mandate request to compel respondent California State Department of Education to return money withheld from appellant's scheduled apportionment of state funds.


OVERVIEW: Appellant high school district sought the return of funds that respondent California State Department of Education had deducted from its scheduled apportionment payment. The trial court found in favor of respondent. Appellant challenged the judgment, contending that respondent was bound by the doctrine of equitable estoppel from rejecting appellant's claim for repayment of the recaptured funds. The court agreed with appellant's contention finding that respondent was estopped from denying appellant's claim for the return of recaptured funds. The court ruled that respondent was obligated to inform appellant of the availability of funds because that was its function, that respondent instead discouraged appellant from applying for funds, and that appellant expended resources to add courses in reliance on respondent's advice. The court also found that respondent was not authorized to recapture funds from appellant because the relevant statute did not create a right to claim back funds, rather, the plain words of the statute barred appellant from filing amended reports in the future. Accordingly, the court reversed the holding of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that found in favor of respondent California State Department of Education. The court held that respondent was estopped from rejecting appellant high school district's claim for the return of recaptured funds because appellant expanded its vocational education program in reliance on respondent's representations that appellant's claim for funding was approved.

 

1329 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Fain


Crim. No. A019142


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


139 Cal. App. 3d 295; 188 Cal. Rptr. 653; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1330



January 21, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 1, 1983. A petition for a rehearing was denied February 14, 1983, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 23, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 106958, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We have decided, however, in deference to the board's authority, that the matter should be remanded to the board for further review and determination in light of our opinion. (See fn. 6.) The trial court should retain jurisdiction over the matter, pending the board's action. 6



6 This disposition of the case makes it unnecessary to resolve Fain's contention that rescission of his parole amounts to an "ex post facto law" and is therefore unconstitutional for that reason.

Ex post facto laws are prohibited by both the federal and state Constitutions. (U.S. Const., art. I, § 9, cl. 3, art. I, § 10, cl. 1; Cal. Const., art. I, § 9.)

Mere rescission of Fain's parole based upon good cause would not amount to an ex post facto violation. The recent case of In re Stanworth, supra, 33 Cal.3d 176, however, makes it clear that if the board were permitted to rely upon newly established or implemented criteria, such as independent consideration of public outcry, in rescinding Fain's parole this would amount to an ex post facto violation. Moreover, if Fain's parole were rescinded and a new parole date was considered based upon determinate sentencing law criteria rather than Indeterminate Sentencing Law criteria, this, too, would be an ex post facto violation.

In Stanworth, an inmate sentenced under the ISL in 1966 but considered for parole in 1979 under the DSL argued that the DSL's focus on the nature of his crime and its goal of uniformity of punishment among those committing similar crimes conflicted, to his prejudice, with the emphasis on individual rehabilitation of the former ISL. ( Id., at p. 179.)

The Supreme Court concluded that the change from ISL to DSL was more than procedural, thereby invoking ex post facto law principles, noting that the change in sentencing guidelines "followed and reflected the change in the underlying statutory scheme." ( Id., at pp. 181-182.)

The court noted that under the ISL parole release depended upon the individual characteristics of a particular inmate. ( Id., at p. 182.) Summarizing, the court remarked that the new rules generally reflect "an attempt to achieve uniformity and stress the criminal activities of the inmate rather than any social or personal factors." ( Id., at p. 186.)

"The critical issue before us," the Supreme Court declared, "thus becomes not whether a change in the actual date of release has been effected, but whether the standards by which defendant's date of release is to be determined have been altered to his detriment." (Ibid.)

The court concluded that the defendant's postconviction behavior "was treated under DSL very differently from the manner contemplated under the 1976 regulations. It appears that the 'standard of punishment' has substantially changed because postconviction behavior is no longer utilized in the same manner in determining his parole release date." ( Id., at p. 187.) "This alternation in the law may well be to defendant's disadvantage because his postconviction behavior has been given different weight in the fixing of his term." (Ibid.) Thus, "we conclude that the standard of punishment has been altered to defendant's prejudice in violation of ex post facto principles." ( Id., at p. 188.)

Defendant was thus held entitled to parole release consideration under both ISL and DSL standards. (Ibid.)

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Prison Terms sought review of a grant of respondent inmate's habeas corpus petition by the Superior Court of Marin County (California). Appellant also sought a stay of the trial court's decision, which was granted by the appellate court, which stated the public outrage over respondent's new release date was understandable, but it did not constitute "new information" preventing release.


OVERVIEW: Respondent inmate was convicted of murder, rape, sex perversion, kidnapping, attempted kidnapping, escape, armed robbery, and kidnapping arising from the escape, and was sentenced to life in prison. After public outcry, a superior court disallowed a rescission hearing by appellant Board of Prison Terms and ordered respondent's release. On appeal, the hearing was ordered, and a new parole date was set. After extraordinary public outcry, appellant again rescinded parole. The trial court granted respondent's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Appellant sought review, and the trial court's decision was stayed. The appellate court held that the matter was to be remanded to appellant for further review and determination in light of the court's opinion, and the trial court retained jurisdiction over the matter, pending appellant's action. Public outcry did not constitute good cause for rescission of parole, and reliance upon outcry was inconsistent with good behavior, rehabilitation, and other considerations in making a parole determination. Constitutionally, parole revocation proceedings had to conform to minimum due process requirements, and the public opinion's role was limited.


OUTCOME: The grant of respondent inmate's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was proper and the matter, regarding parole rescission on a life sentence for heinous crimes, was to be remanded to appellant Board of Prison Terms for review in light of the court's opinion, and the trial court had to retain jurisdiction. Public outcry did not constitute good cause for rescission of parole, and constitutional due process requirements had to be met.

 

1330 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Diaz v. Oakland Tribune, Inc.


Civ. No. 50954


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


139 Cal. App. 3d 118; 188 Cal. Rptr. 762; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1314; 9 Media L. Rep. 1121



January 18, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On February 17, 1983, the opinion and judgment were modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 508893-1, Robert K. Barber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellants and respondent shall each bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, newspaper owner/publisher and columnist, challenged the judgment of Superior Court of Alameda County (California), awarding respondent individual compensatory and punitive damages for invasion of privacy because a published newspaper article contained embarrassing private facts, specifically that respondent was a transsexual, that were unwarranted, malicious, and caused severe emotional distress. A motion for new trial was denied.


OVERVIEW: Respondent individual chose to undergo a sex change operation which she was happy with, but kept the surgery a secret. Appellant columnist wrote a published article for appellant owner/publisher's newspaper revealing that respondent was a transsexual. Respondent filed suit against appellants for invasion of privacy, claiming the article contained embarrassing private facts that were unwarranted, malicious, and caused emotional distress. The trial court awarded compensatory and punitive damages to respondent and denied a motion for new trial. The appellate court reversed the judgment, holding the trial court misinstructed the jury on the right to privacy and on the burden of proving newsworthiness, which were prejudicial errors. The right to privacy instruction required a "compelling public need" for the intrusion, which was too high a standard that abridged the constitutional right of free speech and press. On the newsworthiness instruction, it was misleading to assert that appellants had the burden of proof. Respondent had the burden of proving the publication was not privileged or newsworthy, but appellants could rebut the showing with newsworthiness evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment awarding respondent individual damages for invasion of privacy to be free from public disclosure of private embarrassing facts, favoring appellants, newspaper owner/publisher and columnist. The "compelling public need" standard for the intrusion was too high, abridging constitutional rights, and the newsworthiness burden of proof on appellants was misleading in jury instructions.

Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers association sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, title company and individual company, and sustained demurrers brought by respondents, state, county and city, in an action for real property title claim, on the ground that the evidence disclosed triable issues of fact.

Seguros La Provincial v. Fremont Indem. Co.


Civ. No. 24952


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


138 Cal. App. 3d 923; 188 Cal. Rptr. 331; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1298



January 4, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 2, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 444678, Carlos A. Cazares, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant California insurer sought review of summary judgment by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) declaring that an automobile policy issued by respondent Mexican insurer did not provide coverage for bodily injuries to an occupant. Appellant claimed that Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.1, required the coverage and appellee should be required to defend against claims by occupant.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Mexican insurer sought and obtained a summary judgment in declaratory relief claim in the trial court, which held that appellee was not the primary carrier in a pending automobile accident claim, and that appellant California insurer was the primary carrier, so appellee was not required to defend litigation based on occupant's bodily injuries, which were excluded in appellee's policy. Appellant sought reversal of the trial court's order and claimed that appellee was required to include coverage for the occupant's bodily injury under Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.1(g), and should be the primary carrier and required to defend. The court affirmed the trial court's summary judgment in favor of appellee. The court held that appellee, as a nonadmitted Mexican insurer, was not required by § 11580.1(g), to maintain coverage for an occupant's bodily injury, and was consequently not the primary carrier and not required to defend against the occupant's claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's granting of a summary judgment in favor of appellee Mexican insurer and against appellee California insurer because appellee was not required to maintain occupant coverage as a nonadmitted insurer and had no duty to defend against the occupant's claims.

 

1333 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Willadsen v. Justice Court


Civ. No. 21184


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


139 Cal. App. 3d 171; 188 Cal. Rptr. 488; 1983 Cal. App. LEXIS 1318



January 4, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Butte County, No. 75748, Reginald M. Watt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which denied plaintiff's motion to dismiss defendant regulatory agency's charge that plaintiff violated Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1603 by altering a stream without an agreement, and denied his petition for a writ of prohibition, alleging the enabling statute, § 1603, and the implementing statute, Cal. Admin. Code tit. 14, § 720 (1977), were void.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff applicant was charged with violation of Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1603, for altering a stream without an agreement by rerouting it for winter runoff. Plaintiff challenged the denial of his motion to dismiss and petition for writ of prohibition. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment against plaintiff and held that the implementing statute, Cal. Admin. Code tit. 14, § 720 (1977) was not void because respondent regulatory agency did not exceed its authority under the enabling statute, Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1603, in designating all state bodies of water as subject to the notification and approval requirements under § 1603 and because the legislature intended that all, not some, state waterways to be governed by § 1603. The court found that § 1603 was not void for lack of standards because respondent was not conferred with unrestricted authority to make fundamental policy decisions and because Cal. Fish & Game Code § 1600 contained sufficiently definite standards to provide directives of conduct for respondent in exercising its delegated powers; and plaintiff had no standing to challenge the arbitration provision because it did not apply to him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff applicant's motion to dismiss and petition for writ of prohibition and held the implementing statute designating all state bodies of water as subject to the applicable requirements was not void because defendant regulatory agency did not exceed its authority under the enabling statute; and the enabling statute was not void for lack of standards because defendant's authority was not unrestricted.

 

1334 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gonda


Crim. No. 19102


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


138 Cal. App. 3d 774; 188 Cal. Rptr. 295; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2278



December 30, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 17, 1983. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 97359, S. Lee Vavuris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The convictions on counts 17, 18, 19, 20, 21, 22, 27 for grand theft are reversed. The remaining convictions appealed from are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant consultants challenged their convictions by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, for grand theft by false pretenses in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 487, and unlawful sale of franchises by means of untrue statements, Cal. Corp. Code § 31201, along with violations of Cal. Corp. Code § 31110, the unlawful offering and selling of a franchise without prior registration.


OVERVIEW: Appellant consultants merged their consulting businesses and invited small and medium-sized firms to join them as "independent contractors" for a fee, to service their clients. The "franchisees" relied on representations of strong earnings potential. Complaints of disappointed "franchisees" resulted in guilty verdicts on counts of grand theft by false pretenses, Cal. Penal Code § 487, unlawful sale of franchises by means of untrue statements, Cal. Corp. Code § 31201, and unlawful offering and selling of a franchise without prior registration, Cal. Corp. Code § 31110. On appeal, the court reversed appellants' § 487 grand theft convictions because they were based on the same facts as the § 31201 unlawful sale of franchises convictions, and appellants could not be convicted of both included offenses. The court affirmed appellants § 31110 convictions and held that there was no error in excluding evidence that appellants relied on counsel in its actions; while reliance on a state official's advice could be a defense, advice of private counsel was not. The court held that the applicable statutes provided an adequate warning as to the forbidden conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant consultants' grand theft convictions because they were included offenses of unlawful sale of franchises by means of untrue statements, but affirmed appellant's other convictions because the evidence was sufficient to support them and appellant could not raise, as a defense, that they had relied upon advice of private counsel in luring investors into their consulting business.

 

1335 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Kurbegovic


Crim. No. 39123


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


138 Cal. App. 3d 731; 188 Cal. Rptr. 268; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2275



December 29, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 29, 1983. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Kurbegovich v. Unknown Politicians, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 158020 (E.D. Cal., Nov. 6, 2014)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Dismissed by Kurbegovich v. Unknown "Politicians", 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 125275 (C.D. Cal., Sept. 17, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-311331, Nancy Belcher Watson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held defendant competent to stand trial and convicted defendant of twenty-five felonies including murder and arson.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. Defendant bombed public areas and sent dangerous packages to government officials as part of his mission to exact revenge for and in order to change the treatment of American aliens. After several years and several decisions that defendant was incompetent to stand trial, he was found to be competent. Defendant was convicted of twenty-five felonies including murder and arson. On appeal, the court held that witness testimony and real evidence showed defendant's competency. The court decided that symptoms where feigned and exaggerated as part of his strategy to be declared incompetent many times so the state would stop pursuing his conviction. The court decided that he was an educated person and very intelligent. The court also held that the trial court properly allowed him to represent himself as he had successfully done in the past, and that a standby attorney request was properly denied. The court also held that interactions with the jury were proper, a motion to suppress evidence was correctly denied, and that admission of photographic evidence was, in fact, quite limited and not an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of the trial court on the grounds that defendant was competent to stand trial and represent himself, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in the trial.

 

1336 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Aptos Seascape Corp. v. County of Santa Cruz


Civ. No. 46963


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


138 Cal. App. 3d 484; 188 Cal. Rptr. 191; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2253



December 23, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied January 21, 1983, and the petitions of all parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied April 20, 1983. Richardson, J., Kaus, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 50142, Roland K. Hall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That portion of the judgment concluding Seascape's property has been taken without just compensation and awarding damages for the taking is reversed. The trial court is directed to modify that portion of the judgment dismissing Seascape's second cause of action for declaratory relief by conditioning that dismissal on the grant of compensating densities to Seascape; as modified, that portion of the judgment is affirmed. Judgment in favor of Seascape on the County's cross-complaint is affirmed. Each party to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiff landowner and defendant county appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which awarded plaintiff damages in plaintiff's inverse condemnation action under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. Defendant also appealed from an adverse judgment on its cross-complaint, which alleged an implied dedication to the public of plaintiff's beach property.


OVERVIEW: After a bench trial in plaintiff landowner's inverse condemnation action, both plaintiff and defendant county appealed the trial court's order finding that a local zoning ordinance effectively prohibited development upon and precluded all reasonable use of plaintiff's property, and that the property was taken for public use without just compensation in violation of U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV and Cal. Const. art. 1, § 19. The trial court awarded plaintiff damages. The court reversed the trial court's damage award, holding that damages were precluded in an inverse condemnation challenge to a zoning ordinance under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV and Cal. Const. art. 1, § 19, although plaintiff could attempt to invalidate the ordinance as excessive regulation through declaratory relief or mandamus. The court further held that under the ordinances, defendant could grant plaintiff compensating densities on both parcels of its land, thus precluding a finding of a taking. On defendant's cross-claim for an implied dedication, the court concluded plaintiff's property was not subject to any public servitude.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's award of damages to plaintiff landowner in an inverse condemnation claim against defendant county, because plaintiff could only challenge the validity of the zoning ordinance and directed the trial court to modify the judgment conditioning dismissal of plaintiff's declaratory judgment claim on defendant's grant of compensating densities. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment on defendant's cross-claim.

 

1337 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



City of L.A. v. Cal.


Civ. No. 66030


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


138 Cal. App. 3d 526; 187 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2255



December 23, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 11, 1983, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 2, 1983. Richardson, J., and Kaus, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C289774, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and attorney general, challenged the judgment entered in favor of respondent city by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared Cal. Gov't Code § 65860(d) unconstitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, state and attorney general, challenged the declaratory judgment of the superior court, in favor of respondent city, which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 65860(d) was unconstitutional on its face, for being vague, contrary to home rule, and a constitutionally improper classification. Section 65860(d) required that charter cities with a population of two million or more were subject to certain requirements contained in the state planning and zoning law, Cal. Gov't Code, § 65000 et seq. The superior court held that § 65860(d) was constitutional on its face. The court reversed and held that cities with a larger population were rationally related to a legislative concern of probable impact land use decisions that large cities would have on the numerous municipalities that surrounded it, and that it surrounded. A failure on the part of respondent to enact zoning ordinances consistent with its general plan jeopardized appellant state's interest in promoting cooperative planning efforts among adjoining municipalities. The court held that the home rule doctrine was inapplicable where the statewide interest superceded the municipalities' interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's determination that a zoning ordinance was unconstitutional and held that the declaration of facial invalidity was incorrect because there was a facially rational reason for the legislature's enactment of the statute.

 

1338 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Krusesky v. Baugh


Civ. No. 24829


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


138 Cal. App. 3d 562; 188 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2260



December 23, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 461475, Carlos A. Cazares, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with instructions to the trial court to overrule the demurrer based on the statute of limitations. The parties shall bear their respective costs on this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff client appealed the sustaining of defendant attorney's demurrer, based on the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6, in her malpractice suit in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California). Plaintiff claimed her malpractice suit was timely because she filed it within one year of her discovery, through use of reasonable diligence, of the facts constituting defendant's negligent act.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff client appealed the trial court's decision to sustain defendant attorney's demurrer, based on the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6, in her malpractice claim. When defendant represented plaintiff in a divorce action in 1969, he incorrectly informed her that she had no right to any part of her husband's military pension. Another attorney informed plaintiff of her community property interest in 1980, and plaintiff brought a malpractice claim eight months later. Although § 340.6, adopting a one-year discovery rule or a four-year occurrence rule, was enacted in 1978, the court decided that a shortened period of limitations could not have been applied retroactively to wipe out an accrued cause of action that was not barred by the then applicable statute of limitations. Because of defendant's advice, the court found that plaintiff's lack of suspicion about not receiving part of her husband's pension benefits was understandable and her delay in investigating his competence was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment sustaining defendant attorney's demurrer, based on the statute of limitations, in the malpractice suit plaintiff client brought complaining of the incorrect advice he provided when handling her 1969 divorce. The court directed the overruling of the demurrer. The court explained that plaintiff filed her malpractice action within one year of her reasonable discovery of the facts showing defendant's negligence.

E. L. White v. City of Huntington Beach


Civ. No. 25416


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


138 Cal. App. 3d 366; 187 Cal. Rptr. 879; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2244



December 21, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellants E. L. White, Inc., and Royal Globe Insurance Company for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 234971 and 337944, Edward J. Wallin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to allow interest from the date that White and Royal Globe paid their portion of the personal injury judgment, and, as modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a construction contractor and his insurer, appealed a judgment rendered in the Superior Court of Orange County (California), that enjoined plaintiff insurer from obtaining a judgment of indemnity against defendant city whose excess insurer was insolvent. Plaintiffs also appealed the indemnity award rendered at trial and sought a prejudgment interest award. Defendant appealed trial court's award of total indemnity to plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a construction contractor and his insurer, appealed a trial judgment on the grounds that they should be totally indemnified by defendant city for wrongful death and personal injury judgments. Plaintiffs also sought prejudgment interest. Defendant city also appealed and claimed that plaintiffs were not entitled to total indemnity. The court modified the judgment rendered by the trial court because it held that plaintiffs were entitled to prejudgment interest pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 3287(a), calculated from the date that plaintiffs paid their portion of a personal injury judgment. The court found that plaintiffs' damages became certain when they satisfied the judgment and that defendant was also aware of the exact amount plaintiffs had paid by virtue of having paid the other half of the judgment award. The court also found the possibility that the underlying judgments were subject to a possible reduction if plaintiff construction contractor was found to have been more than vicariously liable did not render plaintiffs' damages any less certain. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial judgment to allow prejudgment interest from the date that plaintiffs, a construction contractor and his insurer, paid their portion of a personal injury judgment, and, as modified, the judgment was affirmed. The court found plaintiffs were entitled to prejudgment interest because their damages became certain when payment was made and also found defendant city was aware of the exact amount of plaintiffs' payment.

Mobil Oil Corp. v. Rossi


Civ. No. 24670


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


138 Cal. App. 3d 256; 187 Cal. Rptr. 845; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2231



December 17, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 288590, Alicemarie H. Stotler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants gasoline dealers challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) based on a directed verdict for respondent petroleum distributor in its unlawful detainer action and holding that the good cause requirements of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174(a) and Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 20999.1 did not apply to the lease.


OVERVIEW: Respondent petroleum dealer terminated appellant gasoline dealers' lease and sought to regain possession of the gas station through an unlawful detainer action. The trial court directed a verdict for respondent. The trial court held that the lease was an integrated document, and that the good cause requirements of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 20999.1 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1174(a) did not apply to the lease, which was entered before § 20999.1's effective date. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the trial court's rejection of relevant evidence on the nature and extent of respondent's reliance on the former law and treatment of the constitutional impairment of contracts question as being one of law deprived the court of information essential to its weighing process. The court concluded that statutes applied retroactively, but that it was incumbent on the trial court to determine whether the retroactive application was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment for respondent in its unlawful detainer action was reversed because it was incumbent on the trial court to determine whether retroactive application of the statutes was constitutional in the circumstances of the case.

 

1341 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Tolhurst


Crim. No. 11922


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


139 Cal. App. 3d 1; 188 Cal. Rptr. 474; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2293



December 16, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified January 14, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 26939, Frank D. Francis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged an order of the Superior Court of Nevada County (California), which denied their motion to suppress evidence of marijuana that had been destroyed.


OVERVIEW: After marijuana plants were seized from defendants, the sheriff's department retained a sample, then weighed, photographed, and destroyed the remaining plants. Defendants moved to suppress evidence of the destroyed plants, contending the marijuana was unlawfully destroyed under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11479 because the search warrant ordered the evidence to be brought before the court. The motion was denied, and defendants were convicted of possession of marijuana for sale. Defendants sought review. The court found that the statute allowed for the destruction of contraband without a court order when large amounts of controlled substances were seized. The court also held that the sheriff's department scrupulously conformed to the requirements of the statute by segregating a sample, photographing the plants while growing and in bulk, and determining their total weight. The court affirmed judgment convicting defendants because defendants failed to raise their argument that the statute was unconstitutional at the trial level.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendants' motion to suppress evidence of marijuana that had been destroyed because a sheriff was permitted by statute to destroy large amounts of controlled substance without a court order, and the sheriff's office conformed to the statute's requirements.

Dep't of Veterans Affairs v. Duerksen


Civ. No. 21005


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


138 Cal. App. 3d 149; 187 Cal. Rptr. 832; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2218



December 15, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 292237, Horace Cecchettini, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant veteran sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Sacramento County (California), which granted plaintiff department of veteran's affairs' motion for summary judgment in its suit to quiet title under a canceled Cal-Vet contract pursuant to the Veterans' Farm and Home Purchase Act of 1974 (Act), Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 987.50 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant veteran entered into a Cal-Vet contract for the purchase of a home with plaintiff department of veterans affairs pursuant to the Veterans' Farm and Home Purchase Act of 1974 (Act), Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 987.50 et seq. Defendant later entered into an installment contract to sell the property to a purchaser ineligible under the Act. After warning defendant that the subsequent sale was in violation of the Cal-Vet contract, plaintiff canceled the contract and sued to quiet title. The trial court granted summary judgement to plaintiff. The court rejected defendant's contention that the due-on-sale clause of the Cal-Vet contract with plaintiff was an unreasonable restraint on alienation because Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 987.73 authorized the contractual requirement that plaintiff consent to any transfer of the property. The court held that Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 987.77 expressly authorized plaintiff to force a forfeiture where a veteran breached his Cal-Vet contract. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to plaintiff department of veterans affairs in its suit to quiet title under a canceled Cal-Vet contract with defendant veteran because the contract provisions did not constitute an unreasonable restraint on alienation.

McKirdy v. Superior Court


No. A017222


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


138 Cal. App. 3d 12; 188 Cal. Rptr. 143; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2201



December 13, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1983. Grodin, J., did not participate therein.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon issuance of an alternative writ, petitioner psychiatrist filed a petition for a writ of mandate or prohibition to vacate respondent superior court's order validating a search of petitioner's home and office.


OVERVIEW: The attorney general's medi-cal fraud unit obtained a search warrant for the office and home of petitioner psychiatrist from the municipal court. The fraud unit investigators seized 49 files. Petitioner filed a motion in the municipal court to quash the search warrant and for return of property, which was granted. The attorney general petitioned respondent superior court for a writ of mandate or prohibition. The superior court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition vacating the municipal court's order. Upon consideration, the court of appeal concluded that the patients' privacy was not violated and the affidavit and warrant were proper. The superior court's ruling was correct and the municipal court should not have granted the psychiatrist's petition to quash the warrant. The petition for a peremptory writ was denied and the alternative writ was discharged.


OUTCOME: Petitioner psychiatrist's petition for a peremptory writ to vacate respondent superior court's order validating the search was denied because the superior court's ruling was correct and the municipal court should not have granted petitioner's petition to quash the warrant. People v. Bowen


Crim. Nos. 12316, 14575


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


137 Cal. App. 3d 1020; 187 Cal. Rptr. 614; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2197



December 9, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 27, 1982, and the petitions of both parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied March 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-17023, Gerald F. Schulte, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the penalty and the cause is remanded with directions to determine the appropriate disposition consistent with the views expressed in this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. The petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted of murder in the first degree and rape, challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which sentenced him for the murder to life imprisonment without possibility of parole. Defendant claimed inadequacy of trial counsel, insufficiency of evidence, prosecutorial misconduct, erroneous denial of a suppression motion, prejudicial photographic evidence, improper jury instruction and sentencing error.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and rape. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole for the murder and six years for rape. Defendant claimed inadequacy of trial counsel, insufficiency of evidence, prosecutorial misconduct, erroneous denial of a suppression motion, unduly prejudicial photographic evidence, improper jury instructions, and sentencing error. Defendant contended that he could not be sentenced to life imprisonment without parole because he was a minor at the time of the murder and asserted error because he was not sent to the California Youth Authority (CYA) for evaluation prior to his sentencing. The court held that all but defendant's sentencing contention were meritless. The court, reversing as to penalty, held that a minor could not be imprisoned for life without parole and that there was error in refusing to remand defendant to the CYA. The court stated that the effect of Cal. Penal Code §190.2(a)(17)(iii) was unclear on its effect on the penalty applicable to minors and silent regarding appropriate procedures by which the new penalty would be imposed on them and consequently any ambiguity must be resolved in defendant's favor.


OUTCOME: The court partly affirmed the trial court's decision, finding that all but defendant's sentencing contention were meritless. The court reversed as to the penalty, and ruled that the trial court erred in refusing to remand defendant to the California Youth Authority for evaluation before sentencing, and defendant could not be sentenced to life imprisonment without parole because the statute was unclear on penalty applicable to minors.

Ingebretsen v. McNamer


Civ. No. 51806


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


137 Cal. App. 3d 957; 187 Cal. Rptr. 529; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2189



December 6, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 682473, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judgment creditors sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which issued a writ of execution against respondent homeowner's dwelling house and determined the homestead exemption to be $ 40,000.


OVERVIEW: The trial court issued a writ of execution against respondent homeowner's dwelling house and determined the homestead exemption was $ 40,000. Appellant judgment creditors challenged the order. A default judgment was entered against appellee for the wrongful death of appellants' son two years earlier. No effort was made to satisfy the judgment until three years later, when a writ of execution was levied on respondent's homestead. At the time judgment was entered, the statutory homestead exemption was $ 20,000. The legislature increased the amount of the exemption twice, until it totalled $ 40,000. Appellants argued that the applicable exemption was the one in effect at the time of the judgment. On appeal, the court held that the most recent homestead exemption applied. The amount of respondent's exemption was $ 40,000. The court found that the increase in the exemption amount and its retroactive application did not abrogate appellants' rights. The retroactive application did not impair any contractual obligations between the parties and did not violate state or federal constitutions. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the most recent homestead exemption, in the amount of $ 40,000, applied to respondent homeowner. The court found that the increase in the exemption amount and its retroactive application did not abrogate the rights of appellants, judgment creditors. The retroactive application did not impair any contractual obligations between the parties and did not violate state or federal constitutions.

Bonds v. Cal. ex rel. Cal. Highway Patrol


Civ. No. 62385


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


138 Cal. App. 3d 314; 187 Cal. Rptr. 792; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2237



November 24, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 23, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 26, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC74414, Robert H. London, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cross-complainant challenged the judgment of dismissal of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered in favor of respondent cross-defendant State of California as a result of the demurrer filed by the state to appellant's first amended cross-complaint for indemnity, equitable contribution, and declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellant cross-complainant collided with two vehicles parked on the side of the highway. One had been ticketed by the highway patrol for illegal parking. The collision caused serious injuries and death. Appellant's liability insurance carrier filed an interpleader to deposit the proceeds for claims to be made. When defendants, parents of the decedents, filed a cross-complaint for wrongful death, appellant filed a cross-complaint for indemnity, contribution, and declaratory relief against respondent state. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer without leave to amend. The court affirmed the judgment because respondent owed no duty to remove the vehicle from the highway because, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 22651(b), the removal was a discretionary act. The highway patrol action did not establish a special relation because the issued ticket did not create or change a risk to a known person. Government immunity, pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 820.2, was justified because it was established that respondent imposed conditions and limitations on the right to bring suit against it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal because respondent state owed no duty to remove a disabled vehicle from the highway because the removal was a discretionary act. Ticketing of the disabled vehicle by the highway patrol did not establish a special relation because the ticket did not create or change a risk to a known person, and thus government immunity was justified.

 

1347 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Davis v. City of Sacramento


Civ. No. 21435


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


138 Cal. App. 3d 356; 188 Cal. Rptr. 607; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2243



November 24, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 23, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 295631, John M. Sapunor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff disabled police officer appealed an adverse judgment by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate against respondent city, and which judgment determined that Sacramento City, Cal., Charter §§ 413 and 415, were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff disabled police officer challenged the declaration of constitutionality of Sacramento City, Cal., Charter §§ 413 and 415, by the superior court and the denial of his requested writ of mandate. Plaintiff believed that he was denied equal treatment because his mental illness was covered only by ordinary disability rather than industrial disability, which was a lower rate of compensation. The court affirmed and held that although plaintiff's pension benefits were fundamental rights, the issue itself was one of economic and social welfare, which was evaluated by the rational basis test. The constitution did not guarantee uniform treatment, but only that any diversity of treatment would have a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose. The court held that mental illness was difficult to diagnose and that more extensive coverage would entail greater expense to the state, and consequently a greater expense via the contribution rate to the participating employees. The court found a rational legislative reason for the classification scheme and held that the statutes were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and constitutionality of the disability retirement statutes because there was a rational basis in the Sacramento City Employees' Retirement System for covering mental illness differently under the industrial disability retirement section and the ordinary disability retirement section.

 

1348 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reeder


Crim. No. 11309.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


137 Cal. App. 3d 595; 187 Cal. Rptr. 278; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2152



November 22, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 10, 1983 (See 152 C.A.3d 900)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 25680, Terrence J. Keeley, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Nevada County (California), challenging the trial court's application of Cal. Penal Code § 667.6 in assessing his punishment for violent sexual offenses.


OVERVIEW: In sentencing defendant for multiple violent sex offenses against multiple victims, the trial court applied Cal. Penal Code § 667.6 to offenses committed against one victim after the effective date of § 667.6 and ordered that the sentences be full, separate, and consecutive. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions but remanded for resentencing based on its conclusion that discretionary consecutive sentences under § 667.6(c), rather than the mandatory provisions of § 667.6(d), applied. The court found that defendant committed the crimes against the victim on a single evening in one continuous, uninterrupted sequence over a short period of time in the same location. Thus, the end of one criminal episode was not set apart by an interruption of sufficient duration that the beginning of another could be ascertained with reasonable certainty. Accordingly, the same victim on separate occasions requirement under § 667.6(d) could not be invoked to require defendant to serve consecutive sentences for each offense committed against the victim. Further, because it was not clear whether the trial court exercised sentencing discretion, remand for resentencing was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's judgment of conviction for multiple violent sex offenses against multiple victims but remanded for resentencing. Because certain offenses were committed against the same victim on the same occasion, the statutory provision for discretionary mandatory consecutive sentences, rather than the mandatory provision, applied.
