Allen v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 62827.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


137 Cal. App. 3d 652; 187 Cal. Rptr. 192; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2155



November 22, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 3, 1983 (See 34 C.3d 114)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 313948, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that issued a writ of mandate directing appellant to compute respondent former legislators' retirement allowances in accordance with Cal. Gov't Code §§ 9359.1(a) and 9360.9 without giving effect to § 9359.11 and Cal. Const. art. IV, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Respondent former legislators served between 1963-1965. Respondents filed claims with appellant board seeking recalculation of their retirement allowances under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 9359.1(a) and 9360.9, without giving effect to § 9359.11 and Cal. Const. art. IV, § 4. An administrative hearing found appellant had jurisdiction to recalculate, but later appellant vacated that finding and concluded it lacked jurisdiction. Respondents petitioned the trial court for a writ of mandate. The trial court granted judgment in favor of respondents, ordered writ of mandate, and declared that Cal. Const. art. IV, § 4 and Cal. Gov't Code § 9359.11 could not be constitutionally applied to respondents. Appellant challenged that decision, citing that mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1094.5 or 1085 was an inappropriate remedy. The court found that under the doctrine of stare decisis respondents were entitled to calculation of their pensions under the fluctuating formula and cost of living formula because their pension rights had vested, matured, and could not be divested by or impaired by subsequent enactment of a state constitutional amendment or legislative enactment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment holding that the doctrine of stare decisis compelled the conclusion that subsequent enactments to state constitution or legislative amendments could not apply to the calculation of respondents' vested pension rights under both the fluctuating salary formula and the cost of living formula which were added together.

 

1350 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Pierce v. Harrold


Civ. No. 29234


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


138 Cal. App. 3d 415; 188 Cal. Rptr. 458; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2153



November 22, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 21, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 16, 1983. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 381600 and 381599, Ronald Eugene Owen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and the motion for findings per Code of Civil Procedure section 909 is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant candidate appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which ruled in favor of plaintiff contestants, determined that defendant violated Cal. Elec. Code § 29303 by falsely declaring she was a resident of Orange County at the time she filed her declaration for candidacy, and nullified defendant's election pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 20021(c).


OVERVIEW: The trial court nullified defendant candidate's election to municipal court judge pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 20021(c) after it determined that she falsified the residency information on her declaration of candidacy in violation of Cal. Elec. Code § 29303. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that plaintiff contestants did not have to wait for defendant to be convicted under § 29303 to challenge the election. The court held that, because § 29303 did not specify the standard of proof, under Cal. Evid. Code § 115, plaintiffs only had to show by a preponderance of the evidence that defendant committed an offense. The court held that the trial court's findings were supported by substantial evidence, including defendant's admission that she did not live at the address when she made application for appointment. Defendant's interests were not prejudiced by the trial court's issuance of a statement of decision before it entered judgment. The court held that the trial court's order calling for a new election was proper under Cal. Elec. Code § 6612, and because defendant's election had been adjudged a nullity, she had no justiciable interest as a candidate in the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment nullifying defendant candidate's election because plaintiff contestants proved by a preponderance of the evidence that she falsified her residency information on her declaration of candidacy in violation of the elections statute.

 

1351 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Swanson


Civ. No. 65529


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


137 Cal. App. 3d 416; 187 Cal. Rptr. 111; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2101



November 17, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-348519, Philip M. Newman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The second doctrine applies here. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiff declaratory relief against defendants, zoning board members, in an action for a zoning variance to construct a residence on plaintiff's property.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owner applied for a variance from the applicable zoning ordinance to build a residence on his property. Defendants, zoning board members, denied his application. Plaintiff brought a proceeding in administrative mandate to review the denial, which resulted in a judgement denying mandate. Plaintiff then instituted an action in declaratory relief, which the trial court denied on the ground that the denial of the mandate was res judicata. The court affirmed and held that it was proper for plaintiff seek a variance of the ordinance as it applied to his particular property before the zoning agency and its adverse decision was reviewable by administrative mandate and not otherwise. The court concluded that it was not proper for plaintiff to seek declaratory relief as that remedy was appropriate for attacking the overall constitutionality of the ordinance and could not be properly brought before the agency. Therefore, the denial of the mandate was res judicata and the denial of the declaratory relief was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants, zoning board members, in an action for a zoning ordinance variance because plaintiff property owner appropriately sought relief before the zoning board, the decision was properly reviewed by administrative mandate and denied, and the denial of mandate was res judicata on the issue.

 

1352 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Williamson


Crim. No. 11883


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


137 Cal. App. 3d 419; 187 Cal. Rptr. 107; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2102



November 17, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 10, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 75892, Lucian B. Vandegrift, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which denied his request for diversion after he pleaded guilty to cultivation of marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11358. Defendant obtained a certificate of probable cause and claimed that the state's prosecutor had improperly ventured into judicial territory.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was apprehended in a pickup truck containing 110 marijuana plants. At the preliminary hearing he contended that they were for his personal use, but the state's prosecutor declined to initiate diversion proceedings. The prosecutor determined that the marijuana was for distribution and claimed that he had the authority to so determine under Cal. Penal Code § 1000. Defendant contended that the prosecutor lacked the authority to determine that the amount cultivated was not for personal use under Cal. Penal Code § 1000. The trial court denied diversion, and defendant pleaded guilty. The court reversed and held that the prosecutor had exceeded his authority, and assumed a judiciary function when he made that legal determination. The court ruled that only the trial court had the authority to take evidence and find the operative facts under Cal. Penal Code § 1000.2 regarding the intended use of the marijuana. The court held that because defendant's plea of guilty followed upon the erroneous denial of diversion, it had to be set aside.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded defendant's conviction for the cultivation of marijuana for distribution because the state's prosecutor lacked the authority to determine in an accusatory pleading that defendant's possession was not for personal use. The court held that it was a judicial function to make that determination, and that it was upon this improper legal decision that defendant's eligibility for diversion was based.

 

1353 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Macias


Crim. No. 13362


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


137 Cal. App. 3d 465; 187 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2107



November 17, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 6, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 16, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR53102, Donald W. Smith, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), for attempted second-degree murder, possessing a knife in an honor camp, escape from an honor camp, and finding that defendant personally inflicted great bodily injury, and used a dangerous or deadly weapon while committing the attempted murder and escape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for attempted second-degree murder, Cal. Pen. Code §§ 187, 664, possessing a knife in an honor camp, Cal. Penal Code § 4574, escape from an honor camp, Cal. Penal Code § 4532(a), and findings he personally inflicted great bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7 and used a dangerous or deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b), while committing the attempted murder and escape. Defendant was sentenced to fourteen and one-third years comprised of an aggravated term of nine years for attempted murder, a three-year enhancement for inflicting great bodily injury, and two and one-third years consecutive for knife possession and escape. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and held that the consecutive sentences did not constitute improper multiple punishment because the escape and assault were committed independent of one another and had separate criminal intent. The court found that the attempted murder sentence did not violate equal protection of the law and was not cruel and unusual though the sentence was identical to that imposed for attempted first degree murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions in connection with an escape from an honor camp. Consecutive sentences for the escape and for an assault committed during the escape were proper because defendant had separate criminal intent. The attempted second-degree murder sentence did not violate equal protection of the law and was not cruel and unusual though the sentence was identical to that imposed for attempted first degree murder.

 

1354 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Erzinger v. Regents of University of California


Civ. No. 24408


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


137 Cal. App. 3d 389; 187 Cal. Rptr. 164; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2098



November 16, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 27, 1983. The United States Supreme Court denied a petition for writ of certiorari on June 20, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 408559, Franklin B. Orfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and which found that defendant state university's using mandatory student fees to provide student health services including abortion services did not infringe plaintiffs' rights to free exercise of religion and that defendant's regents had legal authority to assess and use mandatory student fees.


OVERVIEW: The lower court denied plaintiff students' motion for summary judgment and found that defendant state university's use of mandatory student fees to provide student health services including abortion services and pregnancy counseling did not infringe plaintiffs' rights to free exercise of religion and that defendant's regents had legal authority to assess and use mandatory student fees. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that plaintiffs' free exercise rights were not infringed because defendant did not coerce their religious beliefs or unreasonably interfere with their practice of religion. The fact that plaintiffs objected on religious grounds to some of the services defendant provided was not a constitutional basis upon which plaintiffs could refuse to pay a part of the fees. The court also held that under Cal. Const. art. IX, § 9(a), defendant's regents had the exclusive authority to assess and spend mandatory student fees. Finally, defendant did not violate 42 U.S.C.S. § 300a-7(d) because mandatory participation in a health program containing benefits for procedures which others might choose did not mean plaintiffs were involved in the performance of those procedures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment in favor of defendant state university. The court held that defendant's use of mandatory student fees to provide student health services including abortion services and pregnancy counseling did not infringe plaintiffs' rights to free exercise of religion, defendant's regents had authority to assess and use mandatory student fees, and plaintiffs were not required to be involved in the actual performance of abortions.

People v. Dotson


Crim. No. 20308.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


137 Cal. App. 3d 288; 186 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2088



November 9, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Dec. 9, 1982 (See 141 C.A.3d 336)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 67744, Richard C. Bartalini, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of judgment from Superior Court of Alameda County (California) which sentenced him to life imprisonment without possibility of parole upon conviction for first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §187 under special circumstances that murder occurred during robbery under Cal. Penal Code §190.2, subd. (a)(17)(i)) and burglary under Cal. Penal Code, §190.2, subd. (a)(17)(vii).


OVERVIEW: When the trial court sentenced appellant to life imprisonment without possibility of parole after a jury convicted him of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §187 under special circumstances that the murder occurred during a robbery, Cal. Penal Code §190.2, subd. (a)(17)(i)), and in the commission of a burglary, Cal. Penal Code, §190.2, subd. (a)(17)(vii), appellant sought review. The court remanded for sentencing. The jury found appellant guilty of burglary, Cal. Penal Code §459, and acquitted him of robbery, Cal. Penal Code §211. The jury also found appellant guilty of the murder of the victim. The record of judgment afforded no basis for determining whether the trial judge would have considered striking the special circumstances as to the robbery finding if he had been aware that he had discretionary power to do so. The trial court's error was not prejudicial as to the burglary special circumstances since the same jury which found that appellant committed a burglary and murder would also have found that the murder was committed while appellant was engaged in the commission of or the immediate flight after committing burglary under Cal. Penal Code §190.2, subd. (a)(17).


OUTCOME: The court remanded for sentencing because the record of judgment afforded no basis for determining whether the trial judge would have considered striking the special circumstances of appellant's acquitted robbery offense if the trial judge had been aware that he had discretionary power to do so.

 

1356 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Howie


Crim. No. 41210.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


137 Cal. App. 3d 258; 186 Cal. Rptr. 888; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2147



November 8, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Jan. 5, 1983, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 525897, Roy L. Norman, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which found him guilty of being a convicted felon in possession of firearm under Cal. Penal Code §12021 after the trial court denied collateral estoppel to another court's order and permitted relitigation of a prior conviction.


OVERVIEW: When appellant was found guilty of being a convicted felon in possession of firearm under Cal. Penal Code §12021 after the trial court denied collateral estoppel to another court's order and permitted relitigation of a prior conviction, appellant sought review. The court reversed and remanded. The trial court erred in permitting relitigation of appellant's prior conviction. Appellant was entitled to assert the ruling of the other court as a defense to the charge. A final ruling on a motion to strike adverse to a criminal defendant precluded relitigation of the issue in future proceedings. Considerations of fundamental and substantial justice subjected respondent people to the same bar. The determination of an issue on a basis other than jurisdictional or purely procedural grounds was one on the merits; a fortiori, a determination that appellant's prior conviction was unconstitutional necessarily reached the merits of the issue presented.


OUTCOME: The court reversed conviction and remanded because the trial court erred in permitting relitigation of appellant's prior conviction; appellant was entitled to assert the ruling of other court as a defense to the charge.

City of Watsonville v. Merrill


Civ. Nos. 51098, 52952


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


137 Cal. App. 3d 185; 186 Cal. Rptr. 857; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2139; 3 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2495



November 4, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 2, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, Nos. 73334 and 74507, Christopher C. Cottle and Roland K. Hall, Judges.



DISPOSITION: In view of our conclusion, the further issues raised by the parties need not be discussed.

In action number 1 Civil 51098/A011921, the order dismissing the action is reversed.

In action number 1 Civil 52952/A012122, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged judgments from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) sustaining respondent county auditor's demurrer in appellant's actions to release funds and declare valid city charter provisions authorizing additional property tax to finance employee retirement costs, and directing appellant to refund additional property tax.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city had a contract with the Public Employees Retirement System (PERS). Appellant directed respondent county auditor to levy and collect additional taxes to cover its PERS indebtedness. Respondent collected the funds, but refused to turn them over. Appellant sued for release of the funds and to declare valid city charter provisions authorizing the additional property tax to finance its PERS obligations. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed the suit. The trial court held the additional property tax to fund appellant's retirement costs was invalid and directed a refund. On appeal, the court reversed because the additional property tax fell within a Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 1(b) exception as a voter-approved indebtedness. The controlling time was when appellant joined the retirement system, which was before voters adopted Proposition 13, limiting state and local entities' taxing authority.


OUTCOME: The judgments dismissing appellant's action to release funds, declare valid city charter provisions allowing additional property tax to finance employee retirement costs, and directing appellant to refund additional property tax were reversed because the provisions were constitutional, and the tax was a voter-approved prior indebtedness in its entirety.

People v. Tucciarone


Crim. No. 13414


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


137 Cal. App. 3d 701; 187 Cal. Rptr. 159; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2160



November 2, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 52850, Larrie R. Brainard, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The conviction of count one, attempted murder with a knife causing great bodily injury (§§ 664/187, 12022, subd. (b), 12022.7) is reversed; in all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged convictions related to the stabbing of his wife, including attempted murder with a knife causing great bodily injury, in the Superior Court of San Diego (California), arguing that the trial court erred in refusing a jury instruction on attempted voluntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with several offenses after stabbing his wife, including attempted murder with a knife causing great bodily injury. At trial, expert testimony was presented regarding appellant's diminished capacity due to a brain dysfunction. The trial court refused to give a jury instruction on attempted involuntary manslaughter, despite counsel's argument that even if the jury found intent to kill, there was evidence of diminished capacity to allow the jury to conclude that the requisite malice was missing. The jury convicted appellant. On appeal, the court reversed the conviction of attempted murder with a knife causing great bodily injury and affirmed in all other respects, finding there was sufficient evidence for the jury to conclude that appellant acted without malice and, therefore, the trial court erred in refusing the instruction on attempted voluntary manslaughter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction of attempted murder with a knife causing great bodily injury and affirmed in all other respects, finding there was evidence for the jury to find appellant acted without malice and, thus, it was error not to instruct the jury on attempted voluntary manslaughter.

12319 Corp. v. Business License Com.


Civ. No. 63226


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


137 Cal. App. 3d 54; 186 Cal. Rptr. 726; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2124



October 28, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C329041, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nightclub owners challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus or prohibition or administrative mandamus after respondent municipal agencies revoked petitioners' business licenses.


OVERVIEW: Following multiple violations stemming from petitioner owners' operation of their nightclub, respondent commission revoked petitioners' business licenses. Respondent board affirmed respondent commission's decision. Although the trial court granted a stay of the operative effect of the revocation, it later dissolved the stay order and denied petitioners' request for a writ of mandamus, prohibition, or administrative mandamus. On appeal, petitioners claimed that the administrative and judicial proceedings were infected by political pressure, bias, and a denial of fairness. The court determined that the evidence was sufficient to support revocation. Petitioners had previously pleaded guilty for sales of alcoholic beverages to minors, and pleaded nolo contendere on a citation for overcrowding. Petitioners' due process rights were not violated by the municipal ordinances governing the revocation of their licenses. Petitioners failed to offer any facts supporting their assertion of bias and prejudice. Hence, under the totality of the circumstances, the revocation of petitioners' licenses met applicable standards. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment denying petitioner nightclub owners' request for a writ of mandamus, prohibition, or administrative mandamus after respondent municipal agencies revoked petitioners' business licenses was affirmed, because petitioners' due process rights were not violated and sufficient evidence supported the revocation.

 

1360 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bunker Hill Associates v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 65353


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


137 Cal. App. 3d 79; 186 Cal. Rptr. 719; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2126



October 28, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA000509, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commercial tenant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent city in appellant's class action challenging the tax imposed upon commercial tenants pursuant to Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.3.3, contending the tax was discriminatory as between tenants of the same class.


OVERVIEW: Appellant commercial tenant challenged the tenant tax imposed by Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 21.3.3, contending that it discriminated arbitrarily between tenants of the same class because it exempted tenants whose landlords paid local property tax but not appellant, whose landlord was exempt from local property tax as an insurance company pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XIII, §§ 27 and 28. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent city, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that the tax was not arbitrarily discriminatory in its application to appellant but, rather, was discriminatory with a rational basis. The court determined that, under respondent's tenant tax, appellant would pay the tenant tax but would not be charged any additional sum for its landlord's property tax through rent, and other tenants would pay their pro rata share of their landlords' property tax bill through rent but not the tenant tax. The court concluded the tax was not unconstitutionally imposed upon appellant's landlord because the landlord was charged only with the responsibility of collecting the tax and could not be charged therefore.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted summary judgment to respondent city in the action brought by appellant commercial tenant challenging the tax levied on commercial tenants whose landlords were not exempt from local property tax assessments, finding that the tax did not arbitrarily discriminate because it provided that tenants pay a pro rata share of their landlord's property tax or the tenant tax, but not both.

 

1361 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sims


Crim. No. 40314.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


136 Cal. App. 3d 942; 186 Cal. Rptr. 793; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2079



October 25, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 27, 1983 (Crim. 22952).
Apr. 24, 1986 Trans. to Ct. App., 2nd Dist., Div. 1, for reconsideration in light of People v. Garcia (1984) 36 Cal. 3d 539 and Carlos v. Superior Court (1983) 35 Cal. 3d 131.
Opn. filed November 5, 1986 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A194636, Edward A. Hinz, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code, §§ 187, 189 and robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and found that the murder was committed during the commission of a robbery in which defendant intentionally aided, abetted, and assisted the actual killer in the commission of murder in the first degree under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189 and robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211. Allegations that the murder was committed during the commission of a robbery in which defendant intentionally aided, abetted, and assisted the actual killer in the commission of murder in the first degree under Cal. Penal Code §§ 190.2(b) were found true. On appeal, the court reversed the special circumstances finding and directed the trial court to resentence on the murder count, concluding that § 190.2(b) was limited in application to those who intended the victim to be killed. The court found no evidence that defendant aided and abetted with the intention the victim be killed. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects because defendant failed to establish duress on compulsion absent a showing that his life was in present and immediate danger had he refused to participate in the crime and evidence of defendant's presence at the robbery, flight with the perpetrators, and request for a share of the proceeds was sufficient to show aiding and abetting.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the special circumstances finding against defendant and directed that he be resentenced on the murder count, concluding that the special circumstance that the murder was committed during a robbery in which defendant intentionally aided and abetted the actual killer in the murder could only be found against those who intended the victim to be killed. The judgment was affirmed in all other aspects.

People v. Smith


Crim. No. 13183.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


136 Cal. App. 3d 961; 186 Cal. Rptr. 650; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2080



October 25, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Nov. 19, 1982; Hg. granted Jan. 27, 1983 (See 187 Cal. App. 3d 666)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRN 6497, Charles W. Froehlich, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which convicted him of first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 209(b), and found that defendant was armed within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022(a) and had prior convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in a robbery and murder, but was not the triggerman. Defendant was sentenced to life following convictions for first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 209(b), with enhancements for being armed under Cal. Penal Code § 12022(a) and prior convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 667.5(b). The court affirmed. Admission of recorded statements was not ineffective assistance of counsel because there was no prejudice where the evidence would have been presented to the jury regardless. Instructional error was harmless as defendant failed to show prejudice. Defendant would not have obtained a better result given felony murder was subject to a minimum penalty of life imprisonment under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i). Jurors against the death penalty were not improperly excluded because they were not a cognizable group. The court remanded for the limited purpose of a hearing on the alleged prior convictions because defendant was not properly advised of his constitutional rights before admitting convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions finding no ineffective assistance of counsel and harmless instructional error where no prejudice was shown. Because defendant admitted his prior convictions without being properly advised of his rights, the court remanded the cause for the limited purpose of a hearing on the alleged prior convictions.

 

1363 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lewis-Westco & Co. v. Alcoholic Bev. etc. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 54605


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


136 Cal. App. 3d 829; 186 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2071; 1982 Trade Cas. (CCH) P65,027



October 22, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 2, 1982. Petitions for a rehearing were denied November 18, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of respondent and real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 3, 1983.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Board below is annulled. The petitions to intervene as real parties are dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensed rectifier of distilled spirits filed an application for an extraordinary writ that sought review of the decision of respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, which suspended its license for selling products at rates not filed with the department as required by Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24756.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner licensed rectifier of distilled spirits had his license suspended for selling its products at prices or quantity discounts at rates not contained in the schedules they were required to file in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24756. Respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board affirmed the suspension finding that it did not have the authority to declare the statute unconstitutional. The court reversed the suspension and held that § 24756 must be declared invalid as an illegal restraint of trade in violation of the Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C.S. §1 et seq., in the absence of state action exemption or constitutional protection. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. XXI, § 2 did not insulate § 24756 from Sherman Antitrust Act compliance. Thus, the court concluded that the consideration of relevant balancing factors compelled a determination that the Sherman Antitrust Act was paramount and that the price posting requirements were invalid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the suspension of the liquor license of petitioner licensed rectifier of distilled spirits, holding that the Sherman Antitrust Act controlled and that the price posting requirement was an illegal restraint of trade.

Phelps v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 65738


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


136 Cal. App. 3d 802; 186 Cal. Rptr. 626; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2068



October 21, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 5, 1983. Richardson, J., and Kaus, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent to vacate its order of February 26, 1982, in Los Angeles Superior Court case No. NEC 65738, entitled Eugene Phelps v. Able Auto Adjusters et al., and reconsider the special verdicts returned that date in conformity with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied its request for partial interlocutory judgment based upon 15 special verdicts in which the trial court declared a mistrial in favor of defendants, bank and auto repossessors, for injuries suffered by plaintiff when his car was repossessed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured party filed an action for personal injuries against defendants, bank, and auto repossessor on the theory of negligence and battery, and for conversion, based on injuries he suffered on repossession of his automobile. The jury returned 15 special verdicts that found defendants negligent and apportioned fault at 80 percent to defendants and 20 percent to plaintiff. Upon poll of the jurors there was discrepancies and the trial court declared a mistrial. Plaintiff filed for a partial interlocutory judgment, which was denied by the trial court. The trial court declined to reconsider the special verdict in light of a recent court decision. The court reversed the trial court and held that the decision in another court case would be given retroactive application in the context of special verdicts on comparative negligence issues. The court let the peremptory writ of mandate issue and ordered the trial court to reconsider the special verdicts. The court held on retrial a determination should be made on what portion of the total general damages are subject to apportionment of fault and what portion was not.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of a mistrial in favor of defendants, bank and auto repossessors, because of discrepancies upon polling of jurors, holding that a recent court decision that applied to this case was be given retroactive effect in the context of special verdicts on comparative negligence issues. The court let the peremptory writ issue and ordered the trial court to reconsider the special verdicts.

People v. Marsh


Crim. No. 38687.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


136 Cal. App. 3d 634; 186 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2048



October 19, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 19, 1983 (See 36 C.3d 134)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CR-15584, William H. Rosenthal, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that convicted him, following his nolo contendere plea pursuant to plea bargaining, of kidnapping for ransom with bodily harm, eight counts of forcible rape, three counts of oral copulation, two counts of sodomy, two counts of robbery, three counts of assault, and the use of a deadly weapon in the commission or all the foregoing crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of kidnapping for ransom with bodily harm, using a deadly weapon in the commission of the subject crimes, and multiple counts of forcible rape, and that he acted in concert, oral copulation, sodomy, robbery, and assault with a deadly weapon by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, all committed when he was a minor. Defendant appealed the denial of his motion to strike the allegation of ransom and bodily harm to reduce the kidnapping to simple kidnapping, and render him eligible for commitment to the youth authority. The court affirmed the convictions as they stood, and held that although Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2 appeared to require an evaluation and report as to a minor's suitability for commitment to the youth authority, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1731.5 only allowed a court to commit anyone less than 21 years at the time of apprehension to the youth authority if he was not sentenced to life imprisonment and was not granted probation. Since defendant was convicted of kidnapping for ransom with bodily harm punishable by imprisonment without possibility of parole, he was statutorily ineligible for commitment to the youth authority.


OUTCOME: Defendant's judgment of conviction for kidnapping, rape, robbery and use of a deadly weapon was affirmed, because the court rejected defendant's contention that the trial court abused its discretion in denying him the opportunity to establish eligibility for training and treatment at the youth authority, and held that the trial court would have abused its discretion if it had granted defendant the opportunity for such eligibility.

In re Gary U.


Civ. No. 24249


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


136 Cal. App. 3d 494; 186 Cal. Rptr. 316; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2033



October 12, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. AD-21776, James F. Judge, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted defendant ex-wife's petition to free her minor son from defendant's parental custody and control and to appoint her as her son's guardian, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232. Defendant claimed he was denied an opportunity to attend the § 232 hearings due to being incarcerated out of state.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was incarcerated out of state when his ex-wife's petition to terminate defendant's parental custody and control over their minor son was granted, which appointed his ex-wife as their son's guardian pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232. Defendant appealed, claiming he was not given opportunity to attend the § 232 hearings due to his out of state incarceration during such. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, holding that defendant was not prevented from attending the § 232 hearings. The court held that the lower court granted several continuances to defendant and it was clear that nothing he or his attorney could do would remove him from prison to attend the § 232 hearings. The court further held that had no right to remove his counsel pursuant to a claimed right of self-representation that he could not possibly provide. The court held that it was in the best interests of the minor child to allow the § 232 hearings to be conducted absent the presence of defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that granted defendant ex-wife's petition to free her minor son from defendant's parental custody and control and appointed her as her son's guardian because defendant was not prevented from being present during such hearings despite his incarceration out of state.

People v. Fuller


Crim. No. 22634


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


136 Cal. App. 3d 403; 186 Cal. Rptr. 283; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2026



October 8, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 5, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Elkington, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 1, 1982. Newman, J., and Broussard, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 71262, John P. Vukasin, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of assault with intent to commit rape while on parole from a previous conviction of rape, and denied defendant's motion for a mistrial in which he claimed that the prosecutor improperly used peremptory challenges in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 16 and U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's judgment convicting him of assault with intent to commit rape while on parole in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 220, 1203.085, 667.5(b), claiming, that the jury selection process was invalid. The court reversed the trial court's judgment of conviction and remanded for a new trial, holding that the prosecution used peremptory challenges to remove three black perspective jurors on the sole ground of group bias in violation of the right to trial by a jury drawn from a representative cross-section of the community under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16 and U.S. Const. amend. VI. There was no independent ground for the challenges, and defendant made a prima facie case. Defendant objected promptly and made the best record possible. He showed that the excluded jurors were of a cognizable group, and he established a reasonable inference of group bias rather than specific bias. The court held that the trial court erred in not requiring the prosecutor to respond to the allegation of group bias and in denying defendant's motion for mistrial without a rebuttal showing by the prosecutor that each challenge was predicated on grounds of specific bias.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for a new trial the conviction of assault with the intent to commit rape. The court found that defendant made a prima facie showing that the prosecutor improperly used peremptory challenges to remove black jurors on the sole ground of group bias, in violation of the right to a trial by a jury drawn from a representative cross-section of the community under the California Constitution and the Sixth Amendment.

People v. Chambers


Crim. No. 21484


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


136 Cal. App. 3d 444; 186 Cal. Rptr. 306; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2028



October 8, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 5, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 22, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-8488, Walter P. Capaccioli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The case is remanded to the trial court for exercise of the court's discretion to determine whether or not there is a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances, and for further sentencing proceedings if and as required after the ruling on that issue. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder during commission of rape with a deadly weapon and commission of credit card fraud. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole for first degree murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 189, 190.2, and defendant's sentence on the remaining counts was suspended.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of first degree murder and rape. The jury found true the allegations of special circumstances that defendant murdered his victim during the commission or attempted commission of rape, and that he used a deadly weapon to commit the murder. Defendant received life imprisonment without possibility of parole. He appealed, alleging that admission of the victim's statement that she planned to meet with defendant on the evening of her death was prejudicial hearsay, and that the trial court should have instructed on lesser-included offenses. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions, finding that the disputed evidence was cumulative of defendant's own testimony and thus harmless. The court also agreed with the trial court that the evidence as a whole was such that a reasonable trier of fact could find appellant guilty of rape beyond a reasonable doubt, and that there was no error in the jury instruction. However, the court remanded to the trial court for a determination of whether the finding of special circumstances should be dismissed in order to render defendant eligible for parole, under Cal. Penal Code § 1385, enacted after defendant's trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions but remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings to determine if there was a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances, in deference to the law enacted after defendant's trial that permitted a trial court to dismiss the finding of special circumstances in order to impose a sentence that allowed for the possibility of parole.

 

1369 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


KSDO v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 27868


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


136 Cal. App. 3d 375; 186 Cal. Rptr. 211; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2024; 8 Media L. Rep. 2360



October 7, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 19, 1982.



DISPOSITION: We hold therefore that the qualified privilege under the First Amendment as above stated protects Brown and KSDO from having to reveal Brown's notes. The order compelling discovery of the notes is improper. A writ of mandate shall issue directing the trial court to annul its order requiring defendants to produce Brown's notes.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a radio station and news reporter, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which ordered defendants to disclose unpublished notes and memos of defendant news reporter which contained information from his source, in a libel suit filed by plaintiffs, members of a police department.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, members of a police department, filed a complaint for libel alleging that defendants, a radio station and news reporter, broadcast a false report. Defendant news reporter in his deposition revealed the sources of his information which led to the news report, and plaintiff brought a motion to produce documents, seeking his notes and memoranda of conversations with his source. The trial court granted plaintiffs' motion. The court reversed the trial court's order and held that plaintiffs failed to show that the information they sought from defendant was unavailable from any other source or that the materials desired actually went to the heart of the claim. The court held that the sources were revealed so that plaintiffs were free to test the reliability of those sources, and there was no showing that it was necessary to have defendant news reporter's notes to do so. Thus, the court held that the qualified privilege under U.S. Const. amend. I protected defendants from having to reveal the notes. The court held that Cal. Evid. Code § 1070 and Cal. Const. art. 1, §2 only provided immunity from contempt, not a privilege against disclosure.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and held that the qualified privilege under the First Amendment protected defendants, a radio station and news reporter, from having to reveal defendant news reporter's notes from his source in a libel action by plaintiffs, members of a police department, because defendant news reporter disclosed his source, and plaintiffs were therefore able to test the reliability of the information without the notes.

American River Flood Control Dist. v. Sayre


Civ. Nos. 20959, 20966


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


136 Cal. App. 3d 347; 186 Cal. Rptr. 202; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2020



October 6, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A writ of mandate will issue directing the board of supervisors to levy and collect the special assessments sought by the petitioners. The assessments shall be levied pursuant to the provisions of Water Code section 12878.35, or as provided in section 12878.40, and pursuant to the Act, as amended September 7, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state agencies filed for writs of mandates to direct respondent board of supervisors (California) to levy and collect ad valorem special assessments on behalf of petitioners pursuant to the American River Flood Control District Act, and Cal. Water Code §§ 12878-12878.45.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state agencies provided for the control and disposition of storm and flood waters, and maintained flood control projects pursuant to the American River Flood Control District Act (Act) and Cal. Water Code §§ 12878-12878.45. Petitioners filed for writs of mandate to direct respondent board of supervisors to collect ad valorem special assessments used by petitioners for maintenance costs, and the court granted the writs. The court determined that the Act and the state statute authorized petitioners to levy the special assessments, and required respondent to collect those assessments. The court concluded that respondent should have collected the assessments based on the ad valorem tax roll, because collection of special assessments based on the present acquisition value rolls was not valid. The court further held that the adoption of Cal. Const. art. XIII A did nothing to change respondent's obligation under the statutes to collect the assessments on behalf of petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court issued writs of mandate for petitioner state agencies, and directed respondent board of supervisors to collect the requested assessments because the statutes authorized petitioners to levy the assessments, and required respondent to collect the assessments based on the ad valorem tax roll.

 

1371 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Katrinak


Crim. No. 42420


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


136 Cal. App. 3d 145; 185 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2000



September 27, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1982. Newman, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Whittier Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Nos. M 140946 and M 141071, Larry S. Knupp, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Municipal Court for the Whittier Judicial District of Los Angeles County, (California), which entered adverse rulings relating to the constitutionality of Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance No. 5860, which related to licensing of escort bureaus. The trial court dismissed the case after making the adverse ruling.


OVERVIEW: The court was asked to consider whether the operation of an escort bureau was constitutionally protected as a proper exercise of the freedom of speech. The court concluded that such activity was not constitutionally protected, and that Los Angeles County, Cal., Ordinance No. 5860 did not offend either the free speech or due process guarantees. The court also determined that the ordinance was a valid exercise of police power, and reversed the lower court's ruling that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court determined that the sale of companionship did not occupy the same position as the sort of personal intimacy protected under the concept of a right to privacy. The county ordinance requiring escort bureaus to be licensed was a valid exercise of the county's police power, and was not unconstitutional. The court also reversed the lower court's dismissal order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of dismissal, holding that the ordinance that regulated escort bureaus did not offend either the free speech or due process guarantees of the state and federal constitutions, and was a valid exercise of police power.

 

1372 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Logan v. Southern Cal. Rapid Transit Dist.


Civ. No. 61989


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


136 Cal. App. 3d 116; 185 Cal. Rptr. 878; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1998



September 27, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 295709, Jerry K. Fields, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The demurrers were properly sustained and the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bus driver sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, (California), which dismissed appellant's action that challenged his discharge after appellant was involved in a dispute with an intoxicated female passenger. The superior court sustained with leave to amend the demurrer filed by respondents, district and union.


OVERVIEW: Appellant bus driver was involved in an altercation with an intoxicated female passenger and had a hearing to review his discharge at which his termination was affirmed. Based on his belief that there was a denial of due process, he filed tort and contract claims. At trial, his complaint was dismissed, and on appeal, the court held that the bare allegation in his complaint that he had exhausted his administrative remedies did not relieve him of the burden of doing so. Further, appellant's actions for damages against respondent district failed because he did not apply for a judicial review of the decision and findings of the administrative agency. Appellant, as a public employee, had a fundamental vested property right in his continued employment, but he failed to plead sufficient facts to show he was terminated without due process. Hence, he failed to state a cause of action for breach of duty of fair representation. Thus, the demurrers were properly sustained, and the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that sustained the demurrers to appellant bus driver's wrongful discharge action. Appellant's failure to plead sufficient facts to show that he was terminated without due process necessitated the ruling that he had failed to state a cause of action for breach of the duty of fair representation.

San Leandro Rock Co. v. City of San Leandro


Civ. No. 42280


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


136 Cal. App. 3d 25; 185 Cal. Rptr. 829; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1989



September 22, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, 447851-1, Charles Zook F. Sutton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Our conclusion supports the trial court's injunction, but only as the ordinance affects this plaintiff. The injunction will be modified to permit enforcement of the ordinance generally. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), that permanently enjoined appellant from enforcement of an ordinance that imposed weight access restrictions on use of a designated road on a finding that appellant's unconstitutional exercise of the police power rendered respondent quarry operator's property valueless as though it were confiscated.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted an ordinance that effectively banned trucks over four and one-half tons from using certain streets within the city, that included a road used by respondent quarry operator for over 60 years. Respondent brought suit that challenged the ordinance and sought injunctive relief on the ground that use of the road at issue was essential to the operation of its business because there was no safe or economically reasonable alternate route. The trial court made findings in accordance with respondent's contentions and issued a permanent injunction against appellant's enforcement of the ordinance. On appeal, the court supported the trial court's injunction, but only as the ordinance affected respondent. It was directed that the injunction be modified to permit enforcement of the ordinance generally. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed. The court found that the evidence was sufficient to support the trial court's finding of no feasible alternate route, and findings on the impact of the ordinance on respondent, but the trial court erred when it substituted its judgment for that of appellant's city council concerning the general wisdom of the regulation.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed as modified to permit enforcement of a road weight restriction ordinance generally but not against respondent quarry operator. The injunction was supported by the evidence as to the lack of a feasible alternate road and the concurrent affect on respondent, but the court found it was error for the trial court to usurp appellant's city counsel's regulatory authority as it applied to general application of the ordinance.

 

1374 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Willis)


Civ. No. 22139.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


135 Cal. App. 3d 1052; 185 Cal. Rptr. 822; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1984



September 21, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Jan. 19, 1983, Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to vacate its order setting bail and to redetermine defendant's eligibility for bail in accordance with Cal. Const. art. I, § 12.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion for bail or release on his own recognizance. On the ground that defendant threatened a witness and a police officer, the people wanted bail denied under Cal. Const. art. I, § 12, as amended. Respondent trial court ruled that retroactive application of the bail provisions of art. I, § 12 as amended, to defendant, whose alleged crimes occurred prior to the provisions' effective date, violated the prohibition in U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 3, of ex post facto legislation. Respondent granted bail and the people sought a writ of mandate. The court held that retroactive application of the bail provisions did not violate the prohibition of ex post facto legislation. The court concluded that although the provisions altered defendant's situation to his disadvantage, they did not have to do with a vested and substantial right possessed by defendant at the time of his offenses. Thus, the court issued a writ of mandate directing respondent to vacate its order setting bail and to redetermine defendant's eligibility for bail under the provisions.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its order setting bail and to redetermine defendant's eligibility for bail in accordance with the amended bail provisions of the California Constitution. The court held that the amended bail provisions did not violate the prohibition against ex post facto legislation.

In re Holmes


Civ. No. 65082.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


135 Cal. App. 3d 1024; 185 Cal. Rptr. 795; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1981



September 21, 1982  
September 21, 1982


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Oct. 21, 1982 (See 145 C.A.3d 934)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus after respondent trial court found her in contempt for assisting her husband to avoid service of process.


OVERVIEW: After a hearing by respondent trial court, petitioner attorney was found in contempt for assisting her husband in his attempts to avoid service of a subpoena. Petitioner sought review of the contempt proceedings by way of a petition for a writ of habeas corpus or, in the alternative, for a writ of certiorari. Petitioner questioned whether a person who knowingly acted to assist another person in evading service of process was acting unlawfully, and, therefore, in a manner that was within the acts or omissions constituting contempt set forth in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1209(8). The court held that writ proceedings were appropriate vehicles for review of contempt orders because contempt judgments were final, conclusive, and not appealable under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1222, 904.1. The court held that advising a person to conceal himself for the purpose of avoiding service of a subpoena violated Cal. Penal Code § 136.1 and, therefore, was unlawful. The court sustained the judgment of contempt, denied the petition for a writ of habeas corpus, and discharged the order to show cause.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the judgment of contempt entered by respondent trial court and denied petitioner attorney's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The court held that petitioner had actual knowledge that process servers were trying to serve a subpoena on her husband, and that she assisted an attempt to avoid service of a subpoena. The court held that petitioner's conduct was an unlawful interference with the process or proceedings of a court.

 

1376 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


KQED, Inc. v. Hall


Civ. No. 49429


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


135 Cal. App. 3d 951; 185 Cal. Rptr. 630; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1973; 37 A.L.R.4th 1199



September 17, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 13, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 763438, Leland J. Lazarus, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant membership chairman sought review of an order from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which set aside appellant's demand for inspection of the membership list of respondent nonprofit corporation under Cal. Corp. Code § 6330 and adopted an alternative procedure proposed by respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant membership chairman demanded inspection of the membership list of respondent public benefit nonprofit corporation pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 6330. Respondent denied the demand, fearing appellant would use the information on the list to garner financial support to oppose respondent and would fail to indicate that appellant was not affiliated with respondent. Respondent proposed an alternative procedure under Cal. Corp. Code § 6331(g), which required appellant to submit to respondent any proposed communications to members for prior review and approval by respondent. When appellant rejected that proposal respondent brought an action pursuant to Cal. Corp. Code § 6331(a) to set aside appellant's demand. The trial court set aside appellant's demand and adopted the alternative procedure proposed by respondent and appellant sought review. The court reversed, finding that the inspection statutes provided a method by which members of nonprofit corporations could communicate with each other, and the statutory scheme devised to regulate that communication did not contemplate censorship or prior restraint.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order setting aside appellant membership chairman's demand for inspection of the membership list of respondent nonprofit corporation and adopting an alternative procedure proposed by respondent, because respondent's prior review of materials to be distributed by appellant to members of the corporation was not contemplated by the statutes.

 

1377 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clark


Crim. No. 5413.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


135 Cal. App. 3d 921; 185 Cal. Rptr. 596; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1970



September 16, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. den. Oct. 13, 1982, and opn. ordered by the Ct. of App., 5th Dist. not to be published in the Official Reports. Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 20075, Nathaniel O. Bradley, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for fraudulent presentation of checks with insufficient funds, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 476a(a), entered by the Superior Court of Tulare County (California).


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for fraudulent presentation of checks with insufficient funds under Cal. Penal Code § 476a(a), the people sought to introduce evidence obtained by a police officer from defendant's bank which proved that defendant's account had been closed before he presented the checks. Defendant's motion to suppress the evidence was denied and he was convicted. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence should have been suppressed because it was obtained in violation of Cal. Gov't Code § 7480(b). The court found that § 7480(b) required the law enforcement officer, who sought to obtain the banks records, to do so under penalty of perjury. The court stated that because the law enforcement officer did not accompany his request for information with a certified statement that a police report had been filed, § 7480(b) was violated. The court held that although the evidence was inadmissible, any error was harmless because it was not reasonably probable that a more favorable result to defendant would have been reached in the absence of the error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for fraudulent presentation of checks with insufficient funds. The court found that the trial court erred in admitting evidence obtained from the bank regarding the status of defendant's account because the officer obtained the information without certifying under penalty of perjury that a crime report had been filed. Any error in the admission, however, was harmless.

 

1378 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Martin


Crim. No. 38923


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


135 Cal. App. 3d 710; 185 Cal. Rptr. 556; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1948



September 14, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied October 13, 1982, and appellants' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied November 10, 1982. Newman, J., and Kaus, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A346780, Gordon Ringer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a judge and an attorney, appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), convicting them of conspiracy to obstruct justice in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 182(a)(5). Defendants claimed that the charging statute was void for vagueness, that there was insufficient evidence to establish guilt, that a post-indictment preliminary hearing was wrongly denied, and that a prison sentence was improper.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted with conspiracy to obstruct justice based upon their collusion to improperly dispose of misdemeanor cases in which individuals were charged with driving under the influence of alcohol and/or drugs. Defendants appealed. Defendants claimed that the charging statute, Cal. Penal Code § 182(a)(5), was void for vagueness, there was an insufficiency of the evidence, that post-indictment preliminary hearing was wrongly denied, and that a prison sentence was improper. The appellate court affirmed. No reference to the code section regarding crimes against public justice was necessary to determine whether defendants committed the offense. Thus, the charging statute was not vague. Because defendants had an intent to do the acts constituting the offense's elements, intent was proven. No evil or corrupt motive was required. Defendants were not entitled to a preliminary hearing under the law as it existed at the time of the proceedings. Thus, the court did not abuse its discretion in denying a continuance to allow defendants to take advantage of a prospective law allowing such a hearing. Sentencing was proper where it did not exceed statutory limits.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's entry of conspiracy to obstruct justice convictions against defendant attorney and defendant judge. Because defendants intended to do the acts composing the offense, that is, to improperly dispose of misdemeanor cases, no showing of an evil or corrupt motive was required. The charging statute was not void for vagueness where it clearly defined the prohibited conduct.

 

1379 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Smith


Civ. No. 48380


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


135 Cal. App. 3d 543; 185 Cal. Rptr. 411; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1927



August 31, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 392875, Eugene M. Premo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order from which Sandra appealed is affirmed. Each party shall bear his or her own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted respondent husband's motion to quash service of summons, and set aside default, interlocutory, and final judgments of dissolution of marriage.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife served summons and petition for divorce on respondent husband. Appellant's mother executed the return of service filed with the trial court. Default judgment was entered against respondent. Subsequently, appellant properly served notice of motion to increase child support on respondent. The trial court granted respondent's motion to quash service of summons and set aside default, interlocutory, and final judgments of dissolution. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that respondent's general appearance at the motion setting did not retroactively make the defective service of summons valid. California's Jurisdiction and Service of Process Act of 1969, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 410.10-418.10, rejected the rule that a defendant's general appearance after entry of a default judgment against him based upon a void service of summons retroactively makes valid that void service and § 410.10 did not provide statutory authority for retroactive waiver. The court concluded that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473.5 did not govern a default or default judgment obtained through a fraudulent return of service of summons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting respondent husband's motion to quash service of summons, and setting aside default, interlocutory, and final judgments of dissolution of marrige. The court held that respondent's general appearance did not make defective service valid.

People v. Perez


Crim. No. 38480.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


135 Cal. App. 3d 406; 185 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1917



August 26, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 21, 1982 (Crim. 22820)
Nov. 26, 1984, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 4, for reconsideration in light of Carlos v. Superior Court (1983) 35 Cal.3d 131 and People v. Garcia (1984) 36 Cal. 3d 539.
Opn. filed Jan. 24, 1985, not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 587995, Ricardo A. Torres, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered a judgment of conviction for first-degree murder, two counts of robbery, and assault with great bodily injury and with a deadly weapon. The jury further found true a special circumstance allegation for felony murder, as well as the use of a firearm for all four counts.


OVERVIEW: An off-duty police officer attempted to stop defendant and accomplice as they were leaving a store they had just robbed. Several shots were fired by the officer and defendant resulting in the injury of the officer and the death of the officer's daughter who was with her father. A jailhouse informant testified that defendant told him that defendant shot the girl to divert the attention of her father; however no eyewitness saw who shot the girl. Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder, two counts of robbery, and assault causing great bodily injury with a deadly weapon. The jury found true use of firearm allegations for all counts, and also found true a special circumstance allegation for felony-murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i). The court affirmed all of the convictions and the use of firearm allegations. The court reversed for retrial of the special circumstance allegation because the trial court failed to instruct the jury that they were required to find that defendant or accomplice had committed the murder in order to find true the special circumstance. The court found no error in the jury instructions given for premeditation, felony-murder, and flight.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first-degree murder, robbery, and assault with great bodily injury and with a deadly weapon and affirmed the use of firearm allegations. The court reversed for retrial on the special circumstance allegation for felony murder because the trial court failed to instruct the jury that the allegation could not be invoked unless the jury first found that defendant or accomplice fired the fatal bullet.

Gates v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 54155


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


135 Cal. App. 3d 309; 185 Cal. Rptr. 330; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1907



August 25, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 478428, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which overruled her demurrer to the complaint in her prosecution for loitering for the purpose of soliciting an act of prostitution pursuant to San Jose Municipal Code § 10.40.010.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested for loitering for the purpose of soliciting an act of prostitution pursuant to San Jose Municipal Code § 10.40.010. Appellant demurred to the complaint on the grounds that the ordinance was preempted by state law and unconstitutional. The court first found that because the issue of the constitutionality of the ordinance was before the court as the result of a challenge to the ordinance by way of demurrer to the complaint, the court would not dismiss appellant's petition for lack of standing because there was no record before the court upon which to determine standing. The court then reversed the denial of demurrer and found that although the ordinance covered both loitering and solicitation, it required an intent to violate state laws governing sexual conduct and was intended primarily to regulate sexual activity. The ordinance was therefore invalid because state criminal law preempted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court denying appellant's demurrer to the complaint in appellant' prosecution pursuant to a city ordinance for loitering for the purpose of soliciting an act of prostitution because state law on criminal sexual activities preempted the city ordinance.
