California State Employees' Assn. v. Way


Civ. No. 20977


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


135 Cal. App. 3d 1059; 185 Cal. Rptr. 747; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1985



August 25, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 287893, William A. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee association and related individual sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent agency employer's motion for summary judgment in a case concerning propriety of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15, § 4038, which prohibited agency employers parole officers from carrying, weapons.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee association and related individual filed a mandamus petition and complaint for declaratory judgment, challenging respondent agency employer's regulation, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 15, § 4038, which prohibited parole officers employed by respondent from carrying firearms while on duty. The trial court granted respondent agency employer's motion for summary judgment. Finally, the challenged regulation might have been the result of a crystallized point of view but such bias, if it existed, was not a basis for overturning the regulation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which granted respondent agency employer's motion for summary judgment denying appellant employee association and related individual the injunctive and declaratory relief they sought concerning a work regulation prohibiting carrying firearms, because the regulation was within respondent's statutory and legislative authority, and was not arbitrary, capricious, or unfair.

 

1383 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Yates


Crim. No. 22997.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


135 Cal. App. 3d 210; 185 Cal. Rptr. 184; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1897



August 19, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 21, 1982 (See 34 C.3d 644)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 24291, Robert J. Cooney, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from a judgment of imprisonment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) that was rendered after appellant was convicted of murder in the first degree, burglary, and robbery, and which imposed a 25 years to life term under Cal. Penal Code § 190.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged and convicted of first-degree murder, burglary, and robbery. Appellant was sentenced to 25 years to life under Cal. Penal Code § 190. Prior to jury selection defense counsel asked the trial court to allow appellant 26 peremptory challenges. The trial court denied the request holding that the defense was entitled to only 10 peremptories. After exercising 10 peremptory challenges defense counsel requested but was again denied additional challenges. Appellant challenged the judgment of imprisonment arguing that under the sentencing statute for first-degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 190, he was entitled to 26 peremptory challenges. The court reversed the judgment of imprisonment holding that, a term of 25 years to life imposed in the absence of special circumstances under § 190 should be construed as life imprisonment for purposes of allowing peremptory challenges. Accordingly, appellant was entitled to 26 peremptory challenges of prospective jurors and the failure of the trial court to grant appellant the additional challenges constituted reversible error.


OUTCOME: The judgment of imprisonment imposing on appellant a 25 years to life term upon being convicted of first-degree murder, burglary, and robbery was reversed. For purposes of eligibility for peremptory challenges an indeterminate sentence of 25 years to life constitutes a life term. Hence, appellant was entitled to 26 rather than 10 peremptory challenges and the trial court's denial to grant the additional challenges was reversible error.

 

1384 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Call v. Kezirian


Civ. No. 63730


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


135 Cal. App. 3d 189; 185 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1894



August 18, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 3, 1982, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 28, 1982. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-302302, Eli Chernow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is reversed with directions to allow plaintiff, if so advised, to amend the complaint in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother, acting as guardian ad litem for a child born with chromosomal anomalies, appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's medical malpractice action against defendant physician, after the trial court sustained without leave to amend, defendant's demurrer based upon the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mother, acting as guardian ad litem for a child born with chromosomal anomalies, brought a medical malpractice action against defendant physician, alleging defendant failed to advise her of the child's potential birth defects. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer on the ground that the statute of limitations had run and because no cause of action existed for failure to advise of potential birth defects and for failure to recommend a eugenic abortion. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the statute of limitations did not begin to run merely because plaintiff had knowledge that she gave birth to a Down's Syndrome baby, because an intelligent person, without specialized medical knowledge, would not necessarily know that Down's Syndrome could be predicted by performing amniocentesis. Thus, the statute of limitations did not begin to run until plaintiff became aware that tests could have been performed, which would have predicted the Downs condition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing plaintiff mother's complaint, and remanded with directions to allow plaintiff to amend the complaint because the complaint alleged facts which, if accepted by a trial jury, would serve to toll any applicable statute of limitations, and the demurrer should not have been granted on the basis that the statute of limitations had run.

 

1385 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pendleton


Crim. No. 39472.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


135 Cal. App. 3d 41; 185 Cal. Rptr. 88; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1878



August 16, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 18, 1982 (See 166 Cal. App.496)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A014705VP, A020561, and A610312VP, Pat Mullendore, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of first degree murder with special circumstances and challenged the jury cross-section and the felony-murder rule and its application.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed the judgment of the trial court but did not challenge the evidence or deny their guilt. Defendants challenged their jury on the basis that it was made up of only registered voters and they challenged the foundation and legality of the felony murder rule. On review, the court affirmed the judgments of the trial court. The court stated that the use of voter registration lists to draw a pool of jurors was a well-accepted method of obtaining a fair cross-section of the community. The court then turned to the felony murder rule and found that the rule encompassed sound public policy by deterring serious violent crimes. Malice was not presumed in the felony murder rule, but malice also did not require any deliberate intent to take human life. Thus, the court found that the culpable state of mind of the underlying felony was sufficient to raise even an accidental killing to the level of murder, and that there was nothing disproportionate or arbitrary in the legislature making a decision that a killing during the perpetration of a felony was murder to be punished accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court because the use of voter registration lists to select a jury pool was a proper method to select a fair cross-section of the community and the felony murder rule was constitutional and expressed a sound public policy aimed at preventing the commission of dangerous and violent crimes.

 

1386 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Box


Crim. Nos. 5456, 6239.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH Dist.


134 Cal. App. 3d 841; 184 Cal. Rptr. 843; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1854



August 6, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted in Crim. 5456, Oct. 21, 1982 (See 152 C.A.3d 461); Hg. in Crim. 6239 is stricken without prejudice to file a petition for writ of habeas corpus.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 11543, Wickson R. Woolpert, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which limited appellant to 10 peremptory challenges and convicted him of second-degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, and use of a firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5.


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant challenged his conviction for second-degree murder and us of a firearm, based upon the trial court's refusal to allow him 26 peremptory challenges, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1070. The court affirmed appellant's conviction, and held that he was limited to the 10 challenges that were permitted by the trial court, because appellant was charged with murder without special circumstances, and § 1070 based the number of peremptory challenges to which a defendant was entitled upon the term of punishment, rather than upon the type of crime committed. The court ruled that 26 peremptory challenges were not applicable to appellant's crime, because punishment could have been less than life imprisonment. Offenses punishable by life imprisonment under § 1070 were the only crimes for which the statute prescribed the term of life. In addition, the court determined that appellant's limit of 10 peremptory challenges did not violate equal protection, because it was not necessary that persons convicted of different crimes be treated equally.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal appellant's conviction, and held that appellant was only entitled to 10 peremptory challenges, because appellant was entitled to the number of peremptory challenges based upon the term of his punishment, and the punishment for his offenses may be fixed at less than a life term.

1388 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Scuri v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 63411


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


134 Cal. App. 3d 400; 185 Cal. Rptr. 18; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1781



July 28, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 29, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 72712, Charles R. McGrath, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) that denied their petition for writ of mandate to set aside a decision of respondents Ventura County, California, Board of Supervisors to annex certain unincorporated areas to two cities in the county. Appellants claimed Cal. Gov't Code § 35150(f) denied them equal protection and that respondent violated the municipal organization act.


OVERVIEW: This case involved the annexation of four "islands" of substantially surrounded unincorporated land, each not more than 100 acres in size, without election as permitted by Cal. Gov't Code § 35150(f). Appellants' constitutional attack failed. The discrimination based on the size of the land to be annexed was at least as rational as a law, previously declared constitutional, that discriminated based on the number of registered voters in an area. Furthermore, § 35150(f) was not subject to strict scrutiny. Although it deprived appellants of the opportunity to vote against annexation, it was well settled that the state could create, expand, diminish, or totally abolish municipal corporations with or without the consent of its citizens, or even against their protest. Thus no fundamental right was implicated. The annexation decision comported with § 35150(f). The "islands" were substantially surrounded by the cities to which they were annexed. The fact that annexation of "prime agricultural land" adjacent to the parcels proceeded first did not mean that the parcels were not eligible for annexation until after the period for which this procedure was authorized expired.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied appellants a writ of mandate. The court held that the procedure for annexing, without election, small islands of unincorporated land to the cities that substantially surrounded them was constitutional. The court also held that the decisions to annex the land were taken in accordance with the statute authorizing the procedure.

Ortega v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 21021, 21029


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


135 Cal. App. 3d 244; 185 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1900



July 27, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' applications for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied October 13, 1982.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners faced trial on charges in Superior Court of San Joaquin County, (California) of robbery and conspiracy to murder and allegations of special circumstances of torture under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(18), atrociousness under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(14), and others, and sought a writ of prohibition alleging they had been improperly held and that unauthorized persons were permitted to attend the preliminary examination.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners faced trial on charges of robbery, murder and allegations of special circumstances of torture under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(18), atrociousness under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(14), and lying in wait under Cal. Penal Code, § 190.2(a)(15). Petitioners contend they were being held improperly under Cal. Penal Code § 995 because unauthorized persons were permitted to attend the preliminary examination. Petitioners claim that it was impermissible to allow a juvenile witness to be accompanied by her mother and mother's interpreter. The court held that the term "prosecuting witness" should be given broad meaning as it will help obtain relevant and material testimony. Petitioners' second joint contention is the evidence adduced at the preliminary examination is the fruit of the illegal arrest. They argue the police officers had no probable cause to arrest petitioner Ortega and thus all of the evidence should be suppressed. The court held that there was probable cause. The court further held that there was sufficient evidence to support all the charges.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and ordered petitioners to face trial on all charges and held that they were not being held improperly, and that the evidence adduced at the preliminary hearing supported the charges. The court held that the arrest was proper and therefore the evidence should not be suppressed.

Wilson v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 64102.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


134 Cal. App. 3d 173; 134 Cal. App. 3d 1062; 185 Cal. Rptr. 678; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1845



July 23, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Modified and Reprinted August 20, 1982; Hg. granted November 18, 1982 (See 34 C.3d 777)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent court to vacate its ruling denying defendant's motion, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538, to exclude evidence of drugs found in defendant's possession.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession of cocaine and hashish oil in violation of Cal. Health and Safety Code §§ 11350, 11357, and he moved pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 to exclude as evidence the narcotics found in his possession. The motion was denied and defendant filed a Petition For Writ of Prohibition (Stay) and Writ of Mandate to vacate the order which were summarily denied. Defendant then filed an alternative writ which the state supreme court granted. The court discharged the alternative writ and denied a preemptory writ because Proposition 8 was constitutional on its face and the Truth-in-Evidence provision, Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), which abrogated Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, and California decisional law had retroactive effect. Also, the court held that defendant was not "seized" for purposes of U.S. Const. amend. IV because no reasonable person would believe he was not free to leave; officers had approached defendant in an airport and asked to search his luggage.


OUTCOME: Defendant's peremptory writ seeking suppression of evidence was denied and the alternative writ was discharged because the "Right to Truth-in-Evidence" initiative of Proposition 8 was constitutional and defendant was not "seized" for purposes of U.S. Const. amend. IV.

People v. Buttes


Crim. No. 5255


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


134 Cal. App. 3d 116; 184 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1840



July 21, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 30, 1982. Newman, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 16654, William Silveira, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which found him not guilty by reason of insanity of committing forcible oral copulation, Cal. Penal Code § 288a, and under Cal. Penal Code § 1026, committed him to the state hospital for an additional two years because the jury found that by reason of mental disease, defect or disorder, he represented a substantial danger of physical harm to others.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed concluding appellant had not been denied equal protection by the absence of an amenability requirement at the time of his hearing resulting in the extension of his term. The court held that to continue the confinement, the prosecution had to show beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant was mentally ill and show that he was a physical danger to others, and it did. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in allowing testimony from a physician that appellant engaged in homosexual activities because such activity was not intended to show homosexuality as a mental illness per se, but was relevant to appellant's willingness to conform to the requirements of the hospital and his unwillingness to take responsibility and was relied upon in part for the expert's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the confinement, holding that appellant had not been denied equal protection by the absence of an amenability requirement at the time of his hearing resulting in the extension of his term, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in allowing physician's testimony about appellant's homosexual activities because the testimony showed appellant's willingness to conform to the requirements of the hospital.

Peterson v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 26356.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


134 Cal. App. 3d 31; 184 Cal. Rptr. 429; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1833



July 20, 1982

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 469113, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the judgment rendered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that upheld the constitutionality of a special municipal election that was conducted solely by mail ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city's counsel members authorized a special municipal election to be conducted solely by mail ballot. Appellant taxpayer and voter filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the constitutionality of the election. The preliminary injunction was denied. The election was conducted and the convention center ordinance was defeated. The trial court upheld the special election procedure declaring the procedure did not violate the constitutional provision for voting secrecy. Appellant sought review. Appellant contended that the citywide election endangered the integrity of the election process and undermined the constitutional mandate for secret balloting by not assuring secrecy when the vote was cast. Appellee asserted there was a presumption of constitutionality in favor of the city council's action that was not directly challenged by any proof of election abuses in the case. The court reversed the judgment. The presumption that the ordinance was constitutional was overcome because appellee offered no compelling reason to justify the election procedure in light of its direct conflict with the constitutional mandate for secrecy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that upheld the constitutionality of the special municipal election that was conducted solely by mail ballot because the ordinance was in direct conflict with the state constitution requiring secrecy in voting and appellee city offer no compelling reason to justify the election procedure.

State of California ex rel. State Lands Com. v. County of Orange


Civ. No. 26210


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


134 Cal. App. 3d 20; 184 Cal. Rptr. 423; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1832



July 20, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 6, 1982, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 21, 1982. Mosk, J., Newman, J., and Kaus J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 354079, Edward J. Wallin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and cause remanded with directions to reinstate the complaint and reconsider the motion for preliminary injunction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged a decision by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted appellee county's demurrer without leave to amend. Appellant sought declaratory relief, an injunction, an accounting, and restitution for appellee's practice of spending revenue derived from tideland improvements for its own municipal purposes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state brought action against appellee county seeking declaratory and injunctive relief, an accounting and restitution on the grounds that appellee was illegally using revenue derived from tidelands improvements for its own municipal purposes in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 2229. Appellant granted its interest in the subject tidelands to appellee through a trust for the purpose of construction and operation of a harbor. Appellee argued that its spending was authorized by a clause in the grant allowing it to collect and retain rents from improvements. The trial court granted appellee's demurrer without leave to amend. The court reversed and held that appellee had no right to devote proceeds to general municipal improvements unconnected with the trust purposes. The court held that the presence or absence of the term "collect and retain rents" in the trust grant was not dispositive and that no intent was evident from § 2229 that the legislature intended to abandon the public trust and interest in the tidelands and their revenues. The court remanded the case with instructions to reinstate the complaint and to reconsider appellant's motion for a preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order granting demurrer to appellee county because there was no evidence of intent by the legislature to abandon public trust and interest in tidelands and their revenues. The court held that appellee had no right to devote proceeds generated from trust grant from appellant state to general municipal improvements unrelated to trust purpose and directed reinstatement of complaint.

 

1394 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


San Jose Country Club Apartments v. County of Santa Clara


Civ. No. 47586


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


137 Cal. App. 3d 948; 187 Cal. Rptr. 493; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 2188



July 20, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 422070, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to enjoin appellant from enforcing from enforcing an ordinance prohibiting discrimination in rental housing against respondent apartment complex, in respondent's action alleging that the ordinance invalidly precluded them from renting exclusively to persons over the age of 21.


OVERVIEW: Respondent apartment complex, which rented exclusively to adults over the age of 21, filed an action challenging the validity an ordinance prohibiting discrimination in rental housing on the basis of age, parenthood, pregnancy or the presence of a minor child. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction, finding that the ordinance was preempted by state law and invalid. The trial court also found that the ordinance was passed without an environmental impact assessment as required by the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970 (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq. The appellate court reversed, holding that the discrimination addressed by the ordinance was prohibited under the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51-53, and that the legislature specifically stated that the Act did not have pre-emptive effect. Further, appellant rendered the CEQA issue moot by readopting the ordinance with a negative declaration regarding environmental impact. Moreover, the Act specifically authorized appellant's ordinance. Also, the ordinance did not violate the constitutional rights of association, expression, privacy, travel, due process, or equal protection.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the order of the trial court, which granted a preliminary injunction enjoining the enforcement of appellant county's ordinance, which prohibited discrimination in rental housing on the basis of age or parenthood, against respondent apartment complex. The Unruh Civil Rights Act prohibited such discrimination and did not preempt the enactment of the ordinance.

People v. Avalos


Crim. No. 41017.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


133 Cal. App. 3d 947; 184 Cal. Rptr. 286; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1820



July 16, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 21, 1982 (Crim. 22819)
Nov. 26, 1984, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 4, for reconsideration in light of Carlos v. Superior Court (1983) 35 Cal.3d 131 and People v. Garcia (1984) 36 Cal.3d 539.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-449063 William E. McGinley, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of a conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 209, three counts of attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664 and 211, and unlawful possession of a firearm by an ex-felon.


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate was convicted of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 209, three counts of attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code §§ 664 and 211, and unlawful possession of a firearm by an ex-felon, relating to appellant's attempt to rob a food stand and using a victim as a shield during a shootout with police, resulting in victim's death. Appellant sought review of the convictions, and the court affirmed all counts except kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. The court held that appellant's movement of the victim did not constitute kidnapping, and reversed the kidnapping conviction and the special circumstance related to kidnapping, but the conviction reversal did not require a reversal or modification of the life without parole sentence imposed by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court determined that appellant's movement of the victim did not constitute kidnapping and therefore reversed the kidnapping conviction and the special circumstance related to the kidnapping. The court affirmed appellant's other convictions and upheld the sentence of life without parole.

 

1396 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Banas v. Transamerica Title Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 25528


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


133 Cal. App. 3d 845; 184 Cal. Rptr. 262; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1763



July 14, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 198758, Duke D. Rouse, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed on condition, however, that within 30 days after the remittitur is filled in the superior court, plaintiffs file an amendment to their purported cause of action against the County of San Bernardino and Joe Bell alleging facts substantially as recited in this opinion. The trial court is directed to grant plaintiffs leave to file such an amendment. If no such amendment to the complaint is filed within the prescribed time or such further time as the trial court shall for good cause allow, the judgment shall be affirmed. In the interests of justice, the parties shall each bear their own respective costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed a decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that granted defendant county's motion for judgment on the pleadings with respect to the validity of a tax sale and the tax deed on plaintiffs' improved real property.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners purchased a home that was built on two lots, each of which, unknown to plaintiffs, was separately assessed property tax. Plaintiffs however, understood that the home was on one single lot, paid the tax on one lot, and did not realize otherwise until after defendant county conducted a tax deed sale for delinquent taxes. The trial court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings, in which defendant argued that plaintiffs failed to state a cause of action that the tax deed sale proceedings were irregular or there was actual fraud pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 3518 and 3711. The appellate court reversed because its understood that plaintiffs' complaint alleged that the statute that established the procedures for giving notice of impending tax sales and tax deeds as applied to them was unconstitutional. The appellate court observed that where plaintiffs had no reason to know of the tax assessment or delinquency, were equitable owners in actual possession of the property, and their property tax bill was annually paid, due process required posting the notice of impending sale on the property itself rather than by publication.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision to grant defendant county's motion for judgment on the pleadings because it was erroneously based upon a belief that plaintiff homeowners' complaint challenged the irregularity of a tax sale proceeding. Plaintiffs' complaint was actually based upon a claim that due process required posting the notice of the intended tax sale on the property itself when equitable owners had actual possession.

 

1397 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Cornelious


Civ. No. 24624.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


133 Cal. App. 3d 684; 184 Cal. Rptr. 148; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1749



July 8, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 8, 1982 (See 35 C.3d 461)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, No. 10992, William E. Lehnhardt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a putative daughter, sought review of the order from the Superior Court of Imperial County, (California) that denied her petition to administer the estate of her putative father and granted the petition of respondent, the administrator, to administer his estate. Appellant claimed that the conclusive presumption of Cal. Evid. Code § 621(a) that she was the daughter of her mother's husband of 48 years denied her due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a putative daughter, filed a petition to administer her putative father's estate. The trial court held that under Cal. Evid. Code § 621, appellant was conclusively presumed to be the daughter of appellant's mother's husband (husband), that there was no parent-child relationship to allow appellant priority to administer the estate under Cal. Prob. Code §§ 422 and 423, and that denied her petition and granted respondent, the administrator's, petition to administer the estate. On appeal, the court held that although appellant's sickle cell condition might prove that the husband was not appellant's father, it was insufficient to prove that the decedent was appellant's father. Because the husband had six other children with appellant's mother, was married to appellant's mother for 48 years and never was told that he was not appellant's father, appellant's only compelling interest was to inherit the decedent's estate. This interest did not outweigh the state's interest in the stability of titled to property and inheritance rights and in the sanctity of the family. Therefore, the conclusive presumption did not deprive appellant of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant, a putative daughter's, petition to administer her putative father's estate and granting the petition of respondent, the administrator, to administer it. The court held that the state's interest in stability outweighed appellant's interest in the decedent's estate. Thus, the conclusive presumption that appellant was the daughter of her mother's husband did not violate her due process rights.

 

1398 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hebert v. Harn


Civ. No. 26202


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


133 Cal. App. 3d 465; 184 Cal. Rptr. 83; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1732



July 2, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 257478, Robert E. Rickles, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed with instructions to the trial court to allow Helen M. Harn her court or jury trial. 5



5 Appellant has also requested this court dismiss the case because of the failure of the plaintiff to comply with section 581a in not serving the summons within three years after commencement of the action. We decline to do so. We have avoided repeating the lengthy procedural history where Harn has now managed to avoid two default judgments. The first default judgment was set aside after she implored the court for a right to a trial on the merits and her counsel agreed to arbitrate the dispute. She may not now claim plaintiff's failure to serve the summons and complaint within the statutory period warrants a dismissal. Moreover, there is nothing in this record to support her claim that Hebert lacked diligence or was unreasonable in prosecuting her case.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County, (California) that denied her motion to set aside an award entered in favor of plaintiff and for a trial de novo. Plaintiff brought the action to recover damages that resulted from an automobile accident with defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff brought action against defendant to recover damages that resulted from an automobile accident. Defendant declined to appear at the arbitration proceeding, and plaintiff was awarded damages. Defendant made a motion to set aside the award and for a trial de novo. Her motion was denied, and defendant sought review. In reversing the decision, the court held that the local rule was invalid because it contravened Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1141.20. Where a local rule had the effect of closing the courts to the defaulting party and making the decision of the arbitrator the final determination, defendant was denied due process of law. Legitimate considerations could not be expanded to exceed the scope of the legislation authorizing judicial arbitration. The legislature unconditionally provided any party could elect a jury trial upon making a request within 20 days of the award. The intent of the legislature that access to the court for a new trial following a judicially ordered arbitration was to be procedurally unfettered.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that awarded damages to plaintiff and denied defendant's motion for a de novo trial, holding the local rule was invalid to the extent that it imposed a requirement of "good cause" before defendant could seek a new trial. The court ruled that where a local rule had the effect of closing the courts to defendant making the decision of the arbitrator the final determination, defendant was denied due process of law.

 

1399 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Torres


Crim. No. 39449


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


133 Cal. App. 3d 265; 184 Cal. Rptr. 39; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1715



June 30, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, Nos. SM 30568 and 32389, Robert L. Trapp, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction, in consolidated cases, by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), of the sale of marijuana, possession of marijuana for sale, and the sale and possession of concentrated cannabis under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11357, subd. (a), 11360, subd. (a), 11359, and 11357, subd. (a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of sale and possession for sale of marijuana and hashish, and challenged his convictions on numerous grounds. The court affirmed the convictions. It held that the trial court adequately warned defendant of the dangers of self-representation, and that by defendant's own testimony marijuana use was not an indispensable part of his religious faith for the purposes of protection under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court further held that the trial court did not err in refusing to grant defendant's challenge for cause to a juror who affirmed her willingness to act impartially; that the testimony of a criminologist identifying a substance obtained from defendant's residence as marijuana was sufficient; and that the trial court did not err in failing to instruct the jury that defendant was a target of selective law enforcement. The court also held that the trial court adequately warned defendant that he need not testify and that any error in the warning was harmless because the evidence against him was overwhelming, that the prosecutor's comments were not racial slurs, and that defendant did not show that the trial court abused its discretion in denying probation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, holding that the trial court adequately warned him of the dangers of self-representation and of his right against self-incrimination, that defendant's marijuana use was not protected by the First Amendment because it was not an indispensable part of his religious faith, and that the trial court's denial of probation was not an abuse of discretion.

 

1400 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barnhouse v. City of Pinole


Civ. No. 50631


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


133 Cal. App. 3d 171; 183 Cal. Rptr. 881; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1706



June 29, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 29, 1982, and the petition of respondents Earl W. Smith, Earl W. Smith Developers, Ltd., and Earl W. Smith Development Organization for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 8, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 149025, Thomas F. McBride, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The nonsuit in favor of the Smith respondents is reversed. The elimination of the nuisance action in favor of the City and State is also reversed. In all other respects, the judgments are affirmed. Costs to appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which granted respondent developers' motion for nonsuit regarding willful misconduct and fraudulent concealment and eliminated appellants' nuisance theory against government respondents in appellants' action for damages to real property and personal injuries resulting from soil subsidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were owners of single family residences. Respondents were developers and builders of appellants' lots and the government who owned associated drainage systems that drained storm waters into a ditch onto property bordering the lots. Government respondents built a freeway through unimproved land north of appellants' property. Respondent developers and builders completed a subdivision opposite seepage zones. Appellant owners purchased homes from respondent developers and were advised that the houses were on fill, but they were not advised of the preexisting slides and springs. Several slides occurred in the properties. Appellants sued respondents for damages to real property and personal injuries resulting from soil subsidence. The trial jury ruled in favor of respondents. Appellants challenged, arguing that the verdict was not supported by substantial evidence. The court reversed a nonsuit in favor of respondent developers, because there was a duty to disclose property conditions. The elimination of the nuisance action in favor of government respondents was also reversed, because immunity defense was not substantiated. However, judgments were affirmed in other respects.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the decision granting respondent developers' motion for nonsuit and eliminating appellant property owners' nuisance action against government respondent, because respondent developers had a duty to disclose material facts and the elimination of the action against government respondent was the result of error by the trial court. The judgments were affirmed in all other respects.
